# Index for Nógrády Mihály – Glossary of Hungarian surnames.pdf

Michael Nogrady
GLOSSARY
OF
HUNGARIAN SURNAMES
IN
CANADA
Private Internet Edition
OTTAWA 2012



1
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
INTRODUCTORY
Names, whether they denote places or identify people, are derived from the language of the community which bestowed them. Some names, because of their antiquity, do not have a meaning in the spoken language and their understanding presents a problem. This is especially true for immigrant groups and their descendants in Canada whose surnames originated in a language other than English or French. Such group is the Hungarians who call themselves Magyar in their own language. Most people of Hungarian descent accept their surnames as a requisite for meeting the requirements of society and being able to function properly in their daily lives. They do not think it may mean something in the language of their ancestors. Like a Mr. Dave Santa whose last name is mistakenly associated with the Yuletide season's Santa Claus. He was quoted in a newspaper article as saying "I always got bugged as a kid. They would say, 'What are you bringing me for Christmas?' that sort of things [...] It's a Hungarian name, as far as I know. It was shortened. But I don't know what it was originally. Overall it's good. It's my name" (McKeen 1996: C9). The name Santa, spelled Sánta in its original Hungarian form, is indeed a good name but neither has anything to do with the Yuletide season and Santa Claus, nor was it shortened. It belongs to a group of Hungarian surnames that depict defects and blemishes. Sánta, pronounced as Shawn-tah, signifies a 'lame' or 'limping' person. Other names that belong in this category are Csonka 'mutilated', Görbe 'crooked', or 'hunched', Sebes 'covered with sores or having scars'. These words are easy to find in a dictionary, but other Hungarian surnames like Gál, Ilku, Aba or Buda present problems for those not initiated in ancient languages. Names, whether their meanings are known or not, may be used in genealogical research, but are misleading in tracing one's ancestry. A name can only reveal the language from which it was derived, not the racial or genetic background of the predecessors. This is especially true in patriarchal societies where only the father's surname is carried on. The name may point in one direction but the genetic make-up in another. Consequently the name is not a decisive factor in the search for one's ancestry, only a guide. But chances are that if a person's ancestors never left Hungary and they had names that originated in the Hungarian language, then he or she is of Hungarian descent. Some names turn symbolic as soon as the word from which they are derived becomes disused in the spoken language. A name which has no meaning in the current language may baffle most of us, but only few would make an attempt to find its signification, others would put it down as a name. But name researchers have a different approach. They maintain that every name had a coherent meaning at the time of its bestowal and it is worth spending time to unravel their secrets, which in our case is hidden in Hungarian and its cognate languages. A skillful evaluation of names, especially of surnames, may shed light on obscure periods of history. Names can reveal vocational diversification, feudal social order, naming habits and ways of life. Names in early Hungarian society were neither created by rash decisions, nor were they the result of careful contemplation. Their bestowal were not organized or



2
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
regulated by a given formula. They were the product of the community that created many other useful customs to serve as guidelines in the daily discourse of its members. Names were derived from the spoken language, necessitated by the need to differentiate between individuals. Every conversation requires the identification or description of people as well as objects. These descriptions were simplified and reduced to a befitting single name. Name giving was spontaneous in most cases and it may have happened that the same person was called by a different name in the next village. Both names, however, described the person accurately. In his own village he may have been called by his occupation, while in another he was referred to by the name of his birth place. Since the way of thinking is the same among people speaking the same language, the choice of names was uniform in regions far apart from one another. The name giving custom of Hungarians in Transylvania was identical to those living in the Csallóköz (confined to Slovakia, now called Žitný ostrov in Slovakia). The most fitting name eventually became permanent by its regular use and accompanied its owner throughout his/her life. In some cases, when the name was unique, the children inherited it but the derivational suffix -i was added to indicate a descendant. The transfer of the father's name to his descendants became obligatory only after the compulsory registration of names which was usually done at the time the child was baptized. The compulsory use of surnames in front of baptismal names was introduced at the end of the 18th century (Kálmán 1978: 62). The freedom in choosing and changing one's surname has ended at that time. However, the custom of free choice in name giving continues in the form of nicknames to the present day. Nicknames fulfill the same self-satisfying freedom in choice as they did in antiquity. The ancestors of Hungarians did not know that a large number of their names will survive for posterity. Therefore, we should not be surprised about the revealing nature of some names, because the words from which they originated were part of casual conversations, lacking awareness for future potential embarrassment. The single name system was prevalent among the serfs during the Middle Ages. Non-Christian names like Modor, Ziget, Joulegen, Mocus, Suprun, Topa and Bot were recorded according to the yet unregulated spelling system of the period. Their presentday equivalents are Modor, Sziget, Jólegény, Mókus, Sopron, Topa and Bot. Old documents suggest that one person had only one name in Hungary until around the 13th century when the use of Christian names, derived from the Latin, Greek and Hebrew languages, have begun to supplant the old and established Hungarian names. The need for avoiding ambiguity between persons having the same name gave an impetus for the use of what we now call surnames. In the 13th century, some names are already listed with attributes in Latin, the language of administration. Such names are Pousa banus ‘Pósa the warden’, Pousa filius Zombot ‘Pósa son of Saturday’, Pristaldo Pousa ‘Bailiff Pósa’, Pousa Vendegus ‘Pósa the guest’ (Fehértói 1983: 282-286). The old names did not disappear but first continued to be used side by side with baptismal names and then gradually as attributes in front of Christian names, thus the Hungarian system of last name first emerged. From the 13th century on, the surname system slowly acquired popularity as baptismal names gained acceptance and the naming practice was on its way to



3
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
stabilization. The former single name that was derived from the spoken language assumed the role of a surname in the form of adjective. For example, János the son of Sziget became Szigeti János ‘John Szigeti’ by the addition of the derivational suffix -i to the former single name of the father. The derivational or relational suffix -i (archaic -y) was mostly used for denoting geographical origin though. Like, a person from the city of Buda was named Budai.
Another way to indicate descent was to append -fi (archaic -fy) to the father’s name. The element -fi is derived from fiú ‘son’. For example Keményfi means ‘son of Kemény’. But this kind of name formation was rare, only few surnames survived from that era. Girls also inherited their surnames from the paternal line which they kept until marriage. After marriage, women adopted their husband’s surname. They were still addressed by their baptismal name in everyday life, but were recorded in the registry as their husband’s wife by addition of the suffix -né. For example, the wife of Szabó János was registered as Szabó Jánosné. The adaptation of surnames was first practiced by the nobility and the wealthy segment of society. The general public, mostly serfs, was slow to catch up. Surnames, in general, became fashionable by the 16th century but were not inherited by the descendants as a rule. If a Kovács János ‘John Smith’ had a son who became a tailor then he was referred to as Szabó János ‘John Tailor’. Until it was banished by kings of the House of Árpád (896-1301), the Magyars used a rune-like writing system called rovásírás. It was developed to meet the phonetic needs of their language, unlike the Roman alphabet which replaced it after conversion to Christianity around the beginning of the second millennium AD. The Roman alphabet had an unfavorable effect for it has fewer letter than the number of speech sounds in the Magyar language. Scribes had to grapple with the problem of finding appropriate ways for recording the extra sounds by combining the available Roman graphemes. They did this individually, disparate of each other, which resulted in various spellings of old names. Such names are listed for each entry when available. The archetype rovásírás was a consonantal syllabary in character, each sign representing a consonant and an unidentified vowel. The choice of vowel was left up to the reader. The lack of written vowels presented little difficulty owing to the nature of the language. The favoring of consonants provided a much needed flexibility for the Magyars to accommodate the various vowels in different dialects of their language. Later, however, a separate sign was developed for each vowel thus the last version of the alphabet had one letter for each speech sound
KEY TO PRONUNCIATION
Hungarian names and words are printed in Italic, followed by their proper pronunciation in square brackets depicted by the Phonetic Transcription. The stress is always on the first syllable. Occasionally it shifts to a consonant away from the first syllable. When this happens, the stressed consonant is marked by reduplication. The length of consonants is uniform in all cases. The length of vowels without diacritical marks is always short. Some vowel lengths are modified by diacritical marks,



4
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
placed above the letters. Short vowels are marked with dieresis (  ̈ ), which also indicates a change in vowel quality, as in o>ö, or u>ü. Long vowels are marked with the acute accent (  ́ ) as á, é, í, ó; or the double acute accent ( ʺ ) as in ő, ű. Diacritical marks play an important role not only in pronunciation but in meaning too. For example veréb [vere:b] means ‘sparrow’, but if the acute accent is placed above the first vowel it produces véreb [ve:reb] ‘blood hound’. Also, the meaning of úr [u:r] ‘lord’ changes to ‘space’ when the acute accent is replaced by a double acute accent to read űr [y:r]. It should be noted that the combination of two letters, the cs, gy, ly, ny, sz, ty and zs mark a single consonant in Hungarian orthography. The Phonetic Transcription for these consonants will be underlined in this publication. A firm parity exists between the spoken language and its written form. Each Hungarian letter, or combination of two letters, has its distinct sound which remains constant. There is no omission of sounds, every letter has to be pronounced in every word or name without exception. For this reason Hungarian is one of the few languages that have a truly phonetic spelling system. The following table shows all Hungarian letters, their corresponding Phonetic Transcription and samples of how they compare to English sounds.
a a Same as the aw in law. á a: Same as the a in father. b b Same as the English b. c ts Similar to the ts in dots. cs ch Same as the English ch. d d Same as the English d. e e Same as the e in end. é e: Same as the a in mate. f f Same as the English f. g g Same as the English g in gate. gy dj Similar to the initial sound in during. h h Same as the English h. i i Same as the i in hit. í i: Same as the ee in deer. j j Same as the y in yellow. k k Same as the English k. l l Same as the English l. ly j Same as the y in yes. m m Same as the English m. n n Same as the English n. ny nj Same as the initial sound in new. o o Same as the o in coy. ó o: Same as the o in low. ö ø Similar to the i in birth. ő ø: Similar to the i in third.



5
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
p p Same as the English p. r r Same as the English r. s sh Same as the English sh. sz s Same as the English s. t t Same as the English t. ty tj Similar to the initial sound in tune. u u Same as the u in bull. ú u: Same as the oo in cool. ü y Similar to the u in the French du. ű y: Same as above but longer. v v Same as the English v. z z Same as the English z. zs zh Similar to the s in usual.
MOST FREQUENT SUFFIXES IN SURNAMES
-a [a] This suffix may become -e [e] or -ú [u:] as required by vowel harmony. It is an archaic suffix that formed adjectives from nouns and verbs. Believed to be the continuation of the Sumerian adjectival suffix -a described by Marie-Louise Thomsen (1984: 64). The suffix -a once played a considerable role in the formation of adjectives as evidenced by old names and words but it is no longer in active use by Hungarians. It is decidedly safe to conclude that this Sumerian suffix was active in the early Hungarian language, but later it was supplanted by the current -s [sh] when the adjective is formed from a noun and by the participle -ó [o:] when formed from a verb. The change from -a to -s and -ó is most evident in surnames as demonstrated by the following examples: Barta - Bartos - Bartó Csoma - Csomós - Csomó Doba - Dobos - Dobó Síma - Símás - Simó Varga - Vargus - Vargó
-ány [a:nj] May assume the forms -ény, -ony, -uny as required by vowel harmony. It is a nominal suffix that converts verbs and nouns into collective nouns. Examples: hoz 'bring' > hozomány 'dowry'; vet 'sow' > vetemény 'vegetable'.
-ár [a:r] Other forms are -ér [e:r], -or [or] as required by vowel harmony. It is a nominal suffix that forms occupations or action nouns from verbs and nouns. Examples: csap 'faucet; spigot' > csaplár 'bartender; inn-keeper'; tüz 'fire' > tüzér 'artilleryman'; kaland 'adventure' > kalandor 'adventurer'. This is an internationally shared suffix present in the Uralic, Altaic and the Indó



6
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
European languages mostly in the form of -er. It denotes occupations in all of them. Cf. write > writer.
-as [ash] The permanent feature of this suffix is the letter s [sh], which may be preceded by a connecting vowel that conforms to the rules of vowel harmony (-ás [a:sh], -es [esh], -os [osh], -ös [øsh], -us [ush], -üs [ysh]). This suffix forms adjectives from nouns to denote: 1. Occupations; kosaras 'basket maker or seller', from kosár 'basket'. 2. Involvement; játékos 'player', from játék 'play, game'. 3. Character and quality; rendes 'orderly, correct', from rend 'order'. 4. Condition; sáros 'muddy', from sár 'mud'. 5. Resemblance; kékes 'bluish', from kék 'blue'. 6. Status and rank; gyáros 'industrialist', from gyár 'factory'; százados 'captain', from század 'company of one hundred soldiers', derived from száz 'hundred'. 7. Possession and provision; csizmás 'one who owns and wears a pair of highboots', from csizma 'high-boot'; fegyveres 'one who has been provided with weapons (armed)', from fegyver 'weapon'. It may be of interest to note that the Persian suffix -esh (pronounced the same as the Hungarian -es) forms nouns of quality or activity. Cf. Persian âmûzesh 'knowledge', Hungarian tudás; Persian namayesh 'exhibition', Hungarian kiállítás (Mace 1971:197). The exact likeness of the Hungarian -es was also present in the Sumerian language in the form of -eš according to Marie-Louise Thomsen. One of the examples given is the Sumerian da-re-eš ‘for ever’ (Thomsen 1984: 66), cf. Hungarian örökös ‘perpetual’. The word-final element in both words are the same [sh] preceded by a vowel.
-ász [a:s] Also spelled -ész [e:s] when required by vowel harmony. This is a formative suffix which converts nouns and in some rare cases verbs into names of specialized occupations or professions. It functions much the same as the English suffixes -ist, -er and -ician. Examples: gépész 'machinist', from gép 'machine'; halász 'fisher', from hal 'fish'; szülész 'obstetrician', from szül 'give birth'.
-at [at] Also spelled -át [a:t], -et [et] and -ét [e:t] when affected by vowel harmony. This suffix converts verbs and nouns to "derived nouns". It may be called a nominal suffix with caution when appended to verbs: húz 'draw' > huzat 'draught'; kel 'rise' > kelet 'east'. But, to my knowledge, there is no generally accepted appropriate English term when suffixed to a noun: müvész 'artist' > müvészet 'art'; mag 'seed' > magzat 'embryo, offspring'.
-da [da] The suffix -da is present in many Hungarian surnames that are derived from old place names. It appears to be a grammatical designator denoting a place, but the complexity of its multiple functions has never been explored. The suffix -da used to be active in the formation of place names during the



7
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Middle Ages in Hungary, which is an indication that its use must have been extensive in the spoken language. Its usage, however, was allowed to diminish until it was revived in the early 19th century for a short period of time, only to fall out of use again. During its short lived renewed activity one distinct characteristic of -da was the formation of nouns from verbs. The resultant noun denoted a place which people either inhabited or used for a specific activity. Examples: fog ‘hold’ > fogda ‘prison’; nyom ‘press’ > nyomda ‘printing shop’; zár ‘lock, close’ > zárda ‘convent’. While -da was the most frequently used suffix in the formation of place names, occasionally a single -d or a transposed -ad, or -ta was used for the same purpose. The phoneme d and t are regarded transposable. Examples: Várda; Várad; Érd; Zajta. The suffix -da is present in languages other than Hungarian. In the Turkish language, it is a particle forming the locative case (Akdikmen 1986: 90). It is also an Estonian suffix that indicates a place. The Sumerians used it too in ancient Mesopotamia. The Sumerian suffix -da appears to be more complex than its modern equivalents. Labat inform us that both da and ta denote a district or neighborhood as independent words (Labat 1948: No. 335). The present-day Hungarian equivalent of the Sumerian ta would be táj [ta:j] ‘region, neighborhood’. According to Deimel, the Sumerian -da was also used as an adverbial suffix of place (Deimel 1939: 196). This function may be exemplified by the Hungarian oda ‘to there’ derived from ott ‘there’ and ide ‘to here’ derived from itt ‘here’. Both René Labat and Anton Deimel disclose that da and ta have an additional meaning ‘side’ as independent words. The word ta is still being used in this sense in Hungary. When a stableman wants a horse to move its rump to one side, he says far ta ‘rump to the side’.
-fi [fi] Also spelled -ffi [fi], -fy [fi] and -ffy [fi] 'son of'. Derived from the word fiú 'son', it is a patronymic suffix, attached to the father’s surname. This kind of naming is similar to the English patronymics, like Emerson ‘son of Emery’. Hungarian examples: Gálfi 'son of Gál'; Bátorffi 'son of Bátor'; Pálfy 'son of Pál'; Bánffy 'son of Bán'.
-i [i] Generally referred to as a derivational suffix, but its more proper description would be relational suffix (viszonyrag in Hungarian). Also spelled -y but pronounced [i] in surnames of medieval origin when some scribes substituted the Greek y for the Roman i. In rare cases the suffix -i is rendered -e, as a result of wrongly applied vowel harmony (Abele instead of Abeli), and when that happens it is interpreted by a number of linguists as being the possessive suffix -é [e:]. Hungarian scholarly views differ about the function of this suffix. Some identify it as a form of patronymic turned possessive (Kálmán 1978: 65). Others classify it as an adjectival suffix pertaining to place and time (Bánhidi et al. 1965: 219), when in fact its function includes both and even more. It is used with reference to geographical names to indicate where a person resides or comes from and in surnames to indicate parental origin. The -i or -y are suffixed to nouns to form:



8
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
1. Patronymics, when the paternal name precedes the suffix - Mihályi 'Mihály's, descendant of Mihály'. Cf. Sumerian I7 ‘descendant’, I9 ‘origin’ (Labat 1948: No. 381, 579). 2. Adjectives, pertaining to place and time, e.g. gyári munkás 'factory worker'. The phrase reggeli műszak 'morning shift' designates the time of work. 3. Place of origin, e.g. Aradi 'a native of Arad'. The surname denotes the residence or the place where the person or his ancestors came from. 4. Endearments. For example the endearing form of pajtás 'chum, mate' is pajti.
The derivational suffix -i is also present in languages other than Hungarian. It is shared by European and Oriental languages such as Spanish, Iranian (Persian) and Semitic. A Persian example would be shah 'king' > shahi 'royal', of which the Hungarian equivalent is király 'king' > királyi 'royal'. The ultimate source of this suffix may be the Sumerian language where it also added adjectival value to a noun: Uri 'a resident of Ur'. Ur was the name of a city where Sumerian civilization reached its peak as early as 3500 BC. Interestingly, Hungarians still call a refined urbanite úri ember 'a man from Ur'. Úr also denotes a 'gentleman' and a 'lord' in their language.
-ik [ik] Also spelled -ic [ik] in old names. This suffix is shared by the agglutinative (Sumerian, Uralic and Altaic) and the inflective (Indo-European) languages where it forms adjectives from nouns in addition to expressing relationship. In the words of Francois Lenormant, the Akkadian -ik was employed in forming the names of agents and adjectives with an active signification, as in nirik 'prince' from nir to govern', uddaik 'preeminent' from uddu 'to rise' (Lenormant 1877: 392). There was no differentiation between the Sumerian and Akkadian languages at the time of Lenormant.
-ka [ka] Endearing suffix. It may originate from the independent Sumerian word ku (Labat 1948: No. 468) which assumed the form -ka or -ke after becoming a suffix in the Hungarian language. The meaning of the Sumerian ku is 'innocent, gracious, friendly', when attached to a name as a Hungarian suffix, it indicates that the person has those qualities. The suffix -ka at the end of place names may be the Hungarian variant of the Sumerian ki 'place, land, country, region' (Labat 1948: No. 460). For example the wordfinal element -ka in Szabadka, a Hungarian toponym (confined to Serbia in 1020, now called Subotica) may mean 'place'. Szabad means 'free' in the Hungarian language, thus 'free place'. A fair amount of caution should be exercised in the semantic role of -ka. It is used as a diminutive suffix in the Slavonic languages and Hungarians living in Slavic speech communities tend to use it in that sense. Also, Hungary was in the Slavic sphere of influence from 1945 to 1991 and during that time some linguists promulgated that the Hungarian -ka fulfills the same diminutive function as the Slavonic -ka. But it does not.



9
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
The Hungarian diminutive is the prefix kis- as in kislány 'little girl'. The adjective kis means 'small, little' as an independent word. Kislányka translates as 'darling little girl'.
-nok [nok] A suffix that forms occupations and earned or awarded status from nouns and verbs. Examples: tár 'store' > tárnok 'storekeeper'; fő 'head' > főnök 'supervisor'; baj 'challenge, distress' > bajnok 'champion'. There is a possibility that this suffix is the continuation of the Sumerian formative nig (Labat 1948: No. 597), frequently used in the forming of nouns with both physical and non-physical attributes. Example: nig-gig 'evil, harm' from gig 'darkness, black' (Labat 1948: 427).
-ó [o:] Participle suffix, forms adjectives from verbs. It may take the form -ő or another appropriate vowel as a result of vowel harmony. Adjectives derived from verbs usually denote occupations. The suffix is used in this capacity in most surnames. Examples: csap 'hit, strike' > Csapó 'fuller, beater'; jár 'walk' > Járó 'walker'.
VOWEL HARMONY
The principle of phonetic agreement between the initial and subsequent vowels in a word is referred to as vowel harmony. The vowels in most agglutinative languages, like Turkish and Hungarian, must either be all frontal (e.g. e, ö, ő, ü, ű) or all back (e.g. a, á, o, ó, u, ú). Loan words from foreign languages, compound words and intermediate vowels (e.g. é, i, í) are the only exceptions, they do not require harmonization but all others do. The rules of vowel harmony require that suffixes attached to the root word must all contain either front or back vowels, not a combination of both. If the vowel of the root word happens to be a back vowel, like in the case of Hun, then the vowel of the suffix must be of the same class: -gar.
TREATMENT OF HUNGARIAN NAMES IN CANADA
Read to the delegates of the XVI International Congress of Onomastic Sciences held at the Université Laval in Québec 16-22 August 1987. First published in JeanClaude Boulanger ed. Proper Names at the Crossroads of the Humanities and Social Sciences. Proceedings of the XVIth International Congress of Onomastic Sciences. Québec: Les presses de l’Université Laval. 1990. Pages 433-440.
Summary
Hungarian immigrants to Canada are faced with linguistic decisions concerning their given and family names due to the phonetic and orthographic differences between the English and the Hungarian languages. The changes made are usually minor, so that the written name looks similar to its original form. Changes include the removal of



10
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
diacritical marks, the replacement of unfamiliar letter combinations and adoption of English given names.
Introduction
Most Hungarian immigrants arrive in Canada knowing about the country’s two official languages, but not fully understanding the effects the English or French languages will impose on their names. They are seldom aware of the changes that will have to be made, due to the different naming practices and the different use of the Roman alphabet in the Hungarian language. These two elements account for changes made to their names immediately after entering the country and for other modifications made thereafter. Hungarians entering Canada could potentially be affected by both English and French influences. Both of these languages use the Roman alphabet for writing, the same as Hungarian, but the sound-letter relationship varies between them and the Hungarian language. Thus, to simplify the discussion, this paper will focus on the treatment of Hungarian names in the English speaking environment in Canada.
Hungarian names without diacritical marks
Hungarian names which have no diacritical marks can continue functioning in their original forms, much to the satisfaction of their bearers. These names are comprised of letters that look familiar to speakers of the English language. The sequence of letters may not be familiar in some cases and may present problems for pronunciation, mainly due to differences in articulation. Such surnames are Gazdag [gazdag] ‘rich’, Baka [baka] ‘infantryman’, Jeles [jelesh], ‘eminent’, Torma [torma] ‘horseradish’ or Csala [chala] ‘enticer’. As for Csala, English speakers will likely say [sele] while a Hungarian will say [chala].
Hungarian names with diacritical marks
Names without diacritical marks comprise only a small number of all Hungarian names recorded in Canada. Most of them do have diacritical marks and Hungarians having such names retain the original letters without showing them. The omission of diacritical marks results in new orthographic forms. Some of these new forms can also have new meanings in the Hungarian language. For example, when Csörgő [chørgø:] becomes Csorgo [chorgo:], the meaning changes from ‘rattling’ to ‘trickling’; similarly Körmös [kørmøsh] ‘clawed’ becomes Kormos [kormosh] ‘sooty’. Or, when Gömbös [gømbøsh] becomes Gombos [gombosh], a change in meaning takes place from ‘spherical’ to ‘one whom has buttons’. These changes, especially when they result in genuine Hungarian names, become misleading for the present and subsequent generations of name researchers in Canada. A name researcher would have no reason to further investigate the etymology of Gombos, because it is indeed a name, and would not suspect that it may be the mutilated form of Gömbös. Some names appear to be unrelated to the Hungarian language after their loss of diacritical marks. For example, Láng [la:ng] ‘flame’ becomes Lang, [lang] which means ‘long’ in German.



11
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Hungarian given names with recognizable equivalents
The loss of diacritical marks is only one of the mutations a Hungarian name may suffer. Given names present additional problems, mainly in pronunciation, and many immigrants prefer to abandon them in favor of their English equivalents. To find the English equivalent for a Hungarian Christian name is comparatively less difficult than for those names of uniquely Hungarian origin. The spelling of Christian names does not vary too much from one language to another and adopting the English version does not present problems. Examples:
Hungarian Hungarian English spelling pronunciation equivalent
József [jo:zhef] Joseph Dávid [da:vid] David Mária [ma:ria] Mary, Marie Erzsébet [erzhe:bet] Elizabeth Teréz [tere:z] Theresa, Teresa Ábrahám [a:braha:m] Abraham Mihály [miha:j] Michael
Most Hungarians having Christian forenames have converted them to their English equivalents in order to expedite the transition to English Canadian society, both for social acceptance and increased chances for success in the search for economic opportunities.
Hungarian given names without comparable equivalents
The problem is different with given names derived from the Hungarian language which have no English equivalents. It is even difficult to coin adequate English versions which would approximate the original orthography and pronunciation. Names like Boglár [bogla:r] ‘rosette’, Bulcsú [bulchu:] ‘sage’, Hajnal [hajnal] ‘dawn’ and Gyöngy [djøndj] ‘pearl’ are usually retained intact for official use, but in everyday life Béla may prefer to be called Bill and Gyöngy may function as Georgia. However, in the majority of cases, their colleagues, friends and close acquaintances learn the proper Hungarian pronunciation and use it successfully. Since the Hungarian custom is to have only one first name, one solution for those with difficult Hungarian given names would be to choose and officially adopt an English second name and retain the original first name. But two or more given names are rare among first generation immigrant Hungarians and they would not feel comfortable having two given names; therefore, this practice is seldom followed.
Given names of second generation Hungarians
The naming of second generation Hungarians follows the Canadian custom, but given names are carefully selected. One of the names is either Hungarian, or the orthographic difference between the English and Hungarian version is so small that



12
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Hungarian speaking relatives have no difficulties in identifying them. For example Robert - Róbert, Albert - Albert, Abel - Ábel, Lauren - Laura, Nellie - Nelli. There was no research done on the naming habits of subsequent generations, although one can suspect that the process of acculturation would have diluted any desire to reflect Hungarian forenames in the naming of children.
Adaptation of Hungarian surnames
Surname problems are of a different nature. While given names can be modified or adapted without many difficulties, a surname has legal status and cannot be changed arbitrarily, unless it is legally changed - a move few Hungarians are willing to undertake. Surnames derived from the Hungarian language have unusual orthographic and phonetic shapes, not only in Canada but in most countries of the world. This peculiar nature of Hungarian names is the main reason why they are difficult to handle by English speaking Canadians. The spelling and pronunciation of names that originate from cognate languages do not cause problems for the average person, but names from non-cognate languages, like Hungarian, pose difficulties. All Hungarian immigrants are exposed to name related problems, but they are not entirely on their own in finding solutions to the pronunciation of their names. A variety of pronunciations are introduced to them by their neighbors, colleagues and friends in the normal course of daily transactions. These informal pronunciations vary and may be numerous, because those who are in contact with their names and offer advice have no experience in dealing with unknown names that originate in an unknown language. Their solutions have very little onomastic or phonetic rationale. As a result, some Hungarians become accustomed to responding to several different pronunciations of their surnames and the one they hear most often is usually adopted for the sake of simplicity, regardless of the phonetic departure from the original form. For example, Gombóc [gombo:ts] ‘dumpling’ is pronounced as [gambak]. However, within their families and among fellow Hungarians, the proper pronunciation prevails. A common Hungarian surname in Canada is Nagy [nadj] ‘big’, constantly mispronounced either as [negi] or [nedzhi]. But those who have this name prefer the preservation of the original written form and not the adequate phonetic rendition [nadj]. Most Hungarians in Canada retain the original letters of their surnames without retaining the diacritical marks. How is the choice made as to whether to retain or modify the spelling? As far as the individual is concerned, if the name no longer functions in its original form and creates difficulties both in spelling and pronunciation, then a modification is inevitable. Those who prefer phonetic fidelity make spelling modifications in an attempt to have their names pronounced the Hungarian way. This is achieved by modifying the orthographic form in accordance with the sound-letter relationship of the English language. The choice in spelling is decided by the bearer of the name, who in most cases is not trained linguistically: therefore, the exact duplication of the Hungarian pronunciation is not achieved. As a rule, it cannot be achieved because only closely cognate languages offer orthographic forms which are suitable for exact phonetic translation. For example, Németh [ne:met] ‘German’ becomes Namath; Császár [cha:sa:r] ‘emperor’ > Chasar; Csomós [chomo:sh] ‘knotty’ > Chomos; Szabó [sabo:] ‘tailor’ > Sabo; Juhász [juha:s]



13
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
‘shepherd’ > Yuhas, and so on. Even then, the name is pronounced largely on the basis of sound similarity because there is no common word stock to consider between the two languages. Most orthographic changes are restricted to the sounds which are denoted by two letter combinations in the Hungarian language and are unfamiliar to English speakers. Some names are shortened in the process. An artist called Devecseri signs his name as Devy, which still sounds extraneous to speakers of the English language, but can be pronounced easily. The number of modified names is statistically low; I estimate them at less than three percent of the approximately 10,000 different Hungarian surnames in Canada. However, it is very unlikely that all individuals having the same surname would choose alteration, as most Hungarians maintain the original spelling as closely as permitted by the English alphabet, even if they must endure phonological mistreatment and have their names assume different meanings in the original Hungarian. The finding that the vast majority of Hungarian immigrants to Canada retain original spellings is natural and expected. Forces stronger than linguistic conformity are at play. A family name continues the lineage from generation to generation. By immigrating, an individual severs all patriotic and family ties, his name may be the only piece of heritage left. In the same vein, a family name is part of any individual’s personal identity and is difficult to surrender or modify.
Surname changes
Complete surname changes among Hungarian-Canadians are very rare, I am aware of only one. András György, a tailor, changed his surname to George for general use. For his clients, he is Mr. Andy George, but his identification papers remained unchanged and carry his original surname György. He established a dual identity: one for official registration and one for business purposes.
CONCLUSION
Hungarian immigrants to Canada have the choice of retaining the original form of their names, including the diacritical marks. However, retention of the diacritical marks, unfamiliar and unrecognizable to Canadian speakers of the official languages, would not only be ineffective but almost impossible, due to the characteristics of the Hungarian alphabet. The original Hungarian pronunciation always changes in Canada because there are no exactly equivalent phonetic values and the succession of sounds is unfamiliar. Phonetic shapes, familiar to English speaking Canadians, have to be found which conform to the familiar combination of sounds. Thus, it would appear to be axiomatic that names from a non-cognate language cannot retain their original forms in a society where an official language is neither phonetically nor orthographically equipped to sustain the non-cognate forms.
GLOSSARY OF SURNAMES
ABA Aba [aba]. An old Hungarian clan name, appears to be derived from the Sumerian ab ‘father' or luab-ba 'elder, old man' (Labat 1948: No. 128). Aba was part of the old



14
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Magyar vocabulary meaning 'father' (Ladó 1978: 125). It was the family name of king Aba Sámuel (990-1061) a descendant of the Aba clan. Present-day spelling is apa. Aba is not only a surname but also part of place names mostly in Borsod-AbaújZemplén County. The name and its variants are present in languages other than Hungarian, cf. Akkadian abu 'father' (Labat 1948: No. 145), Latin abavus 'greatgrandfather', old Turkish apa 'ancestor' (Clauson 1972: 5). "Apa tarxan in Chinese references seem to be interpreted as commander-in-chief" (Clauson 1972: 5). Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 Oba; 1240 aba. ABAFI Abafi [abafi]. The patronymic suffix -fi indicates a 'son or descendant’ of Aba. See above for etymology. ABADI Abádi [aba:di]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from the village of Abád, a place which was the location of a ferry on the Tisza river in Heves County near present-day Abádszalók. This area belonged to the Aba clan around the beginning of the second millennium, thus the reference to Aba. The grammatical designator -d is suffixed to the name to denote a place. See Aba for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 960 Obad; 1251 Abaad, Abad. ABAY Abay [abai]. The word-final element -y is the archaic form of the present-day derivational suffix -i that indicates a descendant of Aba. See Aba for etymology. ABDAI. Abdai [abdai]. The name denotes place of origin, a person from Abda a village in Győr-Sopron County. It is comprised of the Sumerian ab 'father', the suffix -da that denotes a place and the derivational suffix -i. See Aba for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling for the toponym Abda: 1153 Obda; 1221 Abada; 1235 Abda. ABODY Abody [abodi]. The archaic derivational suffix -y (present-day -i) indicates a person from the village of Abod in Borsod-Abaúj-Zemplén County. The name of the village is derived from the Sumerian Aba 'father' to which the grammatical designator -d is suffixed to indicate a place. See Aba for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling of Abód: 1267 Obod; 1284 Obud; 1332 Opud, Abod. ABONYI Abonyi [abonji]. Someone from the village of Abony in Pest County was named so. The name is comprised of the Sumerian ab 'father', the suffix -ony which in this case forms a collective noun used for the identification of a place associated with the Aba clan and the derivational suffix -i indicating origin. See Aba for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1466 Abon. ACS Ács [a:ch]. An occupational name given to carpenters. It is also the name of a village in Komárom County, where a group of carpenters settled in 1260 (Kiss 1978: 38). Old Hungarian spellings: 1233 Alch; 1533 Acz; 1454 Alcz. The Hungarian ács 'carpenter' may be derived from old Turkish cf. Turkmen agačci 'woodworker' which is compounded from agač 'tree, wood' (agaç in modern Turkish) and the occupational suffix -ci [dzhi]. Another possible source may be the Sumerian ab.uš 'carpenter' (Gostony 1975: 139). ADANYI Adányi [ada:nji]. Also spelled Adonyi. The name contains the root ad 'give, hand over', the formative suffix -ány and the derivational suffix -i. It indicates the descendant of a person who was given up for adoption. The verb ad may be of Finno-Ugric origin, similar but not identical words exist in some of the cognate languages, c. f. Finnish antaa. See Adonyi for more explanation.



15
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
The place name Adony in Fejér County is named after its founder. The name was spelled Odun in 1200 and Odon in 1332 (Kiss 1978: 39). AFRA Afra [afra]. 'A wanderer or drifter'. The name is the root of the regional expression afrangol 'to wander, to drift' (B. Lőrinczy 1979: 157, vol. 1). AGG Agg [ag]. 'Very old, aged'. The Hungarian agg may be derived from the Sumerian ukkin 'old' (Labat 1948: No. 40). Old Hungarian spelling: 1315 Og; 1372 ag; 1510 agg; 1337 Ogh; 1436 Ag. AGYAS Agyas [adjash]. 'Clever, intelligent'. The old meaning of the Hungarian agy, spelled ag in 1405, was 'skull' and the brain inside it was called agyvelő 'skull-marrow' (Benkő 1967: 105-106, vol. 1). Present-day dictionaries mistakenly list agy as 'brain'. Agy may be an imperfect rendering of the Sumerian ugu 'skull' (Labat 1948: No. 412). The word-final element -as is the adjectival suffix. Old Hungarian spelling: 1353 Agyas. AJKAY Ajkay [ajkai]. 'One who came from Ajka, a city in Veszprém County. The city was named after a person called Ayka (Kiss 1978: 42). The name may be derived from ajak 'lip' and became a surname probably for some odd configuration of the lip. The root ajak is followed by the archaic adjectival suffix -a to implicate condition and the archaic derivational suffix -y to signify origin. The second a in ajak was omitted by syncopation. The origin of ajak is obscure, it may be derived from aj 'opening' according to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language. But ajak 'lips', the plural of aj, suggests differently. The word aj may have denoted the edge or border of anything in the remote past of the Hungarian language. It is still used in this sense as a prefix for border names and names of small valleys or indentation on the side of a mountain among the Székelys of Transylvania (B. Lőrinczy 1979: 173, vol.1). Old Hungarian spelling: 1284 Ayka. AKACHY Akáchy [aka:tsi]. 'Dweller by the acacia grove' as indicated by the archaic derivational suffix -y. The word akác 'acacia' is an international loan word derived from the Greek akakia 'a point' like a thorn. The akác tree has an overabundance of thorns on its branches. Old Hungarian spelling: 1783 ákátz, Akátz; 1788 akász; 1811 Acatia; 1861 ágácz. ALFOLDI. Alföldi [alføldi]. Also spelled Alföldy. 'A dweller in the lowlands' may have been named so. The name is comprised of the prefix al- 'low', föld 'land' and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) that indicates place of origin. Old Hungarian spelling: 1452 Alfewldy. ALMAS Almás. [alma:sh]. 'Seller or grower of apples'. The adjectival suffix -ás is attached to alma 'apple' to indicate occupation. The Hungarian word alma is shared with the Altaic languages cf. Kipchak alma, Chagatai alma, Osmanli elma, Chuvash ulma (Benkő 1967: 140, vol. 1). Similar forms exist in most Turkic languages. Old Hungarian spelling: 1093 Almas. ALPAR Alpár [alpa:r]. The surname may be derived from the old Turkish alp 'hero' and er 'man'. It is also a place name in Csongrád County and part of place names in other parts of Hungary, such as Abaújalpár. Old Hungarian spelling: 896 Olpar, Opar; 1075 Alpar; 1193 Arpario. ANDA Anda [anda]. Anda is the name of an ancient Asian custom practiced by the Mongols, [Scythians and Huns] that recognizes kinship by sworn brotherhood between



16
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
non-relatives (Kelly 2008: 51). The ceremony is known as blood oath, where the blood of the covenanters is mingled together in wine and then drunk. This is not a Magyar custom; it was practiced by their Hun and Hungar ruling caste in antiquity. Translated into the Magyar language, it is called vérszerződés. ANTAL Antal [antal]. Also spelled Antha, Anthal, Anton, Antóny Antos. This name may be the Hungarian variant of the Roman Antonius (Ladó 1978: 132) which has been translated as 'deserving praise' (Thatcher 1971: 329). The Sumerian an-ta-lu 'chief, boss' is also a possibility, an-ta means ‘above’ and lu 'man, male' (Deimel 1939: No. 12, 191). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Antu; 1208 Antoneu, Anthoneu; 1211 Anta; 1215 Anthonius, Anthonio. APATHY Apáthy [apa:ti]. 'Descendant of the abbot'. The name is a relic from the past when Roman Catholic priests were not required to be celibate. Apát is the Hungarian equivalent of the English abbot 'head of a group of monks in a monastery'. The letter h marks the reduced plosiveness of the preceding t. The word-final element -y is the archaic grapheme for the present-day derivational suffix -i. Another interpretation of the name may be 'of the abbey', denoting an employee in the household of an abbot. The word apát is comprised of the root apa 'father' and the suffix -át which converts verbs and nouns to derived nouns. The ultimate source of apa is the Sumerian ab 'father'. See Aba for etymology. The variants of apát are present in languages other than Hungarian. Cf. Latin abbas, Syrian abba, English abbot, French abbé, German Abt, western Slavic opat. Old Hungarian spelling: 1182 Apati; 1211 Apat; 1291 Abbati. APOR Apor [apor]. A difficult name to etymologize. The earliest known Apor was a military leader in 934 (Györffy 1975: 295). May be derived from the old Turkish apar ‘unruly, disobedient’ (Németh 1991: 124-125). APPONYI Apponyi [aponji]. A variant of Abonyi. ARADI. Aradi [aradi]. 'A person from Arad' a city in Arad of historical Hungary, confined to Romania in 1920. The name is derived from the Hungarian úr 'sir, lord, gentleman' (Kiss 1978: 59), which in turn is derived from the Sumerian ur 'man, hero’ (Labat 1948: No. 575). The grammatical designator -ad denotes a place. The derivational suffix -i indicates place of origin. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Vradi, Vrodi, Vrovdi, Arud, Arod, Arudi, Arhudi; 1202 Orod; 1237 Arad. ARANY Arany [aranj]. The Hungarian arany 'gold' may be derived from the Sumerian ar 'to shine' (Labat 1948: No. 451). The nominal suffix -any converts a verb into a noun. The Sumerian ara 'to glow' (Labat 1948: No. 451) may also be considered, though it appears to be an adjective in view of the suffix -a: 'glowing'. The name may have been bestowed on a wealthy individual or golden-haired (blond) person. Similar words are in the Finno-Ugric languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *sarańa (Benkő 1967: 170, vol. 1). Also compare with Latin aurum, Avestan zaranyaand Sanskrit hiranya-. Old Hungarian spelling: 1267 Aran-; 1391 Arany. ARATO Arató [arato:]. Also spelled Arathó, the h marks the reduced plosiveness of the



17
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
preceding t. The present participle -ó forms an adjective from arat 'to reap, to harvest, to cut down'. Arató 'harvester' denotes a seasonal worker in agriculture. If the -at ending in arat is the frequentative suffix, which it appears to be, then the root of the word would be ar. In that case the Hungarian ar is identical with the Sumerian ar4 'reap, harvest' (Labat 1948: No. 594). Similar words exist in languages other than Hungarian cf. Vogul urel, old Turkish or and Tatar ur 'reap, harvest' (Benkő 1967: 172, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling: 1423 Aratho; 1461 Arato. ARGYELAN Árgyelán [a:rdjela:n]. Árgyelán is a name for a Transylvanian in Moldavia. ARVA Árva [a:rva]. 'Orphan'. Also the name of a river in historical Hungary now in Slovakia renamed Orava. Similar words exist in other languages, cf. Finnish orpo, Latin orbus, Greek orphanos, Ostyak urui.
Old Hungarian spelling: 1152 Arua. ASZODI Aszódi [aso:di]. Also spelled Aszódy. 'One who came from Aszód', a town in Pest County. The name contains the following elements: aszó 'intermittent stream', the grammatical designator -d denoting a place and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicating place of origin. Old Hungarian spelling: 1401 Aza; 1647 Aszód. ASZTALOS Asztalos [astalosh]. ‘Joiner’, literally 'maker of tables'. Compound of asztal 'table', and the adjectival suffix –os. An occupational name, describes a craftsman specializing in making furniture. The word asztal appears to be of old Greek origin, derived from astilos 'post, pillar'. Tables and the stools around them were not always portable as they are now and the floors in houses were of compacted earth. The legs of a table were four posts, dug into the floor to support a slab of wood which served as a table-top. One short post for each stool was also dug into the floor around the table. As it happened, the Greeks call their table trapeze 'a flat surface', e.g. table-top, while Hungarians put the emphasis on the raised position of the structure by its legs and called it asztal. Old Hungarian spellings for asztal: 1293 oztol; 1354 aztal. AVAR Avar [avar]. Name of the Turkic people who ruled the upper Danube valley, the Carpathian Basin and the regions extending beyond Central Asia in the east after the collapse of the Hun Empire. The Avars shaped the course of history in the Carpathian Basin prior to the arrival of Árpád's people who were also of Turkish stock. The name Avar is derived from the old Turkish tribal name Apar 'unruly, disobedient' (Németh 1991: 124-125). Old Hungarian spelling: 950 Avar; 1760 Avar; 1783 Abar; 1801 Abár. BAB Bab [bab]. 'Bean'. The name may indicate a person small in stature, cf. Babszem Jankó 'Johny Bean seed', a diminutive character in Hungarian fairy tales. Hungarians share the word for bean with Slavonic speaking people where it is spelled bob. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Bab. BACS Bács [ba:ch]. A name of Turkic origin. In modern Turkish baş means 'head, chief, leader' (Akdikmen 1986: 43), denoting a civilian dignitary or a military governor. It was also the name of a County and a city in the southern part of the Bácska region of historical Hungary. The area was confined to Serbia in 1920 and renamed



18
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Vojvodina.
Old Hungarian spelling: 1071 Bach; 1111 Bache; 1252 Baach; 1590 Bacz; 1692 bács-. BADA Bada [bada]. A variant of Buda. See Buda. BAJAN Baján [baja:n]. Derived from the name of an Avar military leader who died in AD 602. It was a high ranking Avar military and administrative title derived from the Turkish bay 'rich', "with extended meanings like 'a member of the upper classes’ occasionally 'a man of distinction' " (Clauson 1972: 384). Wealth and leadership were synonymous in antiquity, thus the title. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Boion. BAJTAR Bajtár [bajta:r]. Hungarian rendition of the Turkish baytar 'veterinarian' (Akdikmen 1986: 46). BAJUSZ Bajusz [bajus]. 'Mustache'. A name probably given to one who had a thick prominent mustache. Old Hungarian spelling: 1522 Baÿws; 1535 baiuz; 1552 baiusz; 1604 Bajutz; 1780 bajusz. BAJZA Bajza [bajza]. A regional expression in Tiszadob (Szabolcs-Szatmár County) denoting a 'playful, merry' person (B. Lőrinczy 1979: 313, vol. 1). BAKA Baka [baka]. 'Foot-soldier'. It may be derived from boka 'ankle', the onetime name of 'ankle-boots', the standard foot-ware in the Hungarian army, which transformed into the name of soldiers wearing them (Benkő 1967: 221, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling: 1135 Baca; 1227 Boca; 1418 Baka. BAKO Bakó [bako:]. 'Executioner, hangman'. A derivation from the obsolete bak ‘chopping-block’ to which the participle -ó is suffixed to indicate an occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1271 Bakov; 1338 Bakou; 1345 Bakow; 1418 Bako. BAKSAY Baksay [bakshai]. 'Descendant of Baksa' as indicated by the archaic derivational suffix -y. Baksa is the old Hungarian name for csikó 'foal' (Szamota 19021906: 44). It is also the name of a village in Baranya County probably named so for horse-breeding. BALLA Balla [bala]. The name has no meaning in the current Hungarian language but it means 'cap' in Sumerian (Labat 1948: No. 536). A cap-maker may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1214 Balu, Ballu. BAL Bal [bal]. Literally bal means 'left' but metaphorically the word is associated with anything ‘bad, ill or adverse’. Cf. balsors 'misfortune', baleset 'accident', balsiker 'failure'. In the literal sense the surname Bal may denote a 'left-handed' person but the metaphoric perception suggests an 'ill tempered' or 'accident prone' individual. The Sumerian bal 'hostile, adverse' (Gostony 1975: 42) is similar to the metaphoric sense of the Hungarian bal, both denote unfavorable conditions. Old Hungarian spelling: 1181 Bol; 1213 Bolu; 1372 bal. BALGA Balga [balga]. A variant of bolygó 'wanderer'. Originally a 'vagrant' was called balga (Benkő 1967: 229, vol. 1), but the word is now describes an 'unassuming, naive' person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1343 Bolgo-; 1359 Bolgow-; 1484 Balgo; 1496 Bolygo; 1777 Balga. BALINT Bálint [ba:lint]. Hungarian variant of the Roman Valentinus 'strong, healthy'.



19
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
The letters v and b are regarded transposable. The Roman name originates from the Latin valens, valeo 'strong, muscular'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1558 Baint; 1577 Walent, Walint, Walynth; 1584 Balinth; 1587 Bálint. BALLAGI Ballagi [balagi]. The reduplicated l marks a stressed consonant. The name is comprised of ballag 'walk slowly' and the derivational suffix -i indicating the descendant of a slow walker. Old Hungarian spelling: 1504 Ballago; 1519 ballag-. BALOG Balog [balog]. Also spelled Balogh. 'Left-handed', derived from bal 'left'. The og ending is an archaic suffix that denoted a permanent condition like in hályog 'cataract'. Balog was also a clan name in Gömör County of historical Hungary in AD 900 near a tributary of the Rima River called Balog (Györffy 1987: 463-465). The area is now confined to Slovakia and renamed Vel'ký Blh. According to historical sources members of the Hungarian border patrol were stationed there (Györffy 1987: 463-465), which would indicate the Sumerian bulug 'boundary, frontier'(Labat 1948: No. 96) as the possible alternative source of the name. Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 bolug; 1297 Balug; 1338 Bolugh; 1391 Balogh; 1381 Balog. BALSA Balsa [balsha]. The archaic form of balsó 'on the left' that may indicate someone dwelling on the left side of a geographical feature. Balsa is also the name of a populated place in Szabolcs-Szatmár County first recorded in 1291. BALTAS Baltás [balta:sh]. The adjectival suffix -ás indicates a user, maker or seller of balta 'axe, hatchet'. The word balta is present in all Turkic languages (Clauson 1972: 333), but may have originated in the Sumerian language, cf. bal 'hatchet, axe' (Gostony 1975: 80). Also compare with Akkadian pâltu 'axe' (Labat 1948: No. 595). Old Hungarian spelling: 1429 Baltha. BAN Bán [ba:n]. Also spelled Baán. The name was originally the archaic title of the warden for the Bánát in the southern regions of historical Hungary and the viceroy of Croatia, Slavonia and Dalmatia. The title is similar to the Hungarian verb bán (-ik) 'to treat, to handle, to manage'. The suffix -ik marks a reflexive verb. The Sumerians used both ban and pan to denote an estate held by a superior on condition of feudal obligations (Labat 1948: No. 439). A person of distinction was called banî in the Akkadian language (Labat 1948: No. 144). Old Hungarian spelling: 1171 ban; 1211 Ban-; 1289 Baan-; 1309 Ban. BANATI Bánáti [ba:na:ti]. Also spelled Bánáthy, 'one who came from the Bánát', as indicated by the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). Bánát is a region at the south-east corner of the Great Hungarian Plain. This area was divided then confined to Romania and Serbia in 1920. The name is derived from bán 'viceroy', see above for detailed etymology. The suffix -át converts verbs and nouns into derived nouns. BANYASZ Bányász [ba:nja:s]. The formative suffix –ász attached to bánya 'mine, quarry' indicates a specialized occupation: 'miner'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1463 Banyaz; 1602 Baniaz, Bányász. BARA Bara [bara]. This name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian



20
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
language. The most probable origin would be the Sumerian bar 'leather' which compares favorably with the Hungarian bőr 'leather' (Labat 1948: No. 74). The word-final element a is the Sumerian and ancient Hungarian adjectival suffix signifying a 'leather-worker' or 'seller of leather goods'. The modern equivalent of the archaic Bara is bőrös 'a dealer in leather goods'. The old form was preserved in the name Baros and Baross. BARANY Bárány [ba:ra:nj]. 'Lamb'. One may have been named so for tending lambs or for being docile. The Hungarian bárány may be derived from the Sumerian udubar-rinna 'lamb' (Gostony 1975: 80 (#650). Similar words exist in the Slavonic languages cf. Polish baran 'ram'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1194 Baran; 1598 Báránt; 1604 Bárány. BARCS Barcs [barch]. The name of a village in Somogy County bears the name of its founder. The surname Barcs is of uncertain origin. It may be derived from the Turkish burç 'tower, bastion', or the also Turkish bars 'leopard'. See below for more explanation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1279 Barch. BARCZA Barcza [bartsa]. The root barc may be the Hungarian variant of the old Turkish bars 'leopard' (Kiss 1978: 91) spelled pars in modern Turkish. The archaic Hungarian adjectival suffix -a suggests a person who is 'like a leopard' either by appearance or in character. The name may have been bestowed on a nobleman wearing the traditional kacagány made of leopard skin thrown loosely over one shoulder. A fashionable garb among the Turkish ruling class in medieval Hungary. Place names derived from Barca were numerous in the Transylvanian region of historical Hungary until confined to Romania in 1920. Barcaság was a locality in the south-east part of Transylvania. Barcánfalva now called Bîrsana, Barcaszentpéter now called Sînpetru, Barcaújfalu now called Satu Nou. The Polish barc 'beehive' and the Italian barca 'boat' have no relevance to the Hungarian name. Old Hungarian spelling: 1272 Borch; 1278 Barch. BAROSS The reduplicated ‘s’ indicates a stressed consonant. The name is derived from the Sumerian bar ‘skin, leather’ (Labat 1948: cuneiform no. 74), cf. Hungarian bőr ‘skin, leather’. The adjectival suffix -os indicates an occupation: maker of leather goods. The modern Hungarian equivalent is bőrös. BARS Bars [barsh]. The name of a county and its first steward in medieval Hungary (Kiss 1978: 93). The entire county was confined to Slovakia in 1920. The village Óbars was renamed Stary Tekov. The name is a probable derivation from the Turkish pars ‘leopard’. The form bars is shared with very early Iranian, probably a loan-word. It is one of the animals of the 12 year Turkic animal cycle (Clauson 1972: 368). BARTA Barta [barta]. Also spelled Bartha. A common Hungarian surname compounded of two Sumerian elements: bar 'circular wall' (Deimel 1939: No. 49) and ta 'outside' (Labat 1948: No. 89). The present-day Hungarian equivalent of bar is vár 'fort'. The letters b and v are regarded as transposable. Várta 'sentry-post' seems to be the phonetically advanced form of Barta. Sentries were always posted outside the walls of forts and this is what both barta and várta describe. The name may have become popular as a collective noun when



21
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
families settled in the vicinity of fortifications and were referred to as bartas, i.e. those outside the wall of the fort. Old Hungarian spelling: 1576 Bartha; 1595 Barta. BATO Bató [bato:]. The name is comprised of the Sumerian bat 'bat, scepter, stave' (Waddell 1927: 23), identical with the Hungarian bot both in meaning and phonetic value. The Hungarian participle -ó suffixed to bat indicates a 'person in charge'. Names derived from bot 'stick' denoted leaders in ancient Hungarian society, cf. botos 'overseer' (B. Lőrinczy 1979: 574, vol. 1). The oldest record of Bató is noted in AD 6 as the name of two leaders when the Pannonians and their neighbors the Illirs had risen against Roman rule (Cook 1934: 369, vol. 10). BATOR Bátor [ba:tor]. 'Brave, daring'. The name is of old Turkic origin cf. Osmanli batir 'brave, hero', Mongolian bayatur 'hero, brave'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Bahatur; 1177 Batur; 1323 Bathor-. BAZSA Bazsa [bazha]. A derivation from bazsaly(-og) 'a concealed smile' (Benkő 1967: 258, vol. 1). A person habitually smiling in such a way may have been named Bazsa. The name is comprised of the root bazs and the archaic adjectival suffix -a to indicate character. The word bazsaly is a regional dialect derived from mosoly 'smile'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Basa, Bosa. BEKES Békés [be:ke:sh]. 'Peaceful, gentle'. The name contains béke 'peace' and the present-day adjectival suffix -s. It is also the name of a county and a city in Hungary. Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 bekes; 1202 Beques; 1215 Bikes. BELA Béla [be:la]. One of the most difficult Hungarian names to interpret. The following explanations have been proposed: 1. From the archaic Hungarian word bél 'internal parts' of the body (Kálmán 1978: 42) [present meaning is 'intestines'] and the suffix –a. 2. From the Turkic languages where it means 'noble'. 3. The endearing form of the Slavic Boleslav. 4. From the Hebrew Bela 'swallower of earth'. 5. The endearing form of the German Berta or Bella (Ladó 1978: 138). 6. Akkadian bêlu cognate with ba'al 'lord'. The most probable source for the name may be the Sumerian language where be means 'lord, nobleman' (Labat 1948: No. 69, 99), and lu is 'man' (Labat 1948: No. 330). The Semitic Akkadians borrowed it in the form of bêlu. The Turkish bey 'sir, ruler' (Akdikmen 1986: 49) may have also been derived from the Sumerian be. Old Hungarian spelling: 1092 Bela; 1168 beel; 1219 Belh; 1221 Bella; 1597 Bélla. BEREND Berend [berend]. The surname Berend is derived from the name of an old Turkic tribe that settled in Keve County of historical Hungary in the 11th century. The place of their settlement was also called Berend at first and then changed to Baranda (Györffy 1987: 315, vol. 3). This area was confined to Serbia in 1920 but the name remained Baranda. Berend may be derived from the old Turkic *berendi 'one who surrendered' (Kiss 1978: 702). Old Hungarian spelling: 1340 Berend.



22
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
BERES Béres [be:resh]. 'Farmhand'. Derived from bér 'wage' and suffixed by the adjective -es it denotes a ‘hired hand’. The word béres may have been derived from the Sumerian ubara 'fees, dues, settlement' (Gostony 1975: 65). Old Hungarian spelling: 1387 Beres; 1602 Béres. BESE. Bese [beshe]. The old name of a raptorial bird, probably of present-day sólyom 'falcon'. It appears to be cognate with the Uighur baša 'falcon, hawk' (Benkő 1967: 288, vol. 1). A keeper or trainer of falcons may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Bese. BESENYO Besenyő [beshenjø:]. The name is derived from the ethnic denomination Besenyő 'Pecheneg'. It was the name of a Turkic people who settled in Hungary during the second half of the 10th century. They dispersed throughout historical Hungary. Records show the presence of Pechenegs in about fifty localities. The counties with substantial Pecheneg population are Heves, Fejér, Győr, Nyitra, Sopron, Csanád, Tolna and Somogy (Ács 1986: 32, 33). The origin of their name is obscure, possibly derived from the Turkic bača 'brother-in-law' according to one source (Németh 1991: 85). Another possible origin is the Uighur baša 'falcon, hawk', denoting a people being experts in falconry. The present form for Besenyő in the Turkish language is baçanak (Akdikmen 1986: 37). Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 Beseneu; 1211 Beseney; 1279 Besenev; 1322 Bessenew; 1455 Besenyw. BESZEDES Beszédes [bese:esh]. The adjectival suffix -es is added to beszéd 'speech, oration' to indicate a ‘chatty’ person or one ‘having oratorical skills’. Though the word beszéd is shared with some of the Slavonic languages (cf. Slovak beseda) its ultimate origin may be the Sumerian bé 'to speak, to talk' (Labat 1948: No. 214). Old Hungarian spelling: 1403 Bezedes; 1575 beszédes. BIRO Bíró [bi:ro:]. The participle -ó is suffixed to the verb bir 'to have power' (Benkő 1967: 305, vol. 1). Before the establishment of courts of law, distinguished members of communities were vested with authority to settle disputes between litigious parties. Those people were called bíró 'judge'. In small communities, up to the end of World War II, a bíró was elected to govern and to maintain the law. He was also the chief administrator of the village. Old Hungarian spelling: 1306 Byrou; 1354 Byrow, Byro; 1423 Biro. BOD Bod [bod]. The archaic spelling of the present-day bot 'stick, club'. This kind of name was given to a leader in ancient Hungarian society. The letters t and d are regarded transposable. See Buda for additional pertinent detail. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Bod. BODA Boda [boda]. A variant of Buda, the archaic form of the current Botos 'one with a stick'. The name is comprised of bod the old form of bot 'stick, club' and the archaic adjectival suffix -a. It denotes a leader. See Buda for additional detail. Old Hungarian spelling: 1202 Boda. BODO Bodó [bodo:]. The participle -ó is suffixed to bod the archaic form of the current bot 'stick, club' to indicate a leader. See Bod, Boda and Buda for more detail. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Bodou. BODOGH Bódogh [bo:dog]. The name is the archaic form of the present-day boldog



23
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
'happy, joyous'. The letter h marks the reduced plosion of the preceding g. The Hungarian boldog may be derived from the Sumerian ba-dug or ba-a-u-dug 'happy (Gostony 1975: 22, 143). Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 Bodogh; 1700 boldog. BOJTAR Bojtár [bojta:r]. 'Young herdsman'. Bojt appears to be a variant of bujt 'initiate movement or action' (Benkő 1967: 383, vol. 1). The element -ár corresponds to the Sumerian úr 'man' (Labat 1948: No. 575), affected by vowel harmony. Old Hungarian spelling: 1660 bujtár; 1742 Bojtár; 1898 bajtár. BOKOR Bokor [bokor]. 'Bush'. The name suggests a dweller by a bushy, scrubby area. Old Hungarian spelling: 1055 bukur; 1095 bucur; 1193 Bocor; 1330 bukur; 1415 Bokor. BOR Bor [bor]. A maker or seller of wine may have been named so. The Hungarian bor 'wine' is also present in the Turkic languages cf. Uighur bor, Kipchak bor (Benkő 1967: 341, vol. 1). However, the ultimate source may be the Sumerian bur 'wine' (Gostony 1975: 72, 85). Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 bor. BOROS Bőrös [bø:røsh]. 'Leather-dealer, tanner'. The adjectival suffix -ös is attached to bőr 'leather' to indicate occupation. The Hungarian bőr has a dual meaning 'skin' and 'leather', same as the Sumerian bar ( Labat 1948: No. 74). Old Hungarian spelling: 1219 Boroch. BORSOS Borsos [borshosh]. The name is comprised of bors 'pepper' and the adjectival suffix -os and denotes a ‘seller of peppers’. The word bors is present in the Turkic languages in the form of burç and murç, probably derived from the Iranian marc (Benkő 1967: 349, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling: 1075 Bors; 1215 Burs; 1395 bors; 1469 bars-. BOTA. Bota [bota]. The archaic form of the present-day Botos 'one with a stick'. The archaic adjectival suffix is affixed to bot 'stick, club' to indicate a 'leader, overseer'. See Bod, Boda and Buda for more detail. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Bota, Bote, Bata. BOTOS Botos [botosh]. An official entrusted with the administration and security of a community is called botos in a regional dialect (B. Lőrinczy 1979: 574, vol. 1). Literally, botos means 'one who carries a staff, stick'. The name is comprised of bot 'stick, staff' and the adjectival suffix -os to indicate status or rank. The Hungarian bot is probably related to the Turkish budak 'twig, branch'. See Buda for more detail and etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Botus, butus; 1347 Buthus; 1356 Bothus; 1426 Bothos; 1602 Botos. BUCSUS Búcsús [bu:chu:sh]. A búcsús could be either a participant in a pilgrimage to a holy place or a merchant who sells devotional articles to pilgrims. The name is comprised of búcsú 'pilgrimage' and the adjectival suffix -s. The Hungarian búcsú may be derived from the Sumerian búr-ša, búr-šu 'deliverance of heart' (Gostony 1975: 49). Old Hungarian spelling of búlcsú: 1095 buchu; 1198 Bulchov; 1199 Bolchu; 1210 Bulsuu; 1211 Bolchou, Bolsou. BUDA Buda [buda]. Originally an ancient title or rank, Buda was preserved in surnames and place names in historical Hungary. The name is comprised of bud the old form of the



24
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
present-day bot 'bat, stick, staff' and the archaic adjectival suffix -a, that was present in the Sumerian and old Hungarian languages. The bat or staff, an ancient symbol of authority, lent its name to denote a leader or ruler and became a title both in the Sumerian and old Hungarian cultures. The Hungarian bud is the variant of the Sumerian bad, bat 'bat, club, stick (Nogrady 1996: 52). The Buda segment in Budapest signifies that the head of state, the buda resided there. The following Hungarian surnames are derived from the same root as Buda: Bot, Both, Bod, Bud, Bada, Boda, Bató; Bota; Bata; Batu; Batta; Botus; Botos; Bodos; Budus; Butus; Bothu; Boton; Botond; Bodon; Bodun; Botona. The earliest known record of Buda is from the fifth century, the name of a local leader, mistakenly identified as the brother of Attila, king of the Huns. BUJDOSO Bújdosó [bu:jdosho:]. Derived from búj 'hide', followed by the connecting consonant d, the adjectival suffix -os and the participle -ó, bujdosó is a 'fugitive, refugee'. Hungary had plenty of refugees during her turbulent history. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Budusuo; 1416 budoso-. CAKO Cakó [tsako:]. Also spelled Czakó. The old name for the present-day gólya 'stork'. The dweller of a house that had a stork nest may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1426 Chako; 1565 czakô; 1629 czákó; 1808 cika. CSABA Csaba [chaba]. One of the oldest personal names in Hungary that, according to historians, was given to the youngest son of Attila, king of the Huns. He was Attila's favored son, designated to succeed him in leading the Hun nation. The name has always been associated with the Turkish language by Hungarian linguists. It is supposed to be a derivation from the Chagatai čaba 'gift' (Kiss 1978: 99) or from the Osmanli Turk çoban 'shepherd, wanderer’ (Ladó 1978: 146). The problem with the Osmanli Turk derivation is that the word çoban, Hungarian spelling csobán, was absent in the Hungarian vocabulary until 1579 (Benkő 1967: 546, vol. 1), well after the Turkish conquest in 1526. Furthermore, the Turkish çoban appears to be a derivation from the Persian shaban 'shepherd' (Lambton 1954: 93). In addition, calling the crown prince of the Hun ruling caste a shepherd would have been inconsistent with contemporary Turkish naming practices, which tended to indicate noble origin. But Prince Csaba, without the Turkish connection, is a legendary figure in Hungarian folklore; he is supposed to return, riding along the Milky Way, to liberate the Magyars from foreign oppression. His name can be etymologized in the Sumerian language: ša6 means 'favorable, preferred' (Labat 1948: No. 356) and be (listed as ba9 in the index) means 'lord' (Labat 1948: No. 69). The compound šaba would then mean 'favorable lord'. The sound change from s [English sh, Sumerian š] to cs [English ch] frequently occurs in the Hungarian language, cf. sajka > csajka 'boat'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1151 Caba; 1237 Chaba. CSAKANY Csákány [tsa:ka:nj]. Also present in the Slavonic and Turkic languages in similar forms, csákány 'pick-axe' denotes a maker or seller of pick-axes. The most likely origin of the root csák is the Sumerian sig 'to hit, to crush' (Labat 1948: No. 295). It compares with the Hungarian csap 'to hit, to strike' and the Turkish çakmak 'to hit, to strike'. The nominal suffix -ány is appended to the root word to transform it into a noun. Old Hungarian spelling: 1277 Chakan; 1490 Chykan; 1520 czakan; 1602 Csákán, Csákány. It is also a surname in Kazakhstan and Kirghizstan spelled Čakan (Benkő



25
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
19967: 469, vol. 1). CSAKO Csákó [cha:ko:]. A maker or supplier of csákó 'shako, cap' to the Hungarian military may have been named so. The shape of a modern csákó resembles a truncated cone but it resembled a tall sugar loaf in antiquity, ending in a point. The point was bent down to one side occasionally. The word csákó is comprised of csák 'pointed end, tip' (Benkő 1967: 468, vol.1) and the participle -ó. Derivation from the Sumerian sag-šu4 'headgear' or the Akkadian šaku 'high, tall' (Deimel 1939: No. 252) should also be considered. Old Hungarian spelling: 1667 Csákó. CSAP Csap [tsap]. A maker of spigots may have received the name. Csap means 'tap, spigot' in the Hungarian language and signifies the action of cutting off the flow of liquid. It appears to be a derivation from the Sumerian ša5 'to cut' (Labat 1948: No. 97), or šub 'to separate, to severe' (Labat 1948: No. 68). A similar Hungarian word is szelep 'valve' derived from szel 'slice, cut'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1458 Chap; 1490 Chyap; 1591 csap-. CSEKE Cseke [cheke]. Also spelled Csekő. 'Dweller at the ford'. The old Hungarian name of a ford was cseke (B. Lőrinczy 1962: 35). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Ceke; 1211 Cheke, Cheken. CSENDES Csendes [chendesh]. 'Quiet, placid'. The name is a composite of csend 'silence, tranquility' and the adjectival suffix -es to indicate personal character. The word csend may be derived from the Sumerian šed7 'calm down' (Deimel 1939: No. 32), or še12 'calm' (Labat 1948: No. 103a). Old Hungarian spelling: 1277 Chundus; 1478 Chendes. CSEPLO Cséplő [che:plø:]. Abbreviated form of csépelő 'thresher'. The vowel e is omitted by syncopation. The participle -ő indicates occupation. The word csép may be derived from the Sumerian šub 'to separate, to drop, to fall' ((Labat 1948: No. 68). Threshing is about separating the grain from its capsule. The last act of threshing by hand was to drop the grain from a height for the wind to blow away the light grain-husks. Old Hungarian spelling: 1409 Cheplew; 1483 Cheplee; 1540 Tschyplew; 1548 czyeplo; 1602 Cséplő. CSER Cser [cher]. 'Oak'. The Hungarian cser is derived from the Latin cerus 'oak-tree'. The name indicates a person having the characteristics of an oak-tree, the symbol of strength and endurance. Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 cher; 1285 cheer. CSEREPES Cserepes [cherepesh]. 'Potter, tiler'. The name consists of cserép 'flower pot, roof-tile' and the adjectival suffix -es to indicate occupation. Cserép may be derived from the Sumerian še-ib 'tile' (Gostony 1975: 81). Old Hungarian spelling: 1337 Cheripes; 1388 Cherepes; 1584 cserepes. CSERGEI Csergei [chergei]. Also spelled Csergey and Csurgai. The derivational suffix i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Cserge. The word cser is an altered form of csor (as in csorog 'trickle, dribble') or csur (as in csurog 'trickle, dribble') and may be a derivation from the Sumerian šur 'to spout out, to gush' and 'rain, shower' (Labat 1948: No. 101). The -og ending is the Hungarian frequentative suffix. The cserge was a portable bathing apparatus of the Scythians. A leather water



26
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
tank with holes in it was placed in an elevated position and water spurted down as it does from a shower-head. The maker or caretaker of such baths was called Cserge or Csurga. This bath was recorded as tzerga by the Greek emperor Constantinus Porphyrogenitus (905-959) in his De administrando imperio. He (mistakenly) refers to it as a 'Turkish bath', namely “skytha tzerga”. This would be explained by a similar word in old Turkish: çur [chur] "an onomatopoeic for the sound of milk falling from the udder into the pail; also used of any liquid when it makes a noise" (Clauson 1972: 428). This interpretation of the Turkish çur is not convincing because identical words in cognate languages clearly describe the action of liquid gushing or trickling from a container. This action can take place with or without the presence of a pail. The -ge ending in cserge is the archaic Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a that was subjected to phonetic change by the rules of vowel harmony and changed to -e. Deimel suggests that the suffix -a is occasionally preceded by either a g or a k (Deimel 1939: 108 in section 29 Adjektiv und Adverb). Csorog is also present in the Uralic languages in various forms having the same meaning as in Hungarian and Sumerian. The reconstructed Uralic proto-word is *ćorɜ (Benkő 1967: 557, vol. 1). CSIGA Csiga [chiga]. 'Snail'. The name may mark a person for being 'slow like a snail'. The Hungarian csiga may be derived from the Sumerian šika 'shell' (snail, oyster) (Labat 1948: No. 55). Old Hungarian spelling: 1272 chyga; 1395 chÿga; 1863 tsuga-. CSILLAG Csillag [chilag]. 'Star'. A name given for no obvious reason. May be a derivation from the Sumerian zalag 'to shine' (Labat 1948: No. 393). Old Hungarian spelling: 1373 Chylagh; 1388 chilak; 1799 csillag-. CSINOS Csinos [chinosh]. 'Neat, elegant'. The name is comprised of csín 'neatness, elegance' and the adjectival suffix -os. The word csín may be derived from the Sumerian šen 'clean, distinguishing, pure' (Labat 1948: No. 8). Old Hungarian spelling: 1604 Czinos. CSORGO Csörgő [chørgø:]. Derived from csörög 'chatter' to which the participle -ő is suffixed to indicate a 'chatterer'.
