Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 Chergou; 1585 tsergo. CSUROS Csűrös [chy:røsh]. An occupational surname indicated by the adjectival suffix ös. It denotes a farm-hand who looks after the csűr 'cattle or fodder shed'. The word csűr is a probable derivation from the Sumerian šurim 'cattle shed, paddock' (Labat 1948: No. 494). Being a German loan-word derived from Scheuer [correct spelling Scheune] 'barn', as suggested by the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, is doubtful. Old Hungarian spelling: 1570 Churos. CSURGAI Csurgai [churgai]. See Csergei. CUPPAN Cuppan [tsupan]. A person giving loud kisses may have been named Cuppan 'smacker'. The root cupp may be derived from the Sumerian su-ub 'kiss' (Deimel 1939: No. 7). However, the Sumerian su-ub may mean 'suck' instead of 'kiss'. The action performed by the mouth during a kiss is similar to the vacuum created by the muscles of the lips when drawing milk into the mouth during breast feeding. Therefore the habit of



27
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
kissing may have originated either from breast or mouth to mouth feeding of infants when hard food had to be pre-chewed by the mother for her toothless baby. The Sumerian su-ub is phonetically close to the Hungarian szop [sop] 'suck' and cupp [tsup] the sound of a smack. Old Hungarian spelling: 1652 czoppan-; 1787 tzuppan-; 1805 cippan-; 1820 cuppan. DAJKA Dajka [dajka]. 'Wet nurse'. This name is a relic from the distant past. Words of similar forms and meaning are present in many other languages like old Hindi dháyati 'suck, drink'. Farsi dâyag 'wet nurse' (Benkő 1967: 876, vol. 3), Serbo-Croatian dojka 'woman's breast', Sumerian ga-i-ka 'wet nurse' (Gostony 1975: 85). The Hungarian dajka is most likely derived from tej 'milk' (phonetic values may have changed in the following order: tej > dej > daj) and the archaic adjectival suffix -a. The present-day equivalent would be tejes 'one who has milk'. The intermediate k is a connecting consonant. Old Hungarian spelling: 1359 Dejka; 1481 Dayka; 1603 Dajka. DALA Dala [dala]. The archaic form of Dalos 'singer, fond of singing'. Consists of dal 'song' and the archaic adjectival suffix -a. The Hungarian dal may be of Sumerian origin c.f. gala 'song' (Gostony 1975: 65). See Dalos. DALOS Dalos [dalosh]. Also spelled Dallos. The adjectival suffix -os is added to dal 'song' to indicate a person 'fond of singing'. See Dala for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Dolus; 1416 Dalus; 1437 Dalos. DAMA Dama [dama]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. Its source may be the Sumerian dam 'guardian, support' (Gostony 1975: 56). Dam compares favorably with the Hungarian gyám 'guardian' and tám 'support'. The word-final element a is the Sumerian and old Hungarian adjectival suffix indicating status. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Tama; 1237 Dama. DARABOS Darabos [darabosh]. The adjectival suffix -os is appended to darab 'part, piece' to indicate an awkward person who expresses himself in short unrelated sentences. The root dar may be the same as the Sumerian dar 'split, break up' (Deimel 1939: No. 93). The word-final element -ab in darab is an unproductive suffix of unidentified origin. Also compare with Sumerian dur 'part, section' (Labat 1948: No. 108). Old Hungarian spelling: 1305 dorobus; 1335 Darabus; 1407 Darabos. DARAS Darás [dara:sh]. The adjectival suffix -ás follows the root dar from which dara 'groats', darál 'grind' and darabol 'break or cut into pieces' are derived. The name Darás denotes a person involved in either the production or selling of groats. The act of grinding is in fact the breaking up grain into small pieces, an action expressed by the Sumerian dar 'to break up, to cut to pieces' (Deimel 1939: No. 93). The Turkish dari 'millet', listed in the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language as the source from which dara is derived (Benkő 1967: 593, vol. 1), is questionable. DARAZS Darázs [dara:zh]. 'Wasp'. A name given for no obvious reason. It may be derived from the Sumerian dar 'motley' (Labat 1948: No. 114). The last syllable -ázs appears to be the voiced variant of the adjectival suffix -ás. Old Hungarian spelling: 1349 Daras. DARDA Dárda [da:rda]. An occupational surname. A maker or seller of dárda 'spear,



28
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
lance' may have been named so. A soldier serving in the army as a ‘lancer’ may also have received the name. An international loan-word dárda is believed to be derived from the Latin dard 'dart' (Benkő 1967: 595, vol. 1) to which the archaic Hungarian suffix -a is suffixed. Old Hungarian spelling: 1554 darda; 1585 Dárda. DEAK Deák [dea:k]. The name is the archaic form of the current diák 'student'. It is a contracted version of the Greek diakonos 'servant, messenger'. The word was used in the Hungarian culture for a 'learned person' or 'one who pursued knowledge'. A deák was treated differently from other servants in the courts of the nobility for his ability to do work in an office. Probably it should be of interest to note that dêkû meant 'officer' in the Akkadian language. Old Hungarian spelling: 1337 -dyak; 1342 Dyak; 1402 Deeak; 1414 Deak-; 1418 Deak; 1643 deák-. DEBRECENI Debreceni [debretseni]. Also spelled Debreczeni and Debreczeny. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates geographic origin, i.e. 'one from Debrecen', a city in Hajdú-Bihar County. Debrecen is located in the vicinity of other places whose names originated in the Turkic languages. It is therefore axiomatic that this name is derived from the city's Turkish founder Dëbrësin which means 'one who lives or moves' (Németh 1990: 280). Old spelling of Debrecen: 1235 Debrezun. DER Dér [de:r]. Also spelled Deér. 'Frost'. A name given to a graying or white haired person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1234 Dera; 1291 der-. DERUS Derűs [dery:sh]. 'Cheerful'. The adjectival suffix -űs is added to der, a root from which derít 'to clear, to purify', derűlt 'clear', derül 'clear up' are derived. The word conveys the meaning of ‘pure’ in a sense of being free of contaminants. This would make it cognate with the Sumerian dúru 'pure' (Labat 1948: No. 536). DESI Dési [de:shi]. Also spelled Désy. 'One who came from Dés' a city in SzolnokDoboka County of historical Hungary indicated by the derivational suffix -i. This area has been confined to Romania in 1920 and the city's name was changed to Dej. The toponym has no meaning in the Hungarian language. Its origin has two possibilities. One is the Latin deus 'god' (Kiss 1978: 178). The other is the Sumerian diš 'one', pronounced as ištên [ishten] by the Akkadians (Labat 1948: No. 480). Considering that the Hungarian word for 'god' is isten [ishten], the Sumerian diš may mean 'the only one', a reference to god. The Church is called egyház in Hungary, compounded from egy 'one' and ház 'house'. Both the Latin and Sumerian etymologies point in the same direction. See Istenes and Dicsházi for additional information. Old Hungarian spelling of Dés: 1184 Des; 1211 Deus; 1214 Dees. DESZKAS Deszkás [deska:sh]. 'A maker or seller of boards and planks'. The name is comprised of deszka 'board, plank' and the adjectival suffix -ás to indicate occupation. The Hungarian deszka is derived either from the Latin discus or the Greek diskos, an internationally shared word which originally meant a 'round plate' in both languages. Similar derivations exist in most European languages. Old Hungarian spelling: 1469 Dezkas; 1636 deszkás.



29
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
DICSHAZI Dicsházi [dichha:zi]. ‘From the house of god’. The initial element dics is an archaic root-word in the Hungarian language. All of the meanings derived from it pertain to 'glory, greatness, fame and respect'. For example dicsfény, comprised of dics and fény means 'halo', the radiating light around the head of saints in paintings. The second element ház 'house' is followed by the derivational suffix -i. The fact that most words derived from dics are of religious nature provides a compelling reason to believe that the Hungarian dics was associated with god and holiness in antiquity. Its phonetic value is almost the same as the Sumerian diš [dish] which means 'one' but also means 'continuity' (Labat 1948: No. 480). It was also the sign of the god Anu. If the cuneiform sing is interpreted as the only one that continues and never cease to exist, then we are in the realm of an omnipresent divinity. The cuneiform sign of diš was pronounced ištên [ishten] by the Semitic Akkadians whose language supplanted Sumerian in Mesopotamia and contained numerous Sumerian loan-words. The Akkadian ištên meant 'one' but a phonetically identical word isten [ishten] means 'god' in the Hungarian language. Consequently the interrelation between dics, diš, isten and ištên is axiomatic. This would explain why Hungarians call the Church egyház 'house of one'. It also suggests that the ancestors of Hungarians believed in one god, which would explain why the Unitarian Church has its origin in Transylvania. Transylvania, called Erdély by Hungarians, was integral part of historical Hungary until 1920 when it was confined to Romania. Based on the foregoing information, a conclusion may be drawn that dicsház and egyház mean the same thing: house of god. DIOS Diós [dio:sh]. The name is a composite of dió 'walnut' and the adjectival suffix -ós. It denotes a grower or seller of walnuts. The origin of dió is obscure; probably it is a genuine Magyar word. But according to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language it is a loan-word from the Old Turkic languages, cf. Uighur yangaq (Benkő 1967: 641, vol. 1). This appears to be a rather desperate attempt to provide a solution, but nothing else can be offered to replace it, unless it is accepted as a unique Magyar word. Old Hungarian spelling: 1319 Gyous-; 1338 Gyos-; 1353 Gyas-; 1416 Dyos-; 1423 Dyas; 1430 Dyos; 1602 Diós. DOBA Doba [doba]. The archaic form of the present-day Dobos 'drummer' that retained the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a. DOBOS Dobos [dobosh]. 'Drummer'. The name is derived from dob 'drum' to which the adjectival suffix -os is attached to indicate an occupation. It may also denote a maker or seller of drums. In rural Hungary the town crier's announcement was preceded by a prolonged drumbeat to attract the citizens for the communiqué. In some regions of historical Hungary the town crier was called kisdobos 'one who carries a small drum'. In orchestras, in the military, or anywhere else a drummer was called dobos. The word dob may be a relic from the distant past, judging from the presence of ub3 'drum' (Deimel 1939: No. 256) in the Sumerian language which may be the aphetic form of dub > dob. Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 dobos; 1221 Dubus; 1269 dobus; 1308 Dobus.



30
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
DOLGOS Dolgos [dolgosh]. 'Hard working'. The name is derived from dolog 'work, chore' to which the adjectival suffix -os is added to denote character. The Hungarian dolog may be derived from the Sumerian dù 'to create, to build' (Labat 1948: No. 230). Old Hungarian spelling: 1512 Dolgos. DOLMANY Dolmány [dolma:nj]. Derived from the Turkish dolama 'dolman' (Akdikmen 1986: 107), a kind of wide jacket having narrow sleeves. As a garment of the old Turkish military it found its way into many European languages including Hungarian. Old Hungarian spelling: 1504 Dolomaan; 1511 dolman; 1520 dolama; 1530 Dalman. DOMA Doma [doma]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It is probably derived from the Sumerian dumu 'child, descendant' (Labat 1948: No. 144). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Dume. DOMBAI Dombai [dombai]. Also spelled Dombay. The name is comprised of domb 'hill', the archaic adjectival suffix -a and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). It denotes the descendant of a dweller on or by the hill. See Dombi for etymology. DOMBI Dombi [dombi]. Also spelled Domby. Comprised of domb 'hill' and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) the name indicates the descendant of a 'dweller on or by a hill'. The word domb is most likely derived from the Sumerian du6 'hill' (Labat 1948: No. 459). DONATH Dónáth [do:na:t]. Derived from the Latin donatio 'donation, gift', a child whose birth was greatly appreciated by the parents may have received the name. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Donat; 1455 Donath-; 1602 Donát. DONGO Dongó [dongo:]. 'Hummer'. One who talks or sings in a low voice may have been named so. The name incorporates the word dong 'buzz, hum' and the participle -ó that results in an action noun. The word dong may be derived from the Sumerian du11 'to speak, to talk', or du12 'to make music' (Labat 1948: No. 14, 574). The following Hungarian words may also be derived from the same Sumerian words: duma 'patter'; dudol 'hum'; duda 'bagpipe, horn'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1194 Dungou. DOROGI Dorogi [dorogi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates 'one who came from Dorog’ a city in Komárom County. The place name is of Sumerian origin: dúr 'dwelling' and ùg 'people' (Gostony 1975: 145). The compound dúrùg denotes a 'populated dwelling place'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1181 Dorogh; 1249 Durug; 1270 Durugd; 1278 Drug; 1287 Dorogk; 1326 Durugh. DUBA Duba [duba]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It appears to be the compound of the Sumerian dub 'tablet' (Labat 1948: No. 138) and the adjectival suffix -a. It may denote an 'archivist', a keeper of clay tablets. Modern Hungarian equivalent would be táblás. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Duba. DUDA Duda [duda]. A probable derivation from the Sumerian du12 'to make music' (Labat 1948: No. 574). The word-final element -a is the Sumerian and old Hungarian adjectival suffix. The second d is a connecting consonant. Duda means 'bagpipe, horn' and probably a maker or player of bagpipes was named so.



31
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 Duda. DUDAS Dudás [duda:sh]. 'Piper'. The adjectival suffix -ás indicates an occupation. See Duda for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1494 dwdas. DUHA Duha [duha]. The name appears to be an unaltered original Sumerian appellation. The element duh corresponds with the Sumerian duh 'abundance' (Labat 1948: No. 167), and the word-final element -a is the Sumerian (and archaic Hungarian) adjectival suffix that indicates condition. As a name Duha means 'rich, wealthy'. The Sumerian cuneiform sign for duh is also pronounced as du8, see below. DUS Dús [du:sh]. The present-day Hungarian adjectival suffix -s is added to the Sumerian du8 'abundance' to denote a rich person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1519 Dws. DUH Düh [dyh]. The Hungarian düh 'hot temper, fury' is almost identical with the Sumerian dih 'excited, feverish' (Labat 1948: No. 138). A temperamental person may have acquired such a name. Old Hungarian spelling: 1251 Duh-; 1456 dÿh-; 1493 dwh-; 1517 gyh-; 1550 deoh-; 1831 düh-. EBER Éber [e:ber]. The name denotes an 'alert, vigilant' person. The word éber was first recorded as ewbrewn 'awake' in 1372 (Benkő 1967: 704, vol. 1). A vigilant person may have been named so. The word éber, ébren is present only in the Hungarian language but may be connected with the Sumerian bar which has 'to open' as one of its meanings (Deimel 1939: No. 49). EBES Ebes [ebesh]. A dweller by the 'bog' or 'swamp' may have been named so. Ebes means 'bog, swamp' in the Hungarian language (B. Lőrinczy 1988: 8). The root eb in the name is not related to eb 'dog'. Ebes was first recorded as a place name in 1067 and as a surname Ebus in 1138. ECSEDY Ecsedy [echedi]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from Ecséd, the name of a village in Heves County, spelled Eched in 1332 (Kiss 1978: 196). The name is derived from öcs 'younger brother' (archaic spelling ecs). The personal name directly derived from ecs was recorded in 1067 as Heche and Ichy (Benkő 1967: 20, vol. 3). Öcs is shared with other Finno-Ugric languages where it denotes both younger brother and sister. The reconstructed proto-word is *ećɜ. ED Ed [ed]. The surname Ed is from the root of édes 'sweet'. See below. EDE Ede [ede]. The word-final element -e is the archaic Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a changed to -e by vowel harmony. The name is the equivalent of the present-day édes 'sweet'. See below. The German source Edward, listed in the Book of Hungarian Forenames (Ladó 1978: 152) is doubtful. The name Ede was first recorded in the 5th century by Greek chronicler Priscos Rhetor as Edekon, the name of a nobleman in the court of Attila, king of the Huns. That name is an obvious compound of the actual name Ede and khan (Hallay 1986: 11) a title used mainly for a subordinate ruler in most pre-thirteen-century Turkic languages (Clauson 1972: 630). EDES Édes [e:desh]. The word édes has three meanings: sweet, pleasant and dear. It is a



32
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
composite of the root éd and the adjectival suffix -es. Édes is a variant of ízes 'tasty', derived from íz 'taste', an Uralic word meaning 'odor, smell'. The reconstructed Uralic proto-word is *ipse (Benkő 1967: 250, vol. 2). The name may have been bestowed on a person for having pleasant manners. Old Hungarian spellings: 1138 Edus; 1300 ezes; 1805 ides. EGERI Egeri [egeri]. Also spelled Egery, Egri, Egry. The derivational suffix -i (archaic y) signifies a person from Eger, a city in Heves County. Most Hungarian publications list éger 'alder tree' as source of the toponym Eger (Kiss 1978: 197). The city is located by the Eger river, listed as Egur in documents as early as 1009 (Györffy 1987: 80, vol. 3). The bishopric, named after St. John, already existed at that time in the same place where the city is located now. This raises the possibility of Egur being a compound of the Sumerian e 'church' (Labat 1948: 324) and kur 'mountain' (Labat 1948: 364), suggesting that a temple on high ground near the river gave impetus to the name. EGETO Égető [e:getø:]. 'Burner'. The name contains the root ég 'to burn', the causative suffix -et and the participle -ő. The name may be the archaic equivalent of the presentday fűtő 'stoker'. Old Hungarian spelling was Egueteu in 1211. According to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language ég is probably of Finno-Ugric origin. EGYED Egyed [edjed]. Derived from egy 'one', it may have been given to an only child. Old Hungarian spellings in 1138 were Egud, Egyd, Eguid, Egyig, Egig, Egiud, Egyud, Aegyd, Hegud. EGYENES Egyenes [edjenesh]. The name depicts a 'straight, forward' individual. It includes egyen ‘straight’ and the adjectival suffix -es. Egyen is probably a variant of the Sumerian edin 'plain, flat, steppe' (Labat 1948: No. 168), the gy being the palatal counterpart of the alveolar d. The adjectival suffix -es indicates character and condition. Old Hungarian spelling was igyenes in 1372. EKES Ékes [e:kesh]. 'Ornate, bejeweled'. The name may be a derivation from ék 'wedge' to which the adjectival suffix -es is added to indicate condition. The only way this line of reasoning can be sustained is if we assume that jewels and ornaments were shaped like wedges in early Hungarian society. A person or an object decorated with wedge shaped elements would then be called ékes. Old Hungarian spellings: 1213 Ecus; 1372 ekes; 1490 ykes. ELEK Elek [elek]. This name is a relic from the distant past. It was recorded in the form of Elac by Jordanes, a sixth century Gothic (Alan) historian as the name of one of Attila's sons. Attila was king of the Huns in the fifth century. Hungarian records spell it Ellák. Both the Hun Elac, Ellák and the present-day Hungarian Elek show relevance to the Turkish ilk 'first'. Probably Attila's first born son was named so and the tradition continued in Hungarian naming customs. ELEMER Elemér [eleme:r]. A probable derivation from the Turkish il 'province' and mir 'chief'. ELOD Előd [elø:d]. The name signifies a 'predecessor, ancestor' in the Hungarian language. Recorded in 1150 as Eleud. It may also signify a military commander who leads his troops into battle.



33
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
ELES Éles [e:lesh]. 'Sharp'. A 'keen, smart' person may have been named so. It is comprised of the word él 'edge' and the adjectival suffix -es. The word él may be of Ugric origin. The reconstructed proto-word is listed as *elɜ (Benkő 1967: 737, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spellings: 1138 Elus; 1585 éles. ELLEN Ellen [elen]. Appears to be a tag-name denoting a disagreeable person. The word ellen means 'against, in opposition to' in the Hungarian language. The word was recorded as ellen in 1372. ELO Élő [e:lø:]. 'Living'. The word él 'live' is derived from the Uralic languages in which one of the meanings is 'to recuperate' in addition to 'live, living, existing' and 'reside'. Consequently the Hungarian élő, which is a composite of él and the participle -ő, refers to a person who had recovered from a serious illness. The reconstructed Uralic proto-form is *elä (Benkő 1967: 737, vol. 1). EMBER Ember [ember]. 'Man, human being'. A name given for no obvious reason. The two possible constituents of the name are em, as in emse and eme 'female, woman' and ber 'male'. Old Hungarian spelling was ember in 1195. Another possible etymology would be the Sumerian en-bar 'man' (Gostony 1975: 29). EMESE Emese [emeshe]. A probable derivation from the Sumerian geme-še 'femaleseed'. Another phonetic value of the cuneiform sign for geme is sila which is phonetically analogous with the Hungarian szülő 'one who gives birth' (Labat 1948: No. 59, 60). Also compare with Sumerian emé 'guardian of the infant' (Labat 1948: No. 315). Similar words are in the Uralic languages, cf. Finn emä 'female, mother', Estonian ema 'mother'. The reconstructed Uralic proto-word may be *emä (Benkő 1967: 764, vol.1). The Mongolian eme ‘female’ should also be considered as cognate (Golden 2000: 204, 259). The present-day Hungarian perception of Emese is 'anyácska' that translates as the endearing form of anya 'mother' (Ladó 1968: 44). Old Hungarian spellings: 1150 Emesu; 1211 Emse. ENEH Enéh [ene:]. The name is believed to be the archaic spelling of the present-day ünő 'heifer', a totemistic animal of ancient Hungarians, recorded by Simon Kézai, chronicler of king László (1262-1290). Ine, Enee, Enech, Eneth are the various other forms in old Hungarian chronicles. The mythical Eneh was the mother of Magor, the legendary progenitor of Magyars. The word ünő is of Old Turkic origin, cf. Chagatai and Kipchak inäk, Osmanli inek, Yakut inah, all of which mean 'cow' (Benkő 1967: 1053, vol. 3). ENEKES Énekes [e:nekesh]. 'Singer'. It is a hybrid name incorporating the Sumerian é 'to speak' (Labat 1948: No. 308), the element nok, affected by vowel harmony, which indicates occupation and the adjectival suffix -es. Similar words are in the Finno-Ugric languages, cf. Finn ääni 'sound, voice'. The Finno-Ugric proto-word may have been *äne. Old Hungarian spellings: 1277 Enekus; 1560 enekes. ENGI Engi [engi]. The name may be derived from the Sumerian en-gi 'lord of the reed' (Labat 1948: No. 85, 99) that is the ‘wet-land’. It has no meaning in present-day Hungarian. The Sumerians called their country ki-en-gi 'country of the lord of the reeds' (Roux 1976: 82). Another interpretation of ki-en-gi is ‘land dominated by reeds’, e.g. overgrown by reeds. EPER Eper [eper]. The name may have been bestowed on a berry farmer. The Hungarian



34
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
eper 'berry' is akin to other Ugric words of the same kind, cf. Vogul äprä 'strawberry' (Benkő 1967: 774, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spellings: 1075 Haper-; 1405 Eper-; 1595 epergy. EPERJESSY Eperjessy [eperjeshi]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from Eperjes, the name of a city in Sáros County of historical Hungary. The city was confined to Slovakia on June 4, 1920 by the Trianon Peace Treaty and renamed Prešov. The place name Eperjes incorporates the word eper 'berry', the connecting consonant j and the adjectival suffix -es. The city may take its name from the wild berries which grew abundantly in the area. The reduplication of s (ss) marks a stressed consonant. See Eper for etymology. Old Hungarian spellings: 1075 Haperies; 1230 Apries; 1233 Epuryes; 1252 Epyries; 1793 eperjesi. EPLENYI Eplényi [eple:nji]. The derivational suffix -i denotes a person from Eplény, the name of a settlement which amalgamated with Olaszfalu, in Veszprém County. The word eplény is of Slavonic origin, cf. oplin, oplen 'crosstie on a sleigh' (Benkő 1967: 777, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spellings: 1395 eplen; 1475 epleen; 1792 Eplény. EPRES Epres [epresh]. 'Grower or seller of berries'. See Eper for more information. EPP Epp [ep]. Archaic form of ép 'intact, healthy'. The secondary meaning épület 'building' may be associated with the Sumerian e2 'house' (Labat 1948: No. 324). However, the name Epp most likely denotes a healthy person. Old Hungarian spelling was epp- in 1372. ERDI Érdi [e:rdi]. Also spelled Érdy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Érd, the name of a place in Pest County. Érd is comprised of ér 'brook' and the suffix -d which denotes a place. The name suggests a settlement by a brook. Old Hungarian spelling was Erdu in 1240. ERDEI Erdei [erdei]. The name denotes a dweller in or by the erdő 'forest'. The derivational suffix -i indicates geographical origin. The word erdő may be a derivation from the verb ered 'originate', which is derived from the Uralic *šärɜ- 'stretch, expand, enlarge', according to the HistoricalEtymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language. With all of its attraction, however, the Uralic etymology may not be correct. The concepts of 'originate' and 'expend' do not have any relationship with a wooded area like a forest. But there is yet another possibility, one which is phonetically and semantically more convincing. The original Hungarian word for forest, which became obsolete a long time ago, may have been derived from fa 'wood, tree', like the Turkish orman which means both 'wood' and 'forest'. Other examples are the French bois 'wood' and les bois 'forest and the English wood from which woods 'forest' is derived. The suggested interpretation for the earliest Hungarian word for forest is based on deductive reasoning that the element fajd in the Hungarian name fajdkakas 'woodcock, grouse' cannot be anything else but an early rendering of 'woods, forest'. The word fajd contains the element fa 'tree, wood'. The rest of the word is difficult to etymologize. A regional dialect is fájityúk 'woodhen' (B. Lőrinczy 1988: 254, vol. 2) provides a much closer evidence for fáji being 'of the woods'. The fa segment certainly stands for 'forest',



35
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
the j is a connecting consonant and the -i is the derivational suffix. Similar words that exist in the Celtic languages would substantiate the rational in this argument, cf. old Irish fid 'tree, wood' and Welsh ffawydden 'beech trees' (Thorne 1994: 85). See Erdély for more information. However, erdő means 'forest' in present-day Hungarian, therefore the meaning of Erdei is interpreted accordingly, but it may mean Erdélyi as reasoned below. Old Hungarian spellings: 1138 Erdeidi; 1320 Erdey; 1490 erdei. ERDELY Erdély [erde:j]. This surname may have been inspired by the toponym Erdély, the Hungarian name of Transylvania, a region of historical Hungary until June 4, 1920 when the Treaty of Trianon confined it to Romania. A difficult name to interpret and subject of debate among Hungarian onomasticians. Linguists associate it with the word erdő 'forest, woodland' which, according to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, is derived from ered 'originate' and is synonymous with sarjad 'sprout, shoot-up' (Benkő 1967: 782, vol. 1). The association of these two words with grown trees of a forest seems to cause the problem. There is no doubt erdő means 'forest' in present-day Hungarian, but it is doubtful Erdély is derived from it. It is also doubtful that a forest was called erdő in ancient Hungarian because it does not contain the word fa ‘wood’. For example fás ‘timbered, covered with trees’ would be more appropriate. Furthermore, the name fa was equally used for ‘wood’ and ‘forest’ in 1231 and faas meant ‘covered by trees’ in 1519 (Benkő 1967: 822, vol. 1). The old school of Onomastics maintains that the name Erdeuelu and Erdewelu, as recorded in Latin texts, was the old Hungarian equivalent of the Latin Transylvania 'land beyond the forest'. The entire world, including Hungary, is full of forests and a name like that can be applied to any area, rendering such a name ineffective. The last element elu was associated with the Ugric *älɜ 'beyond' (Benkő 1967: 757, vol. 1), notwithstanding a close resemblance to the Hungarian elő 'before'. The primary meaning of the Latin transis 'across, through'; 'being beyond' is secondary. Consequently the Latin trans-sylvania may be interpreted as 'extending across the forest', i.e. the entire forested area. It is now necessary to make an important assumption, which is that Erdély was originally called Eredély and it denoted the place of origin of the Magyar people. This would also mean that the Magyar speaking Székelys are the indigenous population of Transylvania. As a toponym Erdély marks the original homeland of the Magyars. The e in the middle of the name was omitted by syncopation, a grammatical process that involves the deletion of a vowel within a word, cf. Hungarian cukor 'sugar' > cukros 'sugary, sweet' (instead of cukoros). The word-final element -ély is a suffix that forms nouns from verbs, cf. enged 'to permit' > engedély 'permission, license'. Compare with Erdei. ERDELYI Erdélyi [erde:ji]. The derivational suffix -i denotes a person from Erdély 'Transylvania'. See Erdely for etymology. ERDESZ Erdész [erde:s]. 'Forest-ranger', comprised of erdő 'forest' and the formative suffix -ész. The word erdész is a recent development; it did not surface in literature until 1807. The old Hungarian name for forest-ranger was either erdő-óvó 'forest-protector' spelled erdewow in 1300, or erdő-örző 'forest-guard' spelled erdewewryzew in 1489 (Szamota 1902-1906: 195, 196).



36
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
ERDOS Erdős [erdø:sh]. Another old name for erdész 'forest-ranger'. The name contains erdő 'forest' and the adjectival suffix -ős which denotes an occupation. Old Hungarian spelling was Erdeus in 1202. ERDOSI Erdősi [erdø:shi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant os Erdős 'forest-ranger'. See above for more explanation. EREKY Ereky [ereki]. This name is difficult to etymologize unless it is derived from the Sumerian Erech, the name of a city in ancient Mesopotamia. The archaic derivational suffix -y denotes a person from Erek, a probable variant of Erech. The phonetic values of both names are similar to the Hungarian örök 'eternal' and the Turkic ürük 'long-lasting' (Benkő 1967: 35, vol. 3). Probably Erech was the 'eternal city' for the Sumerians, the same as Rome is for Christians. ERES Eres [eresh]. The adjectival suffix -es is appended to ér 'vein' to suggest a person with bulging veins. The word ér is present in other Finno-Ugric languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *särɜ (Benkő 1967: 779, vol.1). Old Hungarian spelling was Eres in 1522. Another possible source of origin is the Hungarian erős 'strong', which is a derivation from the Sumerian er3, ir3 'strong' (Deimel 1939: No. 28). Similar words are in the Turkic languages, cf. Uighur ärk 'strong' (Benkő 1967: 790, vol. 1). It may be of importance that Ereš [eresh] was also the name of a Sumerian deity. ERI Éri [e:ri]. Also spelled Éry. The name appears to be a relic from the distant past. It is identical with the Sumerian èri 'servant, slave' (Labat 1948: No. 50). ERODY Erődy [erø:di]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a dweller in or by the erőd 'fortress', which is derived from erő 'strength'. See Eres for more detail. EROS Erős [erø:sh]. 'Strong'. See Eres above for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling was Ereus in 1211. ERSEK Érsek [e:rshek]. 'Archbishop'. Also used as an epithet for a pompous, know-it-all person. It is derived from the initial element of the Greek arhie(piskop) 'chief(bishop)’. Old Hungarian spelling was Ersek in 1211. ERSZENY Erszény [erse:nj]. The name of an unidentified Turkic people, recorded as Tokuz Ersen 'Nine Ersen' on a monument erected around AD 720 on the bank of the Tola creek, a tributary of the Orkhon River in Mongolia (Györffy 1975: 59, 275). There is no correlation between the name and the Hungarian erszény 'purse'. ESSO Esső [esø:]. The participle -ő is suffixed to the element es ‘to fall’ to form an adjective from a verb. The reduplicated s (ss) marks a stressed consonant. The Hungarian eső 'rain' is a probable derivation from the Uralic *ećɜ 'let, abandon, drop' (Benkő 1967:795, vol.1). The Sumerian eš10 'water' (Labat 1948: No. 579) may also be considered. Associating it with water instead of falling water is a possibility. The regular Sumerian word for water is a (Labat 1948: No. 578). Old Hungarian spelling was Esu in 1138. ESTEY Estey [eshtei]. Also spelled Estey. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a relationship with este 'evening'. Probably one born in the evening was named so. The word este is short for napeste 'sundown' derived from esik 'fall, drop'. It may originate in the Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *ećɜ. The old Hungarian spelling was estue in 1372. See Esso for more detail.



37
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
ESZENYI Eszényi [ese:nji]. The derivational suffix -i denotes a person from Eszény, a town in historical Hungary's Szabolcs County. It is in an area confined to the Ukraine after World War II and was renamed Javorovo. Eszényi is one of those obscure names that resist all attempts to resolve its etymology. Slavonic origin can be ruled out with certainty for the name was Slavonicized after annexation. Old Hungarian spelling of the place name was Ezen in 1274. ESZES Eszes [esesh]. The adjectival suffix -es is attached to ész 'reason, sense' to denote a smart person. Similar words are in the Turkic languages, cf. Kirghiz äs, Baskir is 'reason, sense' (Benkő 1967: 798, vol. 1). However, the ultimate origin of the word may be the Sumerian uš4 'reason, sense' (Labat 1948: No. 534). Also compare with Osmanli us 'intelligence, state of mind' (Akdikmen 1986: 358). Old Hungarian spelling: 1389 Ezus; 1391 Ezzes; 1466 Ezes. ETE Ete [ete]. An ancient Hungarian name, first recorded around the turn of the First Millennium AD. Ete son of chieftain Ond is credited with the construction of a fortress at the city of Csongrád (Györffy 1987: 882, vol. 1). The name is of Turkic origin. Old Hungarian spelling was Ete in 1138. EVADY Évady [e:vadi]. A name of uncertain derivation. Évad means 'season' in the Hungarian language and its origin cannot be traced to any other tongue. Old Hungarian spelling: 1519 ewad-, evad, évad, ivad. EVEZOS Evezős [evezø:sh]. 'Rower'. Comprised of the root evez 'to row', the participle ő (evező also means 'paddle') and the adjectival suffix -s. The name denotes an occupation. A possible derivation from the Finno-Ugric *evä 'paddle, fin' (Benkő 1967: 814, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling: 1395 ewezo; 1604 evozos. EZELLO Ézellő [e:zellø:]. Archaic spelling of ízelő 'food-taster'. The name is comprised of íz 'taste', the third person singular -el and the participle -ő. The name denotes an occupation, a food-taster in a nobleman's court. Variants of íz are present in the Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *ipse 'smell, taste' (Benkő 1967: 250, vol. 2). FABRI Fábri [fa:bri]. Also spelled Fábry. Derived from the Latin faber 'craftsman' to which the Hungarian patronymic suffix -i (archaic -y) is attached to denote a descendant of a craftsman. The middle e was omitted by syncopation. The Hungarian verb fabrikál 'piece something together, repair' is derived from the same root. Old Hungarian spelling was Faber in 1138. FAGYAS Fagyas [fadjash]. An old form of the present-day faggyús 'suet, mutton-fat merchant, candle maker'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1374 fagyas-; 1413 fagyas. FAHIDY Fahidy [fahidi]. 'Dweller by the wooden bridge', comprised of fa 'wood', híd 'bridge' and the archaic derivational suffix -y. FAKLYAS Fáklyás [fa:kja:sh]. 'Torch bearer', derived from fákja 'torch' plus the adjectival suffix -ás that indicates possession and provision in this case. The Hungarian fákja is a derivative of the Latin facula 'torch'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1416 facl'a-; 1532 fakla-; 1585 fáklya. FALU Falu [falu]. The word falu 'village' has no probable reason to form a surname. Falu shows phonetic and semantic relation with âlu 'village' (Labat 1948: No. 43) the



38
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Akkadian equivalent of the Sumerian uru (Labat 1948: No. 38). It may be a Sumerian loan word in the Akkadian language. Also compare the Sumerian gišbulug and Akkadian palukku 'reed settlement' (Labat 1948: No. 96). Palukku's phonetic value is similar to the Hungarian palánk 'boardfence' erected for protecting a settlement. Similar words exist in the Ugric and Finno-Ugric languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *palyɜ (Benkő 1967: 836, vol. 1). Cf. English palisade ‘stake-fence’, derived from the Latin palus ‘stake’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1075 falu; 1176 falwa; 1214 foloud; 1230 folu; 1378 falow. FALUSI Falusi [falushi]. Also spelled Falussy. The name is comprised of falu 'village', the adjectival suffix -s and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). The name indicates a 'villager'. See Falu for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1410 falusy; 1590 Falusi. FALVY Falvy [falvi]. A different form of Falusi 'villager'. FANCSALI Fancsali [fanchali]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Fancsal, a locality in Borsod-Abaúj-Zemplén County. The name's etymology is obscure; its origin from fancsal 'gloomy look, wry smile' may be possible. Old Hungarian spelling: 1262 Fonchal; 1810 Fandsali. FARADY Farády [fara:di]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from Farád, a locality in Győr-Sopron County. The place name Farád is comprised of far 'backside, behind, rump' and the grammatical designator -ád which denotes a place. It presumably describes an agricultural area in a hindmost location. The word far is present in the Uralic languages, for example Udmurt (Votyak) ber 'backside, rump', Finnish perä 'backside' (Benkő 1967: 841, vol. 1), but the ultimate source may be the Sumerian bar 'the back of an anthropoid ape' (Deimel 1939: 49). The concept and reference to an 'anthropoid ape' by Deimel is unclear. FARAGO Faragó [farago:]. 'Carver'. Derived from farag 'to carve' to which the participle -ó is suffixed to denote an occupation. The root element far is present in other Ugric languages, the reconstructed primary form is *parɜ (Benkő 1967: 842, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling was Farago in 1357. FARKAS Farkas [farkash]. 'Wolf'. The designation farkas literally means 'one who has a tail'. It is derived from farok 'tail' which in turn is derived from far 'behind, rump'. The adjectival suffix -as is added to indicate condition. The word originates from the Uralic *purɜ 'turn around, background' according to the Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language. But having a tail is not a suitable name because most animals have tails, it does not distinguish wolfs from other beasts. Wolfs move around in packs. Therefore an alternate origin may be the Hungarian falkás 'a pack of animals' derived from falka 'pack, flock'. Considering that the wolf is also called ordas in the Hungarian language and the Turkish ordu means 'crowd, horde', the derivation from falka seems more relevant. Old Hungarian spellings in 1138 were Farkas, Farcas, Forcos, Forcus, Forcas. FATRAY Fátray [fa:trai]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from the Fátra Mountain Range in the Highlands of historical Hungary. This area was confined to



39
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Slovakia by the Trianon Peace Treaty on June 4, 1920. Fátra is derived from the German Vater 'father'. Saxon immigrants may have given the name in the 12th Century (Kiss 1978: 443). Old Hungarian spelling was fatra in 1284. FAZEK Fazék [faze:k]. The Hungarian fazék 'pot' may be an early designation for the present-day fazekas 'potter'. Fazék seems to be a derivation from the Hungarian főz 'to cook' to which the frequentative suffix -ik is affixed to indicate continuous action. The original főzik may have been distorted to fazék. The Finno-Ugric origin from *pata 'pot' may also be considered (Benkő 1967: 854, vol. 1).
Old Hungarian spellings: 1296 Fazek; 1395 fazek; 1816 fëzëk-. FAZEKAS Fazekas [fazekash]. The name Fazekas 'potter' is comprised of the word fazék 'pot' and the adjectival suffix -as that denotes an occupation. See Fazek for more information. Old Hungarian spellings: 1348 Fazekas; 1370 Fazakas; 1410 Fazokas. FECSER Fecser [fecher]. An obsolete variant of fecsegő 'babling, chatty'. FECSKE Fecske [fechke]. 'Swallow'. A name of onomatopoeic origin, the sound of a swallow articulated by a human. A probable derivation from fecseg, fecsgő 'babbling, chatty' which resembles the chirping sound of European swallows. Old Hungarian spellings: 1333 Fecske-; 1372 fecske-; 1377 Fychke; 1395 fechke; 1864 fecske. FEGYVERES Fegyveres [fedjveresh]. The name denotes a 'weapon dealer' or 'an armed person'. It is comprised of fegyver 'weapon' and the adjectival suffix -es that indicates an occupation or condition. The word fegyver may be a compound of fegy 'rigor, regimen' and ver 'strike, hit'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1211 Feguueres; 1372 fegyueres. FEGYVERNEKY Fegyverneky [fedjverneki]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a descendant of fegyvernek, a variant of fegyvernök 'armor-bearer'. See Fegyveres for more explanation. Old Hungarian spelling was Fegyuernuch in 1213. FEHER Fehér [fehe:r]. 'White'. A name probably given to a person for having white hair or light complexion. Old Hungarian spellings: 1055 feher; 1075 feir-; 1193 Faer-; 1219 Feier. FEHERVARY Fehérváry [fehe:rva:ri]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a dweller in or by the fehér vár 'white fort'. Old Hungarian spelling was feheruuar- in 1055. FEJA Féja [fe:ja]. The old form of the present-day fejős, fejő 'a milker'. The name incorporates fej 'to milk' and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a to denote an occupation. Old Hungarian spelling was feÿe in 1395. FEJER Fejér [feje:r]. May be a variant of Fehér 'white'. See Feher for more detail. An alternative etymology would link fejér with fej ‘head’ and er Finno-Ugric for ‘male’, cf. Turkish er ‘male, man’, denoting a ‘chieftain, superior’. The title fejedelem is also derived from fej ‘head’. See Fejes for more explanation. FEJES Fejes [fejesh]. The epithet fejes is used for denoting a stubborn, headstrong



40
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
person. In some regions of Hungary a supervisor is also called fejes. The name incorporates the word fej 'head' and the adjectival suffix -es to indicate character and status. Variants of fej are present in other Finno-Ugric languages, cf. Finnish pää 'head', but the ultimate origin of the word may be the Sumerian pa 'head, chief' (Deimel 1939: No. 169). The Hungarian fő is a variant of fej and főnök 'head, chief' is derived from it. Old Hungarian spellings: 1002 few; 1055 feu; 1158 fey; 1268 -fw; 1372 -fe; 1378 fő; 1469 fees. FEJOS Fejős [fejø:sh]. 'A milker'. See Feja for more explanation. Old Hungarian spelling was feyws in 1551. FEJSZES Fejszés [fejse:sh]. 'Maker of axes’ or 'one equipped with an axe'. The name contains fejsze 'axe' and the adjectival suffix -es to indicate occupation. Old Hungarian spelling of fejsze: 1395 feÿsse; 1519 feyzy; 1863 féjszi; 1894 fejszë. FEJTO Fejtő [fejtø:]. 'Hewer, cutter' in a mine or quarry. The name is comprised of fejt 'strip, hack' and the participle -ő denoting an occupation. Old Hungarian spelling of fejt: 1372 feyt-. FEKETE Fekete [fekete]. 'Black'. The name may have been bestowed on a black-haired person. It is also possible that the name marked a member of the Black Hungarians. Ancient chronicles mention the Black and White Hungarians without reference to the source of their names. Since the Huns and Hungarians, but not the indigenous Magyars, were of Turkic origin, the two colors may denote Northern (Black) and Southern (White) Hungarians. According to Turkish folklore Black (dark) represents north and White (light) symbolizes south. Thus, they call the Black Sea Karadeniz because it is north of Turkey and the Aegean Sea Akdeniz (White Sea) for its southern location. The Ugric origin of fekete, suggested by the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, is not convincing. Cf. Ostyak (Khanty) puti 'black' (Benkő 1967: 867, vol. 1).
Old Hungarian spellings were Fekete, Feketeu, Feketeh, Fequete in 1138. FEL Fél [fe:l]. A timid, easily frightened person may have been named so. The Hungarian fél 'fear, fright' is cognate with other Uralic words of the same category. The reconstructed Uralic proto-word is *pele 'fear' (Benkő 1967: 870, vol 1). Old Hungarian spellings: 1135 fele; 1138 Feles; 1490 fyl. FELLEGI Fellegi [felegi]. A fictional name suggesting the bearer of the name is from the clouds. The Hungarian language has two words for cloud: felleg and felhő. Both words describe the physical activity required for the formation of clouds. The initial element fel 'up' describes the direction of the lég 'air' and hő 'heat' as they ascend to the height where the condensation into a cloud takes place. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language assigns both felhő and felleg to Finno-Ugric origin. The reconstructed proto-word is *pilβe. Cf. Finnish pilvi 'cloud', Udmurt piv 'cloud'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1493 felhő, felleg-, 1572 fellÿegh. FENYES Fényes [fe:njesh]. 'Shining, bright'. The name may indicate a 'rich, well-to-do' person. Only rich people could afford to burn more than one candle in their homes in olden times. Their houses were brightly lit at night. Those people may have acquired the



41
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
name Fényes 'bright'. Fényes is derived from fény 'light' to which the adjectival suffix -es is appended to denote condition and quality. Old Hungarian spellings: 1075 fen-; 1093 Fen; 1222 Fenies; 1237 Fenyes. FENYI Fényi [fe:nji]. Descendent of Fény 'light' as indicated by the derivational suffix -i. See Fenyes above for more information. FERGE Ferge [ferge]. A variant of fürge 'agile, quick'. It was spelled Fergued in 1221 and Fürge in 1693. FESUS Fésűs [fe:shy:sh]. An occupational name denoting a 'comb-maker' or 'seller of combs'. The adjectival suffix -s is attached to fésű 'comb' to indicate an occupation. The word fésű is comprised of the root fes (as in feslik) 'separate, unfold' and the participle -ű. Fes(-lik) can be traced to the Ugric or Finno-Ugric proto-word *päčɜ 'part, split' (Benkő 1967: 896-897, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spellings: 1400 Feuses; 1463 Fesus. FESZI Fészi [fe:si]. May be a derivation from fészek ‘nest, home’, or feszeng ‘fidget, be restless’. The derivational suffix -i would indicate a person from the fészek, i.e. from home. FICZERE Ficzere [fitsere]. ‘Fancy’. The name of a person who likes to be decked out with adornments. It is difficult to explain its exact meaning in the English language. Old Hungarian spelling was ficzere in 1584. FILE File [file]. A variant of Füle, the archaic form of the present-day Füles 'one having big ears'. The name contains the word fil (>fül) 'ear' and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -e (a variant of -a, modified by vowel harmony). The Hungarian fil, fül is cognate with the Vogul (Mansi) pil 'ear', Udmurt (Votyak) pel 'ear' and other similar words in the Uralic languages (Benkő 1967: 933, vol. 1). However, the ultimate source may be the Sumerian pi 'ear' (Deimel 1939: No. 170). FINTA Finta [finta]. 'Pug-nosed'. Cognate with fintor 'grimace'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1116 Phinta; 1138 Fintu, Fita. FINTOR Fintor [fintor]. The name indicates a person in the habit of making faces. Cognate with Finta. FITOS Fitos [fitosh]. Also spelled Fitus, 'pug-nosed'. Cognate with Finta. FODOR Fodor [fodor]. A curly-haired individual may have been named so. Fodor means 'curl, frill' in the Hungarian language. Old Hungarian spellings: 1246 Fudur; 1287 Fodor. FOGA Foga [foga]. The name may have been given to a 'jailer', for it contains the verb fog 'to hold' and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a. This suffix was attached to verbs and nouns in antiquity to form adjectives and participles. The name appears to be the archaic form of the present-day Fogó. Though, variants of the Hungarian fog can be found in some of the Uralic languages, the most probable source is the Sumerian pag 'imprison' (Labat 1948: No. 78). The Hungarian fogda 'prison' is also derived from fog. Old Hungarian spelling was Fogou in 1138. FOLDE Földe [følde]. The archaic form of the present-day Földes 'landowner'. It is comprised of föld 'earth, land, top soil' and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -e (a variant of -a, modified by vowel harmony).



42
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spellings: 1130 feld-; 1268 folde; 1302 -fuld; 1456 fed-; 1519 feeld. FOLDES Földes [føldesh]. 'Landowner'. See explanation above. It was spelled feldes in 1193 and Földes in 1511. FONYO Fonyó [fonjo:]. The folksy variant of fonó 'spinner' (in a spinning mill). The participle -ó, attached to fony [fonj] 'spin (make yarn)’, denotes an occupation. The word fon is shared with other Uralic languages, the reconstructed proto-word is *puna- (Benkő 1967:946, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling was Funou in 1244. FORBATH Forbáth [forba:t]. Derived from the obsolete forbátol 'requite like for like' (eye for an eye), a Forbáth is one who behaves like that. The word-final element -h marks the reduced plosiveness of the preceding t. The name was spelled Forbát in 1831. FORGACS Forgács [forga:ch]. 'Wood shavings'. This word has no probable reason to form a surname. The word forgács may either be derived from farag 'carve' or forog 'turn, revolve'. The word-final element ács means 'carpenter' as an independent word, but its function as a suffix is obscure. About a dozen words carry this suffix and their meanings are diverse, cf. korbács 'whip', szakács 'cook'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1336 Furgach; 1337 Forgach; 1339 Fargach; 1792 faragáncs. FORGO Forgó [forgo:]. Derived from forog 'turn, revolve' and the participle -ó, the name may denote a craftsman specialized in making tufts for helmets. Tuft means forgó in the Hungarian language. Old Hungarian spelling was Forgo in 1377. FORRAI Forrai [forai]. Also spelled Forray. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a dweller by a spring well. Forra is the old form of the present-day forrás 'spring well', derived from forr 'boil' (Benkő 1967: 956, vol. 1), a derivative of forog 'whirl swirl, turn', describing the movement of boiling liquid. It is possible that the word forra was used to denote hot springs in antiquity. They are abundant in Hungary. The Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a (present-day -ás) signifies condition. Also compare with forró 'hot'. The Sumerian bùru 'spring well' (Labat 1948: No. 411) has similar phonetic value and identical meaning. FOVENYES Fövényes [føvenjesh]. The adjectival suffix -es is attached to fövény 'sand' to denote an owner or a worker in a sand pit. Fövényes is also an obsolete appellation for a sandy beach. Old Hungarian spellings: 1055 fuegnes; 1193 Fuinnes; 1332 Fuenyes; 1590 föuényes. FOZO Főző [fø:zø:]. 'A cook', comprised of főz 'to cook' and the participle -ó. Similar words exist in the Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *peje 'to boil, to cook' (Benkő 1967: 962, vol. 1). It was spelled Fezew in 1436. FUJTATO Fújtató [fu:jtato:]. An unusual surname, comprised of fúj 'to blow', the causative suffix -tat and the participle -ó. It means 'bellows' and the name may signify an operator of such a contrivance, for example in a traditional black-smith shop. The word fúj ‘blow’ is shared by members of the Uralic language family, cf.



43
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Vogul (Mansi) puw 'to blow' (Benkő 1967: 983, vol. 1). The Uralic words show affiliation with the Sumerian bu 'to blow' (Labat 1948: No. 371), which has similar phonetic form and identical meaning. Old Hungarian spelling was fwthato in 1586. FURO Fúró [fu:ro:]. Comprised of fúr 'to drill, to bore a hole' and the participle -ó. The name Fúró denotes a 'driller'. The reconstructed Uralic form may have been *pura (Benkő 1967: 987, vol. 1), but the Sumerian bùru 'to drill' (Labat 1948: No. 411) should be considered as the ultimate source. Old Hungarian spelling was furu in 1272. FUSI Füsi [fyshi]. Also spelled Füssy. The name suggests a dweller on a grassy tract of land. It incorporates the root fű 'grass', the adjectival suffix -s and the derivational suffix i (archaic -y). The suggested source of fű is the Sumerian ú 'grass' (Labat 1948: No. 318). The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language suggests Uralic origin, the reconstructed proto-word would be *pimɜ (Benkő 1967: 998, vol. 1). GABAI Gabai [gabai]. Also spelled Gabay. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language, except for the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) which indicates a descendant of Gaba. The word gaba means 'chest, breast, bosom' in the Sumerian language (Labat 1948: No. 167) and is phonetically close to the Hungarian kebel 'breast, bosom'. A barrel-chested person or one having a prominent chest may have received such a name. Old Hungarian spelling was Gaba in 1211. GABANYI Gabányi [gaba:nji]. The name denotes a resident of a place called Gabány or a descendant of a person called Gabány. The name contains the Sumerian root gaba 'chest, breast, bosom', the suffix -ány which forms collective nouns from verbs and nouns and the derivational suffix -i. See Gabai for more information. GABOS Gabos [gabosh]. The name is the present-day form of the Sumerian Gaba that has the adjectival suffix -a replaced by its modern equivalent -os. See Gabai for more information. Old Hungarian spelling was Gabus in 1138. GACS Gács [ga:ch]. Also spelled Gách. A surname denoting ethnic origin. It is also the name of a town in Nógrád County, probably named after its one time inhabitants the Ruthenians who were called Gács in old Hungarian. The Ruthenians embarked on a western migration from their original homeland in Galicia during the Thirteenth Century and most of them settled in Máramaros, Bereg, Ung and Zemplén counties of historical Hungary (Ács 1986: 101). Both the people and their country were called Gács by Hungarians, Halicz by the Poles and Galich by the Russians. The etymology of the name is unresolved. GACSI Gácsi [ga:chi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Gács, a town in Nógrád County. See Gács for etymology. GACSO Gacsó [gacho:]. A variant of gacsos 'club-footed'. Spelled Kacsos in 1260 and gacsos in 1700. GADO Gadó [gado:]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It



44
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
may be a variant of the Sumerian gad, gada 'flax, linen' (Labat 1948: No. 90), bestowed either on a flax grower or a weaver of linens. Another word that may have been derived from the Sumerian gada is the Hungarian gatya 'a pair of white wide linen trousers', the traditional garment of men in rural Hungary. Naming a garment or other objects after the material they were made of is practiced by most linguistic communities. For example the English iron is the name of the material of which the heated appliance is made for smoothing dresses. The Hungarian phonetic system allows the change between the speech sounds d [d], ty [tj] and gy [dj] resulting in gaty and gagy. The word-final element -a in the Sumerian gada represented both the adjective and the participle while the suffix -ó is the participle in the Hungarian language and denotes an occupation. GAGYI Gagyi [gadji]. The derivational suffix -i denotes a person from Gagy, a village in Abaúj County, now called Alsógagy 'Lower Gagy'. The place name is derived from the name of its founder. The surname Gagy is cognate with the Sumerian gada 'flax, linen' and may have preserved the occupation of a forebear. See Gado for etymology. Old Hungarian spellings: 1256 Gogy; 1279 Gayg; 1317 Gog; 1332 Gader, God. GAJDOS Gajdos [gajdosh]. 'Piper'. The adjectival suffix -os is added to gajd 'reed pipe' to denote an occupation. Gajd is an international loan word present in the Ural-Altaic and Indo-European languages cf. Osmanli gayda, Arab gaita, Spanish gaita, Polish gajda, all of them meaning ‘pipe’ (Benkő 1967:1013 vol. 1). The probable source of gajd is the compound Sumerian gi 'reed' (Labat 1948: No. 85) and du12 'to make music' (Labat 1948: No. 85). One of the earliest wind instruments was made of reed, abundant in ancient Mesopotamia (present-day Iraq). Old Hungarian spellings: 1272 Goydus; 1402 Gaydus. GAL Gál [ga:l]. Also spelled Gáll, Gaál. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It may be a relic from the Sumerian language where gal was the title of a 'great man', it also meant 'noble, majestic, high-class' (Labat 1948: No. 343). Old Hungarian spelling was Gal in 1138. GALAMB Galamb [galamb]. 'Dove, pigeon'. A frequent Hungarian surname given to a peaceful, gentle person. The word galamb is an altered form of the Latin columba 'pigeon'. The Slavonic source of golob, golub or holub, as suggested by the HistoricalEtymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, is not convincing. Old Hungarian spellings: 1138 Glomb-, Glamb; 1165 golomb-; 1316 Galomb; 1372 galamb. GALAMBOS Galambos [galambosh]. The adjectival suffix -os is attached to galamb 'pigeon' to denote a pigeon breeder or fancier. See Galamb for more information. GALANTAI Galántai [gala:ntai]. Also spelled Galántay, Galánthay. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Galánta, a town in historical Hungary, confined to Slovakia in 1920 by the Trianon Peace Treaty. The name Galánta is derived from the French galant 'gallant' which is a derivation from the Teutonic geil 'wanton'. The town's name commemorates the name of its founder. The word-final element -a in Galánta is the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix



45
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
denoting character. Originating it from the Czech surname Golata (Kiss 1978: 230) only touches on exactness. Old Hungarian spelling was Galantha in 1237. GALDY Gáldy [ga:ldi]. A resident of Gáld. The name contains the following elements: Sumerian gal, the grammatical designator -d denoting a place, and the archaic derivational suffix -y. See Gal for etymology. GALFI Gálfi [ga:lfi]. Also spelled Gálfy. The patronymic suffix -fi (archaic -fy) signifies a descendant of Gál. See Gal for more information. GALGAI Galgai [galgai]. Also spelled Galgay. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a dweller by the stream Galga. The word galga cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. The only clue to its identity is the word-final element -a that marked both adjectives and participles in Sumerian and ancient Hungarian. The names of many major Hungarian water courses have the Sumero-Hungarian suffix -a and its modern Hungarian equivalent -os and -ó. Cf. Duna, Tisza, Dráva, Száva, Rába, Rábca, Mura, Lajta, Zsitva; Maros, Szamos, Kapos, Kőrös, Temes, Aranyos; Sajó, Csalló, Küküllő. In the case of Galga the suffix -a is preceded by the consonant g, a frequent connecting sound along with -k- in front of the adjective -a. The remaining gal means 'majestic' in the Sumerian language. See Gal for more information. GALI Gáli [ga:li]. A descendant of Gál, indicated by the derivational suffix -i. See Gal for more information. GALLA Galla [gala]. The name may owe its origin to the Sumerian gala 'priest' (Labat 1948: No. 211). GALLAI Gallai [galai]. Also spelled Gallay. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) denotes a descendant of Galla. See above for etymology. GALLO Galló [galo:]. A variant of Galla. GALLYAS Gallyas [gajash]. The name may have been bestowed on the owner or breeder of cattle called gallyas (B. Lőrinczy 1988: 593, vol. 2). They called so for the tip of their long upright horn turning to the side. GAMOS Gamós [gamo:sh]. The name is derived from the Sumerian gam 'to bend, to stoop' (Labat 1948: No. 362) to which the adjectival suffix -ós is appended. One may have been named so because of his posture. GARA Gara [gara]. This name has no meaning in modern Hungarian. It may be derived from the Sumerian gar 'to make, to manufacture' (Deimel 1939: No. 336). Cf. Hungarian gyár ‘factory’, gyárt ‘manufactures’. The adjectival suffix -a denotes a manufacturer or a craftsman. The German gar 'done, quite' is an unlikely source. GARAI Garai [garai]. Also spelled Garay. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Gara. See Gara for more information. GARI Gari [gari]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant of Gar. See Gara for etymology. GARAZDA Garázda [gara:zda]. ‘Rowdy, reckless’. One who is especially generous with other people's possessions. Its etymology is obscure. Old Hungarian spelling was Garazda in 1375.



46
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
GARDI Gárdi [ga:rdi]. The derivational suffix -i denotes a person from Gárd, present in the name of two villages in Abaúj County of historical Hungary, confined to Slovakia by the Trianon Peace Treaty in 1920. The name was first recorded as Olgord [Algárd] 'Lower Gárd' in 1280. Felgárd 'Upper Gárd' also existed but both villages were often referred to as Gárd. They both amalgamated with other settlements and their vestige was lost. The name Gárd contains the Sumerian gar 'pose, appearance' (Labat 1948: No. 597). The word gar is not listed in Hungarian dictionaries, only its derivative garral and only examples are given for different meanings in sentences. These examples suggest that the root gar may have had, at one time, a meaning that described the attitude and conduct of a person having abundant self-confidence noticeable in his attitude. One who behaved with much ado. The suffix -d denotes a place. The French garder 'to guard' is an unlikely source. GARZO Garzó [garzo:]. The name has no meaning in modern Hungarian. It may be derived from the Sumerian garza 'religious order' (Labat 1948: No. 295b) implying a person that belonged to such an order. GAYDA Gayda [gajda]. The name means 'bagpipe' in the Turkish language. See Gajdos for more information. GAZDA Gazda [gazda]. 'Farmer, landowner'. It also meant 'head of the family' in archaic Hungarian. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language suggests the Slavonic gospoda 'hotel' as the possible source, but notes that the historical development of gazda cannot be satisfactorily linked to the Slavonic word. The most probable origin is the Sumerian luga-zi-da 'farmer' (Gostony 1975: 76). GECSE Gecse [geche]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It is probably derived from the Turkish geç [gech] 'late, delayed' (as in time). A tardy person may have received such a name. Gecse is also a place name in Veszprém County, named after its founder. Old Hungarian spellings: 1255 Jeche; 1263 Geche; 1270 Gesche; 1306 Gueche; 1309 Getche; 1312 Gechche; 1315 Gerche. GECSEY Gecsey [gechei]. The archaic derivational suffix -y denotes a person from Gecse, a village in Veszprém County. See above for more information. GEMES Gémes [ge:mesh]. The adjectival suffix -es indicates possession of a gém 'the sweep of a lifting mechanism' like a crane. One having a sweep pole well on his property may have received the name Gémes. Another possible origin is the Sumerian geme ‘female’ (Labat 1948: No. 554) to which the adjectival suffix -s is added to indicate a married man. Old Hungarian spelling was Gem- in 1138. GERDE Gérde [ge:rde]. An altered form of gírda 'lean, raw boned'. The following variants have the initial element ger and gir in regional dialects: gerc 'spine'; gérces 'lean (person); gercsa 'spine and ribs together'; gerdó 'lean'; girbic 'lean'; girhes 'lean, raw boned' (B. Lőrinczy 1988: 628, 646, vol. 2). The name Gérde is a probable derivation from the Sumerian gir 'bone' (Labat 1948: No. 10). The surname Csontos 'bony' means the same thing. Csont means 'bone' in present-day Hungarian.



47
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
GERENCSER Gerencsér [gerenche:r]. Derived from the Slavonic grnčar 'potter' (Benkő 1967: 1042, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling was Gherencher in 1389. GERENDAS Gerendás [gerenda:sh]. The adjectival suffix -ás indicates a maker or seller of gerenda 'beam, rafter'. Two theories exist as to the origin of gerenda: 1 From the Slavonic *greda 'beam' (Benkő 1967: 1050, vol. 1). 2 A derivative of the Sumerian girinnum 'truss, beam' (Gostony 1975: 77). Old Hungarian spelling was Guerenda in 1331. GESZT Geszt [gest]. A dweller by the geszt 'grove' may have been named so. Geszt is also the name of a locality in Békés County. Old Hungarian spellings: 1213 Gest; 1332 Kezt-. GESZTI Geszti [gesti]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Geszt, a locality in Békés County. See Geszt above. GIDA Gida [gida]. There are two possibilities for the source of this name. The word gida means 'young goat' in the Hungarian language but it has no probable reason to form a surname. The Sumerian gid(-da) 'long' (Labat 1948: 371) may denote a tall person. GIGAS Gigás [giga:sh]. The adjectival suffix -ás denotes a person with a prominent giga 'throat' (Adam's apple). Giga is a regional dialectic form for gége 'throat'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1138 Gegu, Geugu; 1229 Gegus; 1888 giga. GILICE Gilice [gilitse]. The endearing form of gerlice 'turtle-dove', the Hungarian version of the Slavonic grlica, *g'rdlica (Benkő 1967: 1054: vol. 1). Old Hungarian spellings: 1372 gerlicze; 1416 gorlice; 1558 gilice-. GILVESY Gilvesy [gilveshi]. Anglicized form of Gyülvészi. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Gyülvész, an altered form of Gyülevész, a village in Zala County. The place name denotes a location where people assembled for meetings and other purposes. Old Hungarian spellings: 1406 Gelwez; 1475 Gyelwez. GILYAN Gilyán [gija:n]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. The root gil 'to destroy' (Labat 1948: No. 67) is a Sumerian word which may have been given to an antagonistic person. The second element yan may be the Indo-European -ian that forms adjectives. The initial element Gil was a Hungarian name, also recorded as Gyl in 1211 (Fehértói 1983: 148). GINA Gina [gina]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It may be the continuation of the Sumerian gi-na 'steady, stable' (Labat 1948: No. 85). GIRHES Girhes [girhesh]. The epithet girhes is mostly applied to describe a 'lean, raw boned, sickly' person. Its variant Gera is also a surname. The name Girhes may have been given to a thin, raw boned person. The root gir is identical with the Sumerian gir 'bone' (Labat 1948: No. 10) both in meaning and phonetic form. The h that follows the root word is a connecting consonant. The adjectival suffix -es denotes condition. Old Hungarian spellings: 1322 Gurhes; 1505 Gerwhes; 1561 görhes; 1865 girhes. GOBE Góbé [go:be:]. The name of a 'crafty' person in the Transylvanian dialect.



48
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1617 goba-; 1660 góbé-. GOBOLOS Göbölös [gøbøløsh]. The adjectival suffix -ös denotes a person who fattens oxes for human consumption. The Hungarian word for such ox is göböly. Old Hungarian spellings: 1402 Gwbul; 1585 Göböly; 1801 göböl-. GOCH Göch [gøch]. Derivation from the Turkish göç [gøch] 'goods and chattels of migrating people'. Cf. Hungarian gönc. GOCSEI Göcsei [gøchei]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Göcsej 'undulating country', the south-west region of Zala County (B. Lőrinczy 1988: 671, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling was Gőcsey in 1699. GODOR Gödör [gødør}. A dweller by the gödör 'pit' may have received the name. Old Hungarian spellings: 1251 Gudur-; 1254 -gudur; 1367 Gewder; 1385 Geudeur; 1428 Gwder; 1832 ködör; 1833 gődör. GOGOS Gőgös [gø:gøsh]. 'Arrogantly proud'. Derived from gőg 'pride, arrogance' to which the adjectival suffix -ös is added. Old Hungarian spellings: 1219 Gugus; 1224 Guegus; 1377 Gegus; 1456 geges-; 1495 göggös. GOMBOCZ Gömböcz [gømbøts]. A portly person is referred to as gömböc, derived from gömb 'round mass'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1422 Gembech; 1475 gewmbetz; 1577 gömböcz. GOMBOS Gombos [gombosh]. The adjectival suffix -os denotes a maker of gomb 'button'. The Hungarian gomb may be derived from the Sumerian gam 'bent' or gišgam 'round' (Labat 1948: No. 362). The original fastener, holding two sides of a garment together, may have been a hook-like implement pushed through a hole or a loop formed by a string. The disk or knob and the button hole were later developments. It is conceivable that the original name gam was retained after the hook was changed to a disk shape. Old Hungarian spelling was Gombos in 1211. GOMORI Gömöri [gømøri]. Also spelled Gömöry. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Gömör, the name of a County in historical Hungary. Most of the County was confined to Slovakia in 1920 by the Trianon Peace Treaty and then renamed Gemer. The name is derived from the Old Turkic kömür 'coal' which is also a surname and place name in Turkic speaking countries. Old Hungarian spelling was Gumur in 1150 (Kiss 1978: 244). GONDA Gonda [gonda]. Also spelled Gunda. The name is a probable derivative of the Sumerian gun 'burden, responsibility' (Labat 1948: No. 108) which may be the antecedent of the present-day Hungarian gond 'care, worry'. Comprised of gond and the SumeroHungarian adjectival suffix -a, the name denotes a 'careful, attentive' person. The modern Hungarian form would be Gondos. GONDOS Gondos [gondosh]. See above. GORBE Görbe [gørbe]. The name describes a 'round shouldered, stooping' person. It is a composite of gör, the root of words associated with being bent. Its variants are gördül 'turn, rotate', kör 'circle', kerek 'round', kerék 'wheel'. The connecting consonant b is



49
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
followed by the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -e to indicate condition. The Hungarian gör may be a variant of the Sumerian gar 'to bend' (Labat 1948: No. 106), which is görbít in present-day Hungarian. The southern Slavonic source grba 'hump', listed in the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, is not convincing. GOROG Görög [gørøg]. The Hungarian name for Greek is Görög. Early Hungarian sources, when refer to the ethnic denomination Greek, include all peoples from the Balkan peninsula who belonged to the Greek Orthodox religion. Many Orthodox Serbs, Bulgars, Macedonians and other people were included in this category. Most of them entered Hungary as subjects of the Ottoman Empire during the Turkish occupation. They were privileged merchants who handled the trade between Turkish occupied Hungary and the Balkan (Ács 1986: 139). The name is the Hungarian version of an internationally shared word of obscure origin. The Greeks used it to describe themselves as early as 700 BC. The term was applied to all peoples, cities and states that possessed a common Greek language and culture (McKenzie 1965: 326). Old Hungarian spelling: 1156 Gyrki; 1395 gereg; 1416 gorog; 1500 Gewreg-; 1510 gevreg; 1538 gerewg-, gürüg. GOSZTONYI Gosztonyi [gostonji]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant of Gosztony, the Hungarian variant of Gostun, the name of a Kotrigur khan (AD 584 - 642), vassal of the Avars, whose principle territory was in the Carpathian Basin (Vernadsky 1952: 197). GOZONY Gozony [gozonj]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It may be a derivation from Gozan (no meaning) the name of a Mesopotamian region mentioned in the Bible. It is called Guzanu in Assyrian records (McKenzie 1965: 323). GROF Gróf [gro:f]. Derivation from the Austro-Bavarian grof 'count', cf. German Graf 'count'. The surname denotes a person being aristocratic by disposition if not by title. One who has the manners of an aristocrat. Old Hungarian spellings: 1358 Grof-; 1393 Grof; 1409 Groff; 1553 gróf. GUBA Guba [guba]. Guba is the name of a peasant's long sleeveless shaggy frieze cape. The name depicts a maker or seller of such capes. Guba is the archaic spelling of the more recent Gubás. Both the -a and -s are adjectival suffixes that denote occupations. Old Hungarian spelling was Guba in 1387. GUG Gug [gug]. Also spelled Gugg. This name is the root of guggol 'squat' and denotes a person in the habit of squatting. The Hungarian gug may be cognate with the Sumerian gub, kup which, according to Labat means 'to maintain an upright position' (Labat 1948: No. 206). This meaning may have been erroneously assigned to the word considering that both gub and kup are the roots of the Hungarian gubbaszt 'crouch' and kuporog 'squat'. Consequently, gug, gub and kup may derive from an older common word. Another possible source is the Sumerian gug 'pure, bright' (Labat 1948: No. 591). Old Hungarian spellings: 1548 gugg-; 1552 gug. GULYA Gulya [guja]. The root gu is followed by the connecting consonant ly [j] and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a. Gulya means 'cattle' in present-day Hungarian.



50
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
The word gu is identical with the Sumerian gu4 'ox, cattle' (Labat 1948: No. 297) both phonetically and semantically. Other Hungarian surnames derived from the same root are Gulás and Gulyás.
Old Hungarian spelling was Gulla in 1237. GYALI Gyáli [dja:li]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Gyál, a village in Pest County. The Hungarian Gyál may be a variant of the old Turkish yal 'flame' (Clauson 1972: 929), which also survived in the Hungarian title Gyula also a surname and forename. See Gyula. The location Gyál may have been the site of fire worship. Old Hungarian spelling was Gyal in 1323 (Kiss 1978: 250). GYENGE Gyenge [djenge]. The Hungarian gyenge 'weak, infirm' may be derived from the Sumerian gig 'ill, sick' (Labat 1948: No. 446). The word gyengélkedő 'ill, ailing' is derived from gyenge and has a meaning identical with its Sumerian counterpart. Old Hungarian spelling was Genge in 1347. GYOKERI Gyökéri [djøke:ri]. An unusual surname. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from the gyökér 'root'. The word gyökér is a probable compound of dug 'thrust' and ér 'vein', describing the system of membranous canals penetrating into the soil through which plants fix themselves in the earth to absorb nutrients. The Ugric source jeker, jukar 'uprooted tree', suggested by the HistoricalEtymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, only touches on exactness. Old Hungarian spelling: 1130 Guquer. GYORI Győri [djø:ri]. Also spelled Győry. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Győr, a principal city in Győr-Sopron County, ranking high in industrial production. Several explanations were advanced for the etymology of Győr. One of them is gyűr 'elevated ground in a wetland' (B. Lőrinczy 1988: 770, vol. 2). The original settlement was indeed on Káptalan-domb 'Cathedral Hill' and this etymology seems to be the most convincing. Old Hungarian spellings of Győr: 1009 Jaur-; 1213 Jewr; 1221 Geur. GYOZO Győző [djø:zø:]. 'Victor'. A probable derivation from the Sumerian giš 'successfully accomplish' (Labat 1948: No. 296). The name is comprised of győz 'succeed, win' and the participle -ő which forms adjectives from verbs. GYULA Gyula [djula]. A frequent Hungarian forename and surname. The word-final element -a is the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix which indicates rank and status. It is possible, as suggested by the first element gyul 'to ignite, to light fire', that an ancient Asian religious title of fire worship survived in the name. This is substantiated by the old Turkish yalin, yal 'flame' (Clauson 1972: 929) and present-day yalaz 'flame'. Also compare with the Sumerian ul4 'to flame, to burn' (Labat 1948: No. 10). The original religious title was later extended to include non-religious functions such as civil and military leadership (Németh 1990: 224) spelled jula, jila. The Sumerian ugula 'head, chieftain' (Labat 1948: No. 295) also shows correlation to Gyula, Jula and Gula. Old Hungarian spelling: 950 γυλά; 1075 Jula; 1150 Geula, Gyyla; GYURUS Gyűrűs [djy:ry:sh]. Derived from the Sumerian gur 'to turn around' (Labat 1948: No. 111), the Hungarian gyűrűs means a 'ring maker'. The name is comprised of



51
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
gyűr, (a word synonymous with göngyöl, gördít 'to roll') the participle -ű that forms gyűrű ‘ring’ and the adjectival suffix -s to denote a maker of rings. Similar words exist in the Altaic languages, cf. Turkish yüzük 'ring' but its suggested source is the Turkic yüz 'joint, segment' which is íz in the Hungarian language (Benkő 1967: 1988, vol. 1). For this reason the Turkic origin remains doubtful. HABOLY Haboly [haboli]. The surname is derived from Hobol, the name of a settlement in Baranya County. Now part of Szigetvár. The name is of uncertain origin. The wordfinal element -y is the archaic derivational suffix indicating a person from Hobol. HADAS Hadas [hadash]. The adjectival suffix -as is appended to had to denote involvement in military affairs. Though dictionaries list had as 'army' it is not an exact translation. The Hungarian equivalent of 'army' is hadsereg in which sereg means 'troops, multitude' but had stands rather for ‘weapon’. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language lists had as a variant of *kunta a Finno-Ugric word which can be found in the name of the Ostyak (Khanty) people. The Vogul khont 'war, army', Finn kunta 'crowd', Estonian kond 'family' and other Finno-Ugric words stem from the same root. The only Finno-Ugric word that comes close to the Hungarian had, both in phonetic value and meaning, is the Vogul khont 'war', which would make the Hungarian hadsereg translate as 'war party'. The rest of Finno-Ugric words do not fit into the Hungarian concept of had. Therefore, the source of the word should be searched somewhere else. Onomastic evidence point in the direction of the Sumerian language where the cuneiform sign for pa 'branch of a tree' is also pronounced as gišhad meaning 'wooden bat, stick'.
