The present-day Hungarian equivalent of 'bat' is bot. The Sumerian cuneiform sign for pa was also pronounced as bad, bat 'bat, scepter, stave' (Waddell 1927: 23). But during the Neo-Babylonian and Neo-Assyrian period the pronunciation was zák and zága (Labat 1948: No.295). Both phonetic values correspond to the Hungarian ág 'branch of a tree'. The wooden bat, broken off the tree was the original rudimentary weapon of the ancient warrior and its Sumerian name survived in the Hungarian had in the sense of 'weapon'. Hence had-sereg means 'armed troops'. If the word had were sufficient to denote 'army' then there would be no need for the second word sereg 'troops'. The surname Hadas, therefore, originally meant either a maker or supplier of weapons, or articles of military nature. Hadad, the name of a Mesopotamian destructive storm god (McKenzie 1965: 329) may have been also derived from the Sumero-Hungarian had. The name was recorded in 1138 as a surname in Hungary in the form of Hadadi 'descendant of Hadad'. The word-final element -i is the derivational suffix denoting ancestry. Old Hungarian spellings of had: 1055 hodu; 1082 Hada; 1093 hod-. HADFI Hadfi [hadfi]. The present-day meaning of this name is 'warrior' comprised of had and the patronymic suffix -fi, short for fiú 'boy, young man'. Originally the name may have meant ‘son of a gunsmith’. See Hadas for the etymology of had. HADIK Hadik [hadik]. The initial element had is interpreted as 'weapon' to which the suffix -ik is appended to express an active link with the meaning of the preceding noun. The ancestor of the bearer of this name was probably a professional soldier or had a long



52
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
term relationship with the armed forces in one capacity or another. See Hadas for the etymology of had. HADNAGY Hadnagy [hadnadj]. The present-day meaning of hadnagy is 'lieutenant' but originally it denoted a 'general, commander of the army'. The name is a compound of had (see Hadas for meaning and etymology) and nagy 'great, superior, big' meaning 'chief of the armed forces'. The Hungarian nagy has similar phonetic value and meaning as the Sumerian [nu]g 'large, big' (Labat 1948: No. 72). Old Hungarian spellings: 1237 Hodnog-; 1347 Hodnogh; 1358 hadnag; 1602 Hadnagy. HAGYMASY Hagymásy [hadjma:shi]. The name denotes a descendant of Hagymás as indicated by the archaic derivational suffix -y. Hagymás is comprised of the element hagyma 'onion' and the adjectival suffix -s which denotes an occupation. Accordingly, hagymás means a 'grower or seller of onions'. The principal meaning of hagyma is 'bulb' and should be preceded by a qualifier like fokhagyma 'garlic', tulipánhagyma 'tulip bulb'. The correct form of 'onion' is vöröshagyma 'red bulb' but in routine conversation only hagyma is used. Hagyma is a probable derivation from the Finno-Ugric *koćmɜ or *kaćmɜ (Benkő 1967: 18, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spellings: 1211 Hagymas; 1497 Hagmas; 1211 Haymus, Haimus; 1237 Heymus. HAJAS Hajas [hajash]. 'Hairy'. The adjectival suffix -as indicates condition. The Hungarian haj denotes ‘hair on top of the head’ only. Hair everywhere else on the body is called szőr, except for szakál 'beard'. Haj is shared by Hungarian and other languages of the Uralic group. The reconstructed proto-word is *kajɜ (Benkő 1967: 20, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spellings: 1211 Haia; 1438 Hayas. HAJDU Hajdú [hajdu:]. Also spelled Hajtó, Hajdó. The original form is Hajtó but Hajdú is now a more frequent surname. The name is the participle of hajt 'drive, move' and was the name of Hungarian cowboys participating in long distance cattle drives. Those cattleherders did their job on foot, unlike their mounted counterparts in America. In off seasons they were hired as body guards and uniformed attendants, a job some of them retained, but continued to be called hajdú. Old Hungarian spellings: 1500 Haydo; 1513 Haydow; 1602 Hajdó. HAJNAL Hajnal [hajnal]. 'Dawn'. The name denotes an early riser. The Hungarian hajnal 'dawn' is cognate with the Vogul khuj 'dawn' and with similar words in other Ugric languages (Benkő 1967: 26, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spellings: 1372 haynal-; 1513 halnal, Hajna. HAJOS Hajós [hajo:sh]. The adjectival suffix -s denotes a ship builder, a person who served on a ship or was associated with ships in some way. The word-final element -ó in hajó 'ship' is the participle suffix. The probable root of the word is haj(-lít) 'bend, curve' describing the bent and curving boards or other material ships are built of. The Turkish kayik 'boat' is phonetically and semantically close to the Hungarian hajó but it denotes a 'kayak'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1338 Hayos; 1638 haios.



53
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
HALAS Halas [halash]. The adjectival suffix -as is affixed to hal 'fish' to signify a 'dealer in fish'. The Hungarian hal is related to the Ostyak (Khanty) kul, Mari (Cheremis) kol, Finnish and Estonian kala and other Uralic languages in which they all mean 'fish' (Benkő 1967: 31, vol. 2). The Uralic words appear to be cognate with the Sumerian ku6 'fish' (Labat 1948: No. 589). Old Hungarian spelling was Halas in 1240. HALASI Halasi [halashi]. Also spelled Halassy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) denotes a descendant of Halas. See Halas for etymology. HALASZ Halas [hala:s]. The formative suffix -ász is affixed to hal 'fish' to denote a specialized occupation. Halász means 'fisherman or fisher', equivalent of the English surname Fisher. See Halas for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling was halaz in 1193. HALBOR Halbőr [halbø:r]. The name is a compound of hal 'fish' and bőr 'skin, leather'. There is no apparent reason for such a word to form a surname, unless one's ancestor was a leather-dresser specializing in fish skins. Both elements of the name can be explained by the Sumerian language: ku6 'fish' (Labat 1948: No.589) and bar 'skin, leather' (Labat 1948: No. 74). It may be of importance to note that the same word is used for 'skin' and 'leather' in both the Sumerian and Hungarian languages. Both hal and bőr are present in the Uralic languages. For the etymology of hal see Halas. The reconstructed Uralic proto-word of bőr is *perɜ (Benkő 1967: 346, vol. 1), but the most probable source is the Sumerian bar. HALMI Halmi [halmi]. Also spelled Halmy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) denotes a dweller by the halom 'knoll'. Hungarians share halom with the Slavonic languages cf. Serbo-Croat hum 'heap, mound'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1055 holm-; 1193 -holm; 1303 Halum; 1340 halm; 1418 -halom. HAMAR Hamar [hamar]. 'Quick, fast, soon'. The name may have been bestowed on a fast, hardworking individual. Old Hungarian spelling was Hamar in 1327. HAMIS Hamis [hamish]. 'Insincere, cunning'. Old Hungarian spelling was Homus in 1064. HAMULA Hamula [hamula]. This name can only be partially etymologized in the Hungarian language. The segment hamu means 'ash' in present-day Hungarian but la 'plenty, abundance' (Labat 1948: No. 55) can only be found in the Sumerian language. The compound means 'plenty of ashes', which is considered to be unusual, even as a hybrid name. The Hungarian hamu's Uralic equivalent is the Ezra-Mordvin kulov 'ash, sand' and the Mordvin kulu 'ash' (Benkő 1967: 42, vol. 2). However, the ultimate source of the word may be the Sumerian ukum 'ash, sand' (Gostony 1975: 14). The Akkadian hahu 'ash' (Labat 1948: No.172) is much closer to the phonetic form of the Hungarian hamu and should also be considered as a cognate word. Old Hungarian spelling of hamu was Homu in 1211. HAMVAS Hamvas [hamvash]. The adjectival suffix -as is appended to hamv 'down,



54
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
bloom' to denote a person having a greyish complexion. Hamv is derived from hamu 'ash'. See Hamula for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling was Homvos in 1314. HARAGOS Haragos [haragosh]. 'Angry'. The name describes a person who has a chip on his shoulder. It is comprised of harag 'anger' and the adjectival suffix -os. The Hungarian word shows relevance to the Uralic languages, cf. Mordvin kor 'anger'. The reconstructed Finno-Ugric proto-word is *kore (Benkő 1967: 52, vol. 2). The Sumerian urgú 'anger' (Labat 1948: No. 22+) should also be considered. Also compare with Persian har 'mad, rabid' (Lambton 1988: 176). Old Hungarian spellings: 1211 Horogus; 1274 Haragus; 1487 Haragos. HARCOS Harcos [hartsosh]. The adjectival suffix -os is attached to harc 'fight, combat' to denote a combatant person, perhaps a soldier. The word harc is derived from the root har(-zsol), hor(-zsol) 'graze, scratch' which is identical with the Sumerian hur 'to scratch' (Thomsen 1984: 307). Old Hungarian spelling was harchos in 1560. HARGITAI Hargitai [hargitai]. Also spelled Hargitay. The name denotes a dweller by the Hargita a mountain range in Transylvania, confined to Romania in 1920. The name is of Sumerian origin comprised of har a variant of kur 'mountain' (Labat 1948: No. 364), gi10 'peak' (Labat 1948: No. 173) and ta 'without' (Labat 1948: No. 139). The compound Hargita would translate as 'mountain range without peaks'. The word final element -i (archaic -y) is the derivational suffix. HARMAT Harmat [harmat]. 'Dew'. Also spelled Harmath. A quiet person who achieves results in a subtle way, like the materialization of dew, may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling was Harmat- in 1355. HARSANY Harsány [harsha:nj]. The nominal suffix -ány is appended to the root hars 'loud' to indicate a person having a booming voice. Old Hungarian spelling was Harsany in 1616. HASAB Hasáb [hasha:b]. The name denotes a 'log of split wood', signifying a person involved in either splitting wood or selling it. The root has 'split, crack, tear', to which the unproductive -áb is suffixed, may be cognate with similar words in the Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *kaćɜ (Benkő 1967: 67, vol. 2). The Uralic word may be a derivation from the Sumerian ḫaš 'to break', or the Akkadian hasâsu 'to cut' (Labat 1948: No. 12). Compare with Hungarian hasít ‘split, cleave’. HASKA Haska [hashka]. The archaic adjectival suffix -a signifies a person engaged in splitting (wood?) or breaking (stone?). The intermediate k is a connecting consonant. See Hasab for etymology of has. HATAR Határ [hata:r]. A person from the frontier region may have been named Határ 'frontier, border'. The Hungarian word may be a variant of the Akkadian kudurru 'boundary' (Labat 1948: No. 97). HAZAI Hazai [hazai]. Also spelled Hazay. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) denotes a person from the haza 'native land', derived from ház 'house'. The Hungarian ház is cognate with other Finno-Ugric words like the Ostyak kat 'house'. The reconstructed proto-word is *kota (Benkő 1967: 76, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling was haza in 1256. HEGEDUS Hegedűs [hegedy:sh]. 'Violinist'. The Hungarian hegedű 'violin' is a probable



55
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
derivative of the Sumerian gi-gi-du16 'violin' (Gostony 1975: 73). Old Hungarian spellings: 1394 Hegedus; 1396 Hegedews; 1418 Hegedes; 1550 hegedös; 1552 hegedüs. HEJJA Héjja [he:ja]. Also spelled Hélya. Both names are distorted forms of héja 'hawk'. A keeper of hawks or a rapacious person may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling was Héja in 1602. HEPPES Heppes [hepesh]. The reduplication of p marks a stressed consonant. The adjectival suffix -es is attached to hepp(-eg) 'walk slowly' to denote an individual who walks with a halt. HID Híd [hi:d]. The name suggests a dweller by the híd 'bridge'. See Hidas for more detail. HIDAS Hidas [hidash]. A dweller near a bridge may have been named so. It may also denote a toll-gate attendant at a bridge. The name incorporates the word híd 'bridge' and the adjectival suffix -as which indicates occupation. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language lists híd as an Alanic loan-word. But most likely híd is derived from the Sumerian id 'river' (Labat 1948: No. 579). This Sumerian word is written with the combination of two independent cuneiform signs: a 'water' (Labat 1948: No. 578) and zikum 'top, roof' (Labat 1948: No. 484). Thus, the appropriate interpretation of the cuneiform sign for id would be a rooflike structure across a body of water, that would likely be a (covered) bridge, which Hungarians call híd. Old Hungarian spellings: 1231 Hydus-; 1358 Hydas; HIDASI Hidasi [hidashi]. Also spelled Hidassy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Hidas. See Hidas for more explanation. HIDI Hídi [hidi]. Also spelled Hídy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a dweller by a híd 'bridge'. See Hid and Hidas for more information. HIRES Híres [hi:resh]. The adjectival suffix -es is appended to hír 'news, report' to indicate a 'famous, well-known' individual, i.e. one talked about a lot, or is in the news frequently. Old Hungarian spelling was hyres in 1507. HITES Hites [hitesh]. The adjectival suffix -es is affixed to hit 'trust, faith' to denote a person in a capacity that requires taking an oath, i.e. a sworn, legitimate. Old Hungarian spelling was Hotus, Hutos, Hitu in 1138. HOGYE Hőgye [hø:dje]. Comprised of hőgy 'ermine' and the archaic Hungarian adjectival suffix -e the name denotes an 'ermine-hunter'. This occupation would be hőgyes or hőlgyész in present-day Hungarian. Old Hungarian spelling was heulg- in 1324. HOLLO Holló [holo:]. 'Raven'. The reduplication of l marks a stressed consonant. The name may owe its origin to the jet-black hair of a person. Hungarians refer to deep black color as holló fekete 'raven-black'. A person having such hair may have been named Holló.
Similar words are in other Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *kulɜ (Benkő 1967: 134, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spellings: 1231 Holo; 1315 Hollo; 1602 Holló. HOMOKI Homoki [homoki]. Also spelled Homoky. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y)



56
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
indicates a person from an area of sandy soil. The word homok means 'sand' in the Hungarian language. Sand is pulverized rock, which puts the root hom (the word-final element -ok is the plural number) in a close proximity of the Sumerian hum 'to pulverize, to crush' (Labat 1948: No. 565). Similar words exist in the Turkic languages, cf. Turkish kum (plural: kumluk) 'sand'. Old Hungarian spelling was humuk in 1055. HONFI Honfi [honfi]. Comprised of hon 'home, native land' and the patronymic suffix fi, the name denotes a 'son of the native land'. The Hungarian hon may be a variant of the Sumerian unu 'residence' (Labat 1948: No. 195). HONOS Hónos [ho:nosh]. The name cannot be explained entirely in the Hungarian language. The element hón is present only in hónalj 'armpit', where alj 'bottom' suggests a part of the body under the hón, a term that became obsolete in the Hungarian language a long time ago. The Udmurt (Sarapul-Votyak) word for 'armpit’ is kun-ul (Benkő 1967: 141, vol. 2), in which the element kun is identical with the Sumerian kun 'shoulder' (Labat 1948: No. 77). It is therefore suggested that the Hungarian hón, the Udmurt kun and other similar Uralic words are derived from the Sumerian kun. The adjectival suffix -os, attached to hón, suggests a 'broad-shouldered' person who would be called vállas in present-day Hungarian. HORDOSY Hordósy [hordo:shi]. Also spelled Hordóssy. The archaic derivational suffix -y denotes a descendant of Hordós 'cooper'. The adjectival suffix -s, attached to hordó 'barrel, cask', signifies occupation. The word hordó is comprised of the element hord 'carry' and the participle -ó. The word hord may be compared to the reconstructed Ugric proto-word *kurɜ (Benkő 1967: 145, 146, vol. 2) with reservations. The ultimate source of hordó may be the Sumerian gur7 'tun, cask for wine' (Labat 1948: No. 542) followed by the connecting consonant d and the participle -ó. Old Hungarian spellings: 1391 Hordow; 1587 hordo; 1629 hordós. HORVAT Horvát [horva:t]. Also spelled Horváth. It denotes the ethnic name 'Croat'. The word final element -h is added to mark the reduced plosion of the preceding t. The initial element hor is most likely derived from the Slavonic word for 'mountain' and signifies a mountaineer' or 'highlander', a name probably given to them by lowlanders, people of the plain. Cf. Czech, Slovak hora, Serbian, Croatian gora, Polish góra, from which Góral, the name of a mountain-dwelling people in the Northern-Carpathians, is derived. The Slavonic variants may be cognate with the Persian (Indo-European) kuh 'mountain' (Lambton 1988: 307). However, the ultimate source of the word may be the Sumerian (Non-IndoEuropean) kur 'mountain' (Labat 1948: No. 366). It may be of interest that Hor was also a mountain in ancient Palestine, located in South-West Jordan and identified with the present-day Jebel Harun (Barnhart 1954: 2051, vol. 2). The v connects the root hor to the suffix -át, which converts verbs and nouns to derived nouns. It may be called a nominal suffix with caution, when appended to a verb. But, to my knowledge, there is no generally accepted appropriate English term when



57
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
suffixed to a noun. In that case it may be included in the category of formatives. Examples: nyug 'repose' > nyugat 'west'; mag 'seed' > magzat 'embryo, offspring'; húz 'draw' > huzat > 'draft'. Old Hungarian spellings: 1138 Horuat, Huruat, Hwruat; 1309 Huruath; 1377 Horwath; 1511 Horváth. HULINYI Hulinyi [hulinji]. The name has no meaning in present-day Hungarian. It may be derived from the Sumerian hul 'mischief' (Labat 1948: No. 456) to which -iny is suffixed to denote a collective noun. The word-final element -i is the derivational suffix indicating a descendant of Huliny 'rascal'. HUSSAR Huszár [husa:r]. A member of the Hungarian light cavalry is called huszár. The hussars were formed as a military unit to pursue the fleeing enemy. It may be the altered form of the Sumerian húb-sar 'to pursue, to hunt' (Labat 1948: No. 88). The Slavonic husa 'theft', the Latin corsarius 'pirate' or the Byzantine hosarios 'spy' (Benkő 1967: 174, vol. 2) are not convincing sources. Old Hungarian spellings: 1432 huzor; 1449 Hwzar; 1477 Huzar; 1558 huszár. HUSVAR Húsvár [hu:shva:r]. The word húsvár has no possible reason to form a surname. It is comprised of hús 'meat, flesh' and vár 'fort'. However, hús in this name may have its source in the Sumerian language where kuš means 'leather, pelt, skin' (Labat 1948: No. 7) and the Hungarian vár 'fort' may be derived from the Sumerian bar 'circular wall' (Deimel 1939: No. 49). Therefore kušbar means ‘a wall made of pelts’. A dweller in such dwelling may have been named so. HUVOS Hűvös [hy:vøsh]. ‘Cool, reserved’. The name consists of the root hű(v) ‘cool’ and the adjectival suffix -ös indicates character. Old Hungarian spelling was Huuus in 1211. IFJU Ifjú [ifju:]. Also spelled Ifijú. 'Young man, lad'. The name may be derived from the Sumerian ibila 'young man, lad' (Deimel 1939: No. 112). The word ifiú is listed in the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language as a compound of i and fiú. The initial element i is the same as the Ostyak äi 'small, young' (Benkő 190, vol. 2), while fiú is derived from the Finno-Ugric *pojka (Benkő 1948: 925, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spellings: 1320 Ifyu-; 1372 yfyo-; 1604 Ifiju. IGAZ Igaz [igaz]. 'True'. A 'trusty, faithful' person may have been named so. Old Hungarian spellings: 1300 ygoz; 1348 Igaz-. IHASZ Ihász [iha:s]. A regional form of juhász 'shepherd' in Somogy County. The name is comprised of ih, a variant of juh 'sheep' and the formative suffix -ász to indicate a specialized occupation. Old Hungarian spelling was Ihaz in 1400. IJJAS Ijjas [ijash]. The adjectival suffix -as is preceded by íj 'bow' (weapon) to denote an 'archer or bowyer'. Reduplication of j marks a stressed consonant. The Hungarian íj is of obscure origin, probably derived from an older í, the root of ív 'arch, bend' (Benkő 1967: 195, vol. 2). Another possible source is the Sumerian illu 'arch' (Gostony 1975: 69). The Sumerian origin is supported by the Hungarian surname Illos 'bowyer’, which cannot be explained in the present-day vernacular. The Hungarian íj may be cognate with the Ostyak juxat, Syrian juxal, Cumanian



58
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
jaa, Chinese i, all having the same meaning (Gostony 1975: 69). Old Hungarian spelling was Jgyartho 'bow-maker' in 1446. ILKU Ilku [ilku]. This name cannot be explained in the Hungarian language. It may be the unchanged form of the Sumerian ilku which would translate as 'professional soldier’. The majority of these soldiers was drawn from the upper class of each city-state and was constantly at the king's disposal (Woolley 1965: 59-60, 112-114). The pre-thirteenth-century Turkish ilk 'first in order of time and place' (Clauson 1972: 140) may be cognate with the Sumerian ilku in a sense of indicating the highest ranking social class. In modern Turkish ilk means '(the) first, initial, primary'. ILLOS Illos [ilosh]. The adjectival suffix -os is attached to i 'bow' to denote a 'bowyer'. The reduplicated l indicates a stressed consonant which functions as a connecting sound in this case. See Ijjas for more explanation. ILMA Ilma [ilma]. The name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. It may signify an important leader in the Sumerian language by compounding il 'important, eminent, great' (Labat 1948: No. 205) and mah 'powerful, influential, leading' (Labat 1948: No. 57). The Finnish ilma 'air, atmosphere, weather' may also be considered. Ilma was the name of the weather-god in ancient Finnish society. IMECS Imecs [imech]. The second element -ecs may be the distorted form of the adjectival suffix -es which denotes an occupation. The initial element im 'tablet, document' is a Sumerian word (Labat 1948: No. 339). Therefore Imecs denotes a person involved with clay tablets and documents. Probably an archivist. The word imecs means 'horse fly' in the Gyergyó dialect of Transylvania (B. Lőrinczy 1988: 1077, vol. 2), but would be an unlikely source for a surname. IMOLA Imola [imola]. A dweller near an imola 'swampland full of reed-grass'. Old Hungarian spelling was imola in 1193. IMRE Imre [imre]. The oldest known reference to this name is the son of Hungary's St. Stephen (975-1038) called Imre. Two theories exist for the origin of the name. 1. From the well-publicized German Amalrich which consists of Amal, the name of an old Gothic royal family, and rich, believed to be the German equivalent of the Latin rex 'king' (Ladó 1978: 170). 2. From the Akkadian nimru 'leopard' (Labat 1948: No. 444) of which Imru, Imre, Imró may be the aphetic forms. The name of biblical Nimrod and the ancient Mesopotamian city of Nimrud are derived from the same source. Old Hungarian spellings: 1374 Imbregh; 1438 Emreh; 1466 Embre; 1526 Imreh. IMREDY Imrédy [imre:di]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates place of origin, the suffix -d denotes a place called Imre. See Imre for more information. IMREGH Imregh [imreg]. An archaic form of Imre. See Imre for more information. IMREY Imrey [imrei]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a descendant of Imre. See Imre for more information. INKEY Inkey [inkei]. The name cannot be explained in the present-day Hungarian language. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a descendant of Inke. A probable source is the Sumerian in 'flax, linen' (Labat 1948: No. 148) to which the SumeroHungarian adjectival suffix -e is added to denote an occupation. The intermediate k is a connecting consonant. The name denotes a descendant of a flax grower or processor.



59
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
The surnames Lenke and Lenkei are derived from the present-day Hungarian word len 'flax', an indication that flax was a source of names. ISTENES Istenes [ishtenesh]. 'God-fearing'. The adjectival suffix -es is attached to isten 'god' to indicate character. The Hungarian isten is phonetically identical with the Akkadian ištên 'one, the only one (Labat 1948: No. 1, 480). Akkadian was a Semitic language that supplanted the older Sumerian language in Mesopotamia c. 2000 BC. Many linguists believe that the Akkadians borrowed Sumerian words and there are implications that ištên is one of them. Ištên was the Mid-Babylonian pronunciation of the Sumerian cuneiform sign aš 'one, the only one' (Labat 1948: No. 1, 480). Survived in the Assyrian language as ištin ‘one’. 'One' means egy in the Hungarian language and it may be the present-day reference to the old concept of god. The Church, as an organization, is called egyház 'house of one', i. e. 'house of god' by Hungarians. This is synonymous with their religious belief that god is and always been a single entity. Individual churches in towns and cities were also called egyház as indicated by toponyms such as Nyíregyháza 'house of one in Nyír'. See Dicshazi for more information. Old Hungarian spelling was isten in 1195. JAGER Jáger [ja:ger]. Hungarian spelling of the German Jäger 'hunter'. JAKI Jáki [ja:ki]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Ják, a town in Vas County. This name cannot be explained in the Hungarian language. The most probable source is the Turkish yakmak 'to light, to set on fire' (Akdikmen 1986: 400), derived from the Chagatai -Osmanli root jaq 'to set on fire’ (Györffy 1990: 179). The suffix -mak is often used when listing verbs in Turkish dictionaries. The location of the town may have been a sacred site for fire worship in antiquity, thus the name Ják. JAMBOR Jámbor [ja:mbor]. Compounded from jó 'good' and ember 'man' jámbor means 'pious, humble, tolerant' (Benkő 1967: 258, vol. 2). The word jó 'good' is present in other Ugric languages in similar phonetic forms. The reconstructed proto-word is *jamɜ or *jomɜ (Benkő 1967: 275, vol. 2). See Ember for etymology of the second element in the name. Old Hungarian spellings: 1251 Joember; 1461 Jambor. JANITSARY Janitsáry [janicha:ri]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a descendant of Janitsár, a name derived from the Turkish yeniçeri 'janissary'. The word yeniçeri is a compound of yeni 'new' and çeri 'troops, army'. A soldier of such army that was originally made up of Christian slaves was referred to as janicsár and janitsár by Hungarians. Old Hungarian spellings: 1448 Janchaar; 1553 janicsár-. JANY Jány [ja:nj]. The informal spelling of leány 'girl, maiden' is jány and lyány, which may be etymologized as a compound of the Uralic *laj- 'little' and *ańa 'mother' (Benkő 1967: 734, vol. 2). Another etymology could be the Sumerian lal 'smaller' and ama 'mother' (Labat 1948: No. 481). It is an unusual surname in a patriarchal society unless it originated a long time ago during matriarchal times. Old Hungarian spellings: 1193 leajn; 1339 Leany-, Lyan-; 1526 Lyany; 1596



60
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
leány-. JARAI Járai [ja:rai]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant of Jára 'walker'. The participle -ó supplanted the Sumero-Hungarian suffix -a when adjectives were formed from a verb. It is spelled járó in present-day Hungarian. The root jár means 'walk, go'. The Hungarian jár is of uncertain derivation. It is present in the Finno-Ugric languages, the reconstructed proto-word is *jorɜ (Benkő 1967: 261, vol. 2). But similar forms are derived from yür in the Turkic languages, cf. Turkish yürümek 'to walk, to go'. The word-final element -mek is the infinitive, often used when listing verbs in a dictionary. The Sumerian gir 'foot' may also be interpreted in the sense of 'walk' (Gostony 1975: 32). Old Hungarian spellings: 1195 iarov; 1299 -yarew; 1349 Iar-; 1385 Jaro. JARO Járó [ja:ro:]. 'Walker'. See Jarai for explanation. JASZ Jász [ja:s]. This name denotes a person from the Jász ethnic group in Hungary. Their name, origin and presence in Hungary are unclear. Some historians believe they are the descendants of the Sarmatian Jazigs who lived in Hungary around the beginning of the Christian era (Ács 1986: 87). Others presume them to be of Iranian origin and put their entry into Hungary in the 14th century. The name appears to be cognate with the Ossetian Asy, Asi (Benkő 1967: 263, vol. 1) a people of the Central Caucasus Mountains, now known as Kabardino-Balkaria. Old Hungarian spellings: 1318 Jaz-; 1335 Jaaz; 1416 iaz-; 1881 Gyász-. JATEKOS Játékos [ja:te:kosh]. 'Playful'. The name is comprised of the root ját, the nominal suffix -ék denoting the result of action and the adjectival suffix -os indicating involvement. Old Hungarian spelling was yatekos in 1546. The Finno-Ugric *jukta 'story, talk', tentatively suggested by the HistoricalEtymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, is doubtful. JAVAS Javas [javash]. A probable derivative of the Turkish yavaş 'quiet, gentle'. The Hungarian javas may denote a knowledgeable person who was sought after for advice, a kind of medicine man. JEG Jég [je:g]. 'Ice'. A cool-headed, unemotional person may have been named so. The word jég 'ice' is shared with other Finno-Ugric languages in similar forms and meanings, cf. Finnish jää 'ice'. The reconstructed proto-word is *jäηe (Benkő 1967: 268, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spellings: 1156 Jegu; 1405 ieg; 1510 jeg(-es). JEGES Jeges [jegesh]. 'Ice (delivery) man'. The adjectival suffix -es denotes an occupation. See Jeg for more information. JEKELY Jékely [je:kej]. A probable distorted form of the ethnic denomination Székely, name of the Hungarian speaking indigenous people of Transylvania. See Székely for more information. JENO Jenő [jenø:]. Contrary to popular belief the Hungarian Jenő is not derived from the Greek Eugene 'well born' but from the name of a tribe in Hungary around the turn of the first millennium AD. The name is of Turkic origin, describing a yeni 'new' tribe that



61
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
joined the already existing union of old tribes. Place names in Hungary reveal that members of the Jenő tribe have dispersed in the Carpathian Basin. Old Hungarian spelling was Jeneu in 1236 and Jenew 1461. JOBBAGY Jobbágy [jobadj]. The reduplication of b marks a stressed consonant. The word jobbágy is translated in dictionaries as 'serf' (Országh 1974: 952, vol. 1) but their status was no better than that of slaves. The jobbágys were agrarian people, devoid of rights, who worked on the estate of their landlords. They were subjected to heavy taxes and services by their landlords, the State and the Clergy. Their landlords had jurisdiction over them and they had severe restrictions in personal freedom. The landlord also had the humiliating nászjog 'the right to spend the first night with the bride of his subjects'. There are no reliable records of how and when slavery started in Hungary or what the etymology of jobbágy is. However, there is reason to believe that the Limigantes, slaves of the Sarmatians and Argaragantes, spoke the Hungarian language. Ammianus Marcellinus (c. 330-395) Roman chronicler mentions in his Rerum Gestarum that the Limigantes called Roman Emperor Constantius II (317-361) a marha 'stupid ox' (Libri XIX, Section 11, Entry 10) during an altercation by the Danube in the Fourth Century AD. This derogatory epithet is still used by present-day Hungarians. The suggestion by the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language that jobbágy is derived from jobb 'better' and denoted 'nobility' at one time (Benkő 1967: 276, vol. 2) is beyond comprehension. It does not explain the presence of the second element -ágy, which means 'bed' as an independent word, thus the compound of the two words would mean 'better bed', a term difficult to explain, especially when assigned to denote either nobility or slaves. The Hungarian jobbágy appears to be a distorted compound, derived from various linguistic sources. The following languages may be considered: Latin iugum 'slavery'; Greek opados 'servant'; Hebrew obad ( as in obadyahu 'slave of Yahweh'); Persian bande and abd 'slave'; Babylonian abed 'slave'; Arabic abd ‘servant’ (male), ubayd ‘servant of lower rank’. Old Hungarian spellings: 1229 Joubag; 1247 iobag; 1265 Jobag; 1333 Jobagy. JONAS Jónás [jo:na:sh]. The Hungarian version of Ionian. The name denotes one whom relocated from the Ionian Islands off the coast of Greece. It is comprised of Iona and the adjectival suffix -s. JOZAN Józan [jo:zan]. 'Sober'. A non-drinker may have been named so. Old Hungarian spellings: 1401 Jozan; 1602 Józon. JUHASZ Juhász [juha:s]. ‘Shepherd’. The name incorporates juh 'ewe' and the suffix -ász which denotes a specialized occupation. The Hungarian juh is phonetically close to the Sumerian u8 'ewe' (Labat 1948: No. 494). Old Hungarian spellings: 1400 Ihaz; 1423 Jwhaz; 1602 Joház. JUTASI Jutasi [jutashi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Jutas, a village in Veszprém County. The village may have received its name after Jutocsa, the third son of prince Árpád (c. 900 AD.). The personal name Jutocsa is derived from a similar Turkish name which means 'gluttonous' (Kiss 1978: 306). Cf. Turkish yutmak 'to gulp'. The suffix -mak is often used when listing verbs in a dictionary. The toponym Jutas is comprised of jut, the Hungarian spelling of the Turkish yut,



62
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
and the adjectival suffix -as to denote character. KACSAK. Kacsák [kacha:k]. Derived from the Turkish kaçak 'fugitive'. KACSOH. Kacsóh [kacho:]. Variant of kacsó 'little hand'. A probable derivation from kacs 'tendril' to which the participle -ó is suffixed. Like a tendril, a child's hand grabs and holds on to everything it can touch, thus the analogy. A person having small hands may have been named so. A probable source is the Akkadian qâtu 'hand, palm' (Labat 1948: No. 126). Old Hungarian spelling: 1459 Kaczo; 1750 kacsó-. KADAR. Kádár [ka:da:r]. The name has two possible meanings. Kádár means 'cooper' in present-day Hungarian, comprised of kád 'tub' and the nominal suffix -ár. The word kád is present in languages other than Hungarian, cf. Greek kados 'vessel', German kad 'tank, cistern', Latin cadus 'large earthen vessel'. It is also present in the Slavonic languages in the form of kad, kada, kadi.
However the ultimate source may be the Sumerian kàd 'to tie, to attach'. The staves of a wooden vessel are bound together by hoops and the person who had the skill to bind them would have been called kádár. The German Faβbinder ‘cooper, (tubbinder)’ is also derived from Binde ‘band, tie’. Another equally possible source is the Hun-Scythian kadar 'Lord Chief Justice', which became a surname (Benkő 1967: 301, vol. 2). Compare with Turkish kadi 'Moslem judge' (Akdikmen 1986: 206). Old Hungarian spelling: 1142 Cadar; 1282 Kadar. KAJTAR. Kajtár [kajta:r]. 'Rummager'. The name is comprised of kajt 'rummage' and the nominal suffix -ár indicating habitual activity. Old Hungarian spelling: 1346 Kaythar; 1429 Kaytor. KAKUSZI. Kakuszi [kakusi]. Also spelled Kakuszy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Kakusz. The name may be derived from the Turkish kokuz, Osmanli kokoz 'penniless, destitute' (Clauson 1972: 614). KALDOR. Káldor [ka:ldor]. A probable derivation from the Greek kaletor 'messenger, emissary'. KALLO. Kalló [kalo:]. The name contains the root kall 'hit, beat' and the participle -ó. It denotes a 'fulling mill' or a 'fuller'. The more precise term for a ‘fuller’ is kallós. Old Hungarian spelling: 1237 Colov; 1295 Kallov; 1332 Kollo; 1673 kallo; 1809 Kallós. KALOCSA. Kalocsa [kalocha]. The phrase kalócsa denotes a 'wooden brace', shaped like
ʌ, placed on top of thatched roofs to hold in place the straw or rushes. This phrase is used among the Hungarian speaking Székelys of Transylvania (B. Lőrinczy 1992: 49, vol. 3). A roofer specializing in such braces may have been named so. The initial element kal may be of Sumerian origin meaning 'strong' (Labat 1948: No. 322), which is erős in the Hungarian language. The function of a brace is to fasten or secure erősít in Hungarian. In kalocsa the act of fastening or securing is represented by kal.
KAMPOS. Kampós [kampo:sh]. A descriptive name for a person who uses a walkingstick for support. From the Hungarian kampó 'a hook, a bend' to which the adjectival suffix -s is attached to denote possession of an implement. The Greek kampe 'hook, bend' is identical with the Hungarian word, but the most probable origin is the Sumerian gam



63
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
'to bend, bent' (Labat 1948: No. 362). Old Hungarian spelling: 1643 kajmó-; 1818 kampu, gampó, kompó. KAPALO. Kapáló [kapa:lo:]. There are two words in the Hungarian language to denote a 'hoer' kapás and kapáló. Both are derived from kapa 'hoe'. This agricultural tool is used for scraping the earth in a shallow depth to clear it from weeds. Though, similar words exist in the Slavonic languages, like kopat, kopati 'to scrape, to hoe', the most likely source would be the Akkadian kâparu 'to clear' (Labat 1948: No. 225). The Hungarian kapar 'scrape' appears to be cognate with the Akkadian word. Old Hungarian spelling: 1638 kapallo; 1700 kapáló. KAPOS. kapós [kapo:sh]. The name denotes a popular person, sought after for his/her skills. A respected member of society (Bárczi-Országh 1960: 743, vol. 3). The name incorporates the root kap 'grab, obtain' and the adjectival suffix -ós to denote character. The Turkish kapiş 'snatch' may be cognate with the Hungarian kap. KARIKAS. Karikás [karika:sh]. A bow-legged person may have received such a name. The name is derived from karika 'circular, round' to which the adjectival suffix -s is added to indicate condition. KARPATI Kárpáti [ka:rpa:ti]. 'A dweller in the Carpathian mountains'. The name is comprised of the Sumerian kur 'mountain' (Labat 1948: No. 346), pát a rare Middle Babylonian phonetic value of the Sumerian be that means 'to extend, to stretch out' (Labat 1948: No. 69) and the derivational suffix -i. The Hungarian name for the mountain range is Kárpátok, the word-final element -ok is the plural number. The surname's verbatim translation is 'a dweller in the mountains that stretch out'. KAZI Kazi [kazi]. There are several possibilities for the origin of this name, but none of them are from the Hungarian language. The Persian qazi ‘judge’ which is derived from the Arabic language (Lambton 1988: 118). Then in the Turkic Chagatai language kaz means ‘to wander’ to which the derivational suffix –i is attached, denoting the descendant of a wanderer. The Estonian käzi ‘hand’ may also be considered, c. f. Hungarian kéz ‘hand. KEMENY Kemény [keme:nj]. "Hard, firm'. The name suggests a reserved, strict individual, one that can be trusted. The root of the name kem- is from the Finno-Ugric *kämä. Cf. Finnish kämä 'rigid, unbending', Samoyed and Selkup? koom 'hard' (Benkő 1967: 437, vol. 2). The word-final element -ny is the substantive suffix, indicating an instrument of action. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Kemend; 1267 Kemyn; 1334 Kemeyn; 1448 kemen; 1560 kemeny. KERO. Kérő. [ke:rø:]. The closest translation of this word is 'best man' of the groom. One of the duties of the kérő in rural Hungary was to ask for the hand of the bride-elect. The groom-to-be kept a low profile during this process. The best man arranged and organized the wedding; he was the go between the parents (Ortutay 1980: 543, vol. 3). This practice tapered out after WW II. The name is a composite of the verb kér 'ask, request' and the participle -ő to form an adjective that indicates action. The equivalents of the Magyar kér are present in other Finno-Ugric languages cf. Votyak kur-, kural-, 'ask', Zyryan korni 'ask' (Benkő 1967: 452, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Kere; 1350 Querie; 1395 kerew.



64
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
KILLIAN Killián [kilia:n]. The name originates in the Celtic languages pronounced [killee-un] meaning ‘a churchman’. Also spelled Cillian, it is derived from ceall meaning ‘church’ (McLaughlin 2011: 206, 213). KO. Kő [kø:]. 'Stone'. Name of a dweller by a prominent stone landmark or quarry, in which case he could be a stonecutter named after the material he works with. Another form of kő is kű in a regional dialect. This form is identical with the Sumerian ku 'rock' (Gostony 1975: 59). Similar words are in other Finno-Ugric languages, such as Vogul kü, Zyryan ki, Votyak ko, Finn kivi (Benkő 1967: 601, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1009 Kw; 1055 ku-; 1067 Kuu; 1211 Keuue; 1777 kü. KOLOK. Kölök [køløk]. A regional variant of kölyök 'kid, brat'. Originally denoted the young of an animal while being nurtured by the female. The reason for such surname is obscure, so is its etymology. The most probable origin is among the Turkic languages where the hypothetical reconstructed old Chuwash form is *kölik (Benkő 1967: 608, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1165? chulches; 1219 Culqed; 1221 Kewlked; 1383 Kelked; 1395 kelk; 1530 kelek; 1808 Kölyög, külök. KOMLODI Komlódi [komlo:di]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Komlód, a village in Szatmár County of historical Hungary, confined to Romania in 1920 by the Trianon Peace Treaty and the spelling was romanianized to Comlod. The place name is comprised of komló 'hop plant and its bitter cones', used for flavoring beer. The suffix -d is a grammatical designator denoting a place. The name may have been given to a place abundant in hops. Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 Qumlou-; 1208 Komlou-; 1240 Cumlo-; 1363 Komlo-. The word komló is of old Turkish origin according to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language. It is present in various forms in the Turkic and Finno-Ugric languages, but the explanation is concluded with a contradiction: The Turkish derivation is hindered by phonetic discrepancies (Benkő 1967: 573, vol. 2). Another concept is that the Turkish kunlak 'hop plant' is a German loan word from an [unnamed] verb that means 'to creep'. Variants can be found in many Germanic, Scandinavian, Slavonic and Finno-Ugric languages. However, the earliest recorded form was the Latin humlo, humulo during the 8th and 9th Century AD (Clauson 1972: 628). The Hungarian form Kumolou in 1264 suggests Latin influence. KONYA. Kónya [konja]. A ‘drooping, bent’ person may have been named so. The Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a is added to the root kony which may be cognate with the Sumerian kun4 and kun5 which were translated by René Labat as 'stairs' (Labat 1948: No. 142, 144). A flight of stairs is a surface repeatedly bending at sharp angles to permit ascent or descent from one level to another. The bending of the steps must have been recognized as being the significant feature of their construction if the Sumerian word is compared with the Hungarian köny(ök) 'elbow', and Kany-(ar) ‘curve’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1333 Kona; 1339 Konya; 1679 kónya. KOROM. Korom [korom]. 'Soot'. Either a 'stoker' or a 'chimney sweep' was named so. The word korom is of Turkish origin, cf. Osmanli kurum 'soot' (Akdikmen 1986: 247).



65
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Corom; 1338 Korom-; 1340 Korum; 1405 kurom. KOTELES. Köteles [køtelesh]. 'Rope-maker'. The name is comprised of kötél 'rope' and the adjectival suffix -es to indicate an occupation. The word kötél is derived from köt 'bind, tie' and cognate with other Finno-Ugric words such as the Vogul köt, Finn kytkeä. But the ultimate source may be the Sumerian kád 'to tie, to twist' (Labat 1948: No. 63). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Kuteles; 1456 keteles; 1495 köteles. KOVACS. Kovács [kova:ch]. Also spelled Kovác, Kovách and Kovack. An occupational name, kovács 'blacksmith' may be a relic from the Stone Age. The Hungarian version of the name is comprised of ko, the intrusive v and ács. The initial element ko (kő and kű means 'stone, rock' in present-day Hungarian) is the root of kova 'flint', the material used for making stone implements in the Stone Age. A person involved in such activity would have been called kova ács. The word ács expresses a process which results in a roughhew, like the work of a blacksmith as compared to a goldsmith. As an independent word ács now means 'carpenter' in modern Hungarian, an indication that the work does not require top-grade workmanship. See Acs for etymology. Kova-ács was then compounded into kovács, preserving the name of the original rough method used for stone tools. Old Hungarian spelling: 1075 kouzch-; 1193 coach; 1230 Cuach; 1380 cowach; 1444 Kouacz; 1453 Kowach. The difference between stone, ore and metal may not have been obvious to early men for they all have similar consistencies. While working with rocks containing veins of metal, the discovery was made that part of the rock can be hammered into a desired shape. Thus the material changed, but the name of the process remained the same: kovács.
The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, published during the Soviet Era, identifies the Slavonic languages as the source of kovács, without explaining what each segment means in those languages. The presence of this word in those languages does not automatically point to their origin. The Slavonic form is identical with the Hungarian kovács and means 'blacksmith', but similar forms and identical meanings are present in the Gaelic languages as well. Cf. Scottish gobha, old Irish goba, old Welsh gob, Cornish gof, Breton go and Gaulish gobân (MacBain 1982). Therefore a common source for the Hungarian, Slavonic and Gaelic 'smith' should be sought for. The letters g and k are regarded transposable. Cowan 'blacksmith' is a Scottish surname (Sims MCMLXIX: 32), the form Gowan is present in McGowan 'son of the smith (Cottle 1978: 245). The Hungarian kű was present in the Sumerian language in the perceived phonetic form kù. This word preceded the names of hard, stone-like materials in their language, like kù-an 'meteorite iron' (literally 'rock from heaven') or kù-babar 'silver' (Labat 1948: No. 468). The Sumerian ku is associated both with 'rock' and 'metal' by Anton Deimel (Gostony 1975: 139), which clearly indicates the confusion about rock, ore and metal in antiquity. KOVER. Kövér [køve:r]. A 'fat' person may have been named so. The Hungarian kövér 'fat' is not present in any other language, except perhaps in Sumerian where kur4 means 'to be fat, heavy' (Deimel 1939: No 293). Old Hungarian spelling: 1231 Chuer; 1395 keuer; 1405 kiue[r]; 1519 küér.



66
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
KOZAK Kozák [koza:k]. The Hungarian Kozák corresponds to the English Cossack. The name belongs to one of the many tribes of Altaic descent who in antiquity occupied the vast territory from the Black Sea to the Aral Sea. They adapted to the Slavonic languages after becoming a special military caste of the Czarist regime in Imperial Russia. The earliest written record of their name in Hungary is in 1530 spelled kozák. There are many intriguing explanations and according to one of them the term kozák may have originated from a Tatar word meaning 'freeman' (Barnhart 1954: 1099). Alternatively, the name may not have any ethnic significance at all, but have its origin in a tenth century Arabic word denoting a 'freebooter' or 'nomad soldier' (Longworth 1970: 344). However, most authorities agree that it is derived from the old Turkic root gaz 'to wander', which forms the primary element in the Turcoman gäzäk 'wanderer, nomad' (Németh 1991: 94), in modern Turkish gezgin 'wandering', gezen 'rover' (Akdikmen 1986: 146, 455), and in the Chagatai kaz 'to wander' (Németh 1990: 76). Though there is a tendency to avoid drawing a parallel between Kozák and Kazakh, the phonetic similarities can hardly be ignored. Both groups belonged to the same Turkic-speaking people before the Cossacks were Slavonicized. The Turkish language does not differentiate between Kazakh and Cossack calling both Kazak (Akdikmen 1986: 225). KUN Kun [kun]. The Hungarian name for a 'Cumanian', a people of Turkic origin who first invaded Hungary in the 11th Century, but after repeated defeats they sought permission to settle permanently in the country. They completely assimilated with Hungarians within 200 years. Old Hungarian spelling: 1297 Kun; 1331 Kwn. There are two schools of thought about the meaning of Kun, also spelled Kuhn, Kún and Kuun. The prevailing theory is that it originates in the Turkic qu 'pale, yellowish' (Benkő 1967: 667, vol. 2), describing their complexion. An interesting alternative to the color of skin theory is that Cumanians were simply called 'man' in cognate Altaic languages such as kümün in Mongolian. Similarities can also be found in the Uralic languages, for example the Samoyed kum and Komi-Zyrian komi (Németh 1986: 225). KUTI. Kuti [kuti]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a dweller by the kút 'water well'. Similar words are in other agglutinative languages, cf. Estonian kau 'spring, well', Turkish kuyu 'well, pit’. Old Hungarian spellings: 1055 cut, kut; 1206 cuth; 1242 kut; 1277 kuuth; 1487 kwth; 1638 kut. LABDY. Lábdy [la:bdi]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from Lábod, a village in Somogy County. The letter o is omitted by syncopation. The root láb 'foot' is an ancient Magyar word not present in any other language in this form. The place name is given for no obvious reason. Surnames derived from láb were numerous during the Middle Ages in Hungary. For example Aranylábú 'golden feet' (spelled Aranlabov in 1342), Sárlábú 'muddy feet' (spelled Sarlabo in 1465), széplábú 'beautiful feet' (spelled Zeplabo in 1509) (Szamota 1902-1906: 565). LABUDA. Labuda [labuda]. The most probable origin of this name is in ancient Mesopotamia. It was a title and can be segmented into la-bud-a then etymologized with the help of the Sumerian language. The initial element la may be the distorted form of lu



67
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
'man, male' (Labat 1948: No. 330), bud is a variant of bad 'bat, scepter, stave' (Waddell 1927: 23), forerunner of the Hungarian bot 'bat'. The word-final element -a is the adjectival suffix still active in medieval Hungary. Meaning of the compound name is 'man with a scepter', i.e. a governor. The title survived in Semitic Akkadian language in the form of labuttu 'governor' (Labat 1948: No. 75). Byzantine sources mention a boebodos 'governor' by the name of Lebedias, the leader of a tribe called sabartoi asphaloi in Asia (Moravcsik 1988: 42). The name, without the Greek ending would be Lebedi, a close resemblance to Labuda. The present-day Buda in Hungary was also a title in antiquity and is derived from the Sumerian bad (Nogrady 1996: 52-55). The governor’s title boebodos may be the distorted spelling of the Hungarian főboda ‘head buda’. See Buda, Boda and Bodo for more detail. LAMOS. Lamos [lamosh]. The name describes a 'hairy' individual. Lam 'hair' is an obsolete Magyar word, not part of present-day vocabulary in that sense. The adjectival suffix -os indicates condition (Benkő 1967: 713, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1324 Lamus; 1585 Lamos. LASZLO. László [la:slo:]. A hybrid name, most likely comprised of the archaic Magyar lac 'outdoors, exterior' and the Sumerian lu 'man' (Labat 1948: No. 330). It denotes an 'outdoors man'. Minor changes in spelling took place over the years from affricate c to fricative sz and from u to ó. Other words in which the element lac survived are lacibetyár 'felon from the wilderness', lacikonyha 'camp kitchen', lacipecsenye 'meat prepared outdoors on a spit'. The Slavonic source Vladislav, favored by Hungarian onomasticians of the Soviet era, is not convincing. Laci ‘from the outdoors’ is the endearing form of László.
Old Hungarian spelling: Lácló; 1256 -lazlo; 1404 Lazlo-; 1546 Laczlo. LEGENY. Legény [lege:nj]. 'Bachelor'. A name of probable Sumerian origin, the compound of lu 'man, male' (Labat 1948: No. 330) and gin 'to come and go, to move and bustle about' (Labat 1948: No. 206). The attribute lugin > legény describes the characteristics of a bachelor. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 -legen; 1152 legun-; 1245 -legen; 1819 legin, leginy. LEHI. Lehi [lehi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Leh, a village in Borsod-Abaúj-Zemplén County, that preserved the name of its founder. Leh is the root of lehel 'exhale', probably bestowed on a person who exhaled in blow-like puffs. Old Hungarian spelling: 1282 Leh; LEHOTAY. Lehotay [lehotai]. The archaic derivational suffix -y signifies a person from a Lehota, a Slovak word meaning 'privilege'. Settlements in the Northern Highlands of historical Hungary (present-day Slovakia), where Slovak settlers received complete freedom from serfage in addition to other privileges, were named Lehota (Németh 1991: 205). LENGYEL Lengyel [lendjel]. 'Polish'. The origin of the name is attributed by some to the Old Russian *lędo 'clearing, virgin soil', but its one-time written existence can only be reconstructed through Byzantine and Arab records (Benkő 1967: 751, vol. 1). A more probable source would be the aphetic form of Polander, after the loss of



68
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
the initial syllable Po which means 'near, beside' in the Slavonic languages. The remaining Lander, after undergoing changes to accommodate the phonetic structure of the Hungarian language, would result in the form Lengyel. Old Hungarian spelling: 1130 Lengen. LEVAI. Lévai [le:vai]. Also spelled Lévay. The name denotes a person from Léva in Trencsén County of historical Hungary, confined to Slovakia in 1920 and renamed Levice. The word-final element -i (archaic -y) is the derivational suffix, indicating origin. The rest of the name is comprised of the root lev and the archaic adjectival suffix a and means 'being, existing'. Its present-day form is lévő. The city probably preserved the name of its founder. Old Hungarian spelling: 1156 Leua; 1350 lev; 1693 livő. LIBA. Liba [liba]. 'Goose'. A goose farmer may have been named so. Liba is an old obscure Hungarian word that contains the root lib and the archaic adjectival suffix -a. Old Hungarian spelling: 1565 liba-; 1833 Riba, libó; luba. LIGETI. Ligeti [ligeti]. Also spelled Ligetti. The derivational suffix -i indicate a dweller by the liget 'grove'. The word liget is comprised of the root lig and the formative -et. Old Hungarian spelling: 1121 -legeth; 1206 ligoth; 1208 liget; 1565 lügget. LIKO. Likó [liko:]. Derived from lik, a regional variant of lyuk 'hole', the name describes one who cuts the rows of holes in the ground with a hoe in which seeds are placed, according to lexical information (B. Lőrinczy 1992: 864, 929, vol. 3). The participle -ó forms action nouns. This occupational name originated in an agricultural society and was first recorded in the southern region of the Carpathian Basin during the 1st Century AD as Licco (Mócsi 1975: 64). LISZT. Liszt [liszt]. An occupation that took its name from the product. The Hungarian liszt 'flour' is most likely derived from the Sumerian liš 'fragment, morsel' . The Middle Babylonian form was lis (Labat 1948: No. 377). Flour is the finely fragmented (milled) kernels of wheat. Cf. Finnish leseet 'bran' and lestyjauhot 'fine flour' (Papp 1978: 356). Old Hungarian spelling: 1279 -lyst; 1415 Lyzth; 1597 lizt; 1646 liszt-. LOKE. Lőke [lø:ke]. A relic from the distant past, a name given for no obvious reason. Its most probable origin is the Sumerian lah 'to shove, to push' (Labat 1948: No. 206a). The word-final element -e is the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a, subjected to vowel harmony. Similar words are in other Finno-Ugric languages, cf. Finnish lykätä 'push', Estonian lükama 'push' (Benkő 1967: 794, vol. 2). LOMBOS. Lombos [lombosh]. Derived from lomb 'foliage', the name may owe its origin to a residence surrounded and concealed by vegetation of dense foliage. The adjectival suffix -os indicates condition. Old Hungarian spelling: 1213 Lomb; 1577 lomb. LOVAGI. Lovagi [lovagi]. Descendant of a lovag 'knight' as marked by the derivational suffix -i. The word lovag is derived from ló 'horse' to which the adjectival suffix -ag is attached. The intermittent v is a connecting consonant. Old Hungarian spelling: 1306 Lwag; 1395 louag; 1414 Lowagh; 1484 Lovag. LOVASZ. Lovász [lova:s]. 'Horse-groom, stableman'. The suffix -ász is a formative that marks specialized occupations. See Lovagi for more detail. Old Hungarian spelling: 1055 luazu; 1193 Loaz; 1233 Luaz; 1236 Lowaz; 1544 louasz-; 1546 lowasz; 1674 Lovász.



69
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
LUDAS. Ludas [ludash]. The name is comprised of lúd ‘goose' and the adjectival suffix as. It denotes a farmer engaged in the raising and marketing of geese. It also denotes a ‘goose-herd’. Also, a dubious person is called ludas in rural Hungary. Words similar to lúd are in other Finno-Ugric languages, cf. Vogul lont 'goose', lunt 'wild goose', Finnish lintu 'bird', Estonian lind 'bird' (Benkő 1967: 797, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Ludos; 1329 Ludas; 1438 Ludas; 1731 Ludas. LUKAS. Lukas [lukash]. There are two possible origins for this name. One is the altered form of the Latin Lucas derived from luceo 'light, to shine'. But the generally accepted Hungarian version of the Latin word, also a surname, is Lukács. Another possibility is that the name denotes a dweller in a hollow, as luk is a variant of lyuk 'hole'. The adjectival suffix -as would certainly indicate such derivation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1231 Lycos-; 1297 lwkas-; 1307 Lykas; 1329 Lyukas-; 1462 Lykas-; 1521 lukas. LUKO. Lükő [lykø:]. 'Javelin-thrower, slinger (Szamota 1902-1906:598)'. The name contains the root-word lük a variant of lök 'toss, thrust' and the participle -ő which transforms verbs into action nouns. See Loke for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Lukeu; 1436 Lykew; 1504 Lywkew. MACS Mács [ma:ch]. The name has no meaning in present-day Hungarian. It may be the variant of the Sumerian maš 'lamb' (Labat 1048: No. 76). A shepherd or the owner of a heard of sheep may have received the name. Macs is also a place name near Debrecen and were probably given to honor the shepherds and their sheep that lived there. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Moc; 1214 Moch. MACSAI Mácsai [ma:chai]. The derivational suffix -i signifies a descendant of Mácsa. The Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a denotes occupation which is attached to the root macs to denote a ‘shepherd’. See Macs for etymology. MACSO Macsó [macho:]. The participle -ó indicates 'one associated with lambs'. See Macs for etymology. MADA Mada [mada]. The name has no meaning in modern Hungarian. It may be the unchanged form of the Sumerian ma-da 'land, country, region' (Labat 1948: No. 342). The Hungarian equivalent of the Sumerian ma-da is ország and it is also used as a surname. Mada may denote a member of the indigenous people in the Carpathian Basin. MADACH Madách [mada:ch]. The suffix -ch is an altered form of the adjectival suffix -s [-sh] which indicates status. The name appears to denote a landowner. See Mada for etymology. MADACHI Madáchi [mada:chi]. Also spelled Madáchy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) signifies a descendant of Madách the 'landowner'. See Mada and Madách for etymology. MADAI Madai [madai]. Also spelled Maday. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) denotes a resident of the country, i.e. a dweller in the land. See Mada for etymology. MADAS Madas [madash]. The adjectival suffix -s is attached to the Sumerian ma-da 'land, country' to indicate a landowner. The present-day equivalent of Madas is the surname Földes. Föld means 'land' in the current Hungarian language. MADAR Madár [mada:r]. 'Fowler'. An occupational name. The Hungarian madár 'bird' may be derived from the Sumerian mu-ud 'bird' (Deimel 1939: page 10).



70
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Modor-; 1360 Madar-; 1602 Madár. MADARAS Madaras [madarash]. Also spelled Madaros, Madarass. The adjectival suffix -as indicates an occupation. The name denotes a person who specialized in breeding birds of any kind, including barnyard and game birds. See Madar for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Modoros; 1337 Madaras-.
MADARASI Madarasi [madarashi]. Also spelled Madarasy, Madarassy, Madarassi. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Madaras. See Madar and Madaras for etymology. MADARASZ Madarász [madara:s]. 'One who traps wild birds'. The name is comprised of madár 'bird' and the formative suffix -ász which converts nouns into names of specialized occupations. See Madar for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1379 Madaraz; 1520 Madaraz; 1522 Madarazs. MAGAS Magas [magash]. The adjectival suffix -as indicates a tall person when attached to the root mag 'tall'. The word mag may be the Hungarian variant of the Sumerian mah 'high, eminent' (Labat 1948: No. 57). Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 mogos; 1211 Mogus; 1240 Mocus; 1370 -magas. MAGYAR Magyar [madjar]. Hungarians call themselves Magyar, but the name cannot be etymologized in their current language. Also spelled Megyer, the name denotes the original inhabitants of the Carpathian Basin (Nogrady 1994: 33-35). Their name received gradual recognition after more than a thousand years of subjugation by Sarmatian, Roman, Hun, Avar, Turkish and Austrian rulers. Other nations call them Hungars, the name of their Turkish speaking Hun-gar ruling class. During that period of time only the native serfs, who vastly outnumbered their foreign rulers, called themselves Magyar. The name is most likely derived from the Sumerian language. The phoneme gy in magy is the palatal counterpart of the alveolar d in the Sumerian ma-da 'land, country, region' (Labat 1948: No. 342). By adding the final syllable er 'servant' (Labat 1948: No. 50), the word ma-da-er is obtained that would correspond to a present-day Hungarian spelling ma-gya-er, meaning 'servant of the land'. The name was then shortened to either Magyar or Megyer, depending on regional dialects. Old Hungarian spelling: 1121 Mogioroi; 1150 -moger; 1200 Mogerij; 1225 Mogor; 1230 Mogior; 1275 Magyar; 1283 Mogyor; 1286 Magor; 1323 Maghor; 1329 Magar; 1333 Magyar. MALO Maló [malo:]. The name may have been bestowed on a person associated with a malom 'flour-mill'. The word-final element -ó is the participle denoting an occupation when attached to a verb. The name is probably derived from the Sumerian ma5 'to mill, to grind' (Labat 1948: No. 33). The l preceding the participle -ó is a connecting consonant. Therefore maló may be the original form of the present-day malom 'mill' and/or molnár 'miller'. Similar words exist in many Indo-European languages which are derived from the Latin molo 'to grind' however, its etymology is obscure. The Hungarian máll 'crumble, disintegrate' may also be associated with the Sumerian and Indo-European terms. MATAI Matai [matai]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant of Mata, which cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. It appears to be a derivation either from the Sumerian ma-da 'land, country, region' (Labat 1948: No. 342),



71
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
or its Akkadian counterpart mâtu which means the same thing. Mata, an altered form of mâtu can be turned into a surname the same as ma-da and both of their meanings compare well with the present-day Hungarian surname Ország 'land, country'. MATOS Matos [matosh]. This name appears to be a more recent variant of Mata. The ancient Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a was replaced by the present-day -os. The name suggests a 'landowner'. MERAI Mérai [me:rai]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from the town of Méra. The toponym, first recorded as Mera in 1256 in Abaúj County (Györffy 1987: 118, vol. 1), was originally the surname of Lord Mera. This surname cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. After separating the Sumero-Hungarian suffix –a from the root of the name, the stem mer or mér remains. The Sumerian mer 'passion, anger' (Labat 1948: No. 347) is also the root word of the Hungarian mérges 'angry'. The archaic adjectival suffix –a was replaced by the present-day -es. Mérges is also a surname in Hungary. The name Mérai therefore denotes the 'descendant of a passionate, hot tempered' person. See Mérges for additional detail. MERGES Mérges [me:rgesh]. An ‘angry, hot tempered’ person is called mérges in Hungary. The name is derived from méreg 'anger, poison' to which the adjectival suffix es is added to indicate one's disposition. The word méreg is of uncertain origin, similar words are in both the Finno-Ugric and Indo-European languages. For example Finn myrrky 'poison', Estonian mürk 'poisonous plant', Indo-Iranian (Ossetic) marg 'poison', Persian marg 'death' (Benkő 1967: 899, vol. 2). However, the much older Sumerian mer 'anger' (Labat 1948: No. 347) is the most probable source of the Hungarian Mérges. See Merai for additional detail. MISKA Miska [mishka]. A probable combination of the Sumerian mí 'female' (Labat 1948: No. 554) and ša5 'to cut' (Labat 1948: No. 97). Misa [misha] may be the original form of Miska that forms the basis of the Hungarian miskárol 'to spay', a process applied to female animals to incapacitate them for producing young. The name denotes the action described by the Sumerian mí-ša5. Misa is now the Hungarian endearing form of Miska. The Slavonic miska 'cup' or miška 'mouse' are unlikely sources. Old Hungarian spelling: 1213 Misca; 1215 Mischa; 1219 Miske; 1221 Myska, Miska. MOLNAR Molnár [molna:r]. 'Miller'. Similar words, having similar meanings as the Hungarian molnár, exist in other languages, cf. Slavonic mlinar, malinar, German Müller, French meunier, Latin molitor. However, the Hungarian molnár appears to be derived from the Sumerian mù, ma5 'to mill, to grind' (Labat 1948: No. 33). See Malo for related information. Old Hungarian spelling: 1330 Molnar; 1403 Monar; 1427 Molnaar; 1632 molnar. MOSO Mosó [mosho:]. 'Launderer'. The participle -ó is attached to mos 'to wash' to indicate a person whose chores include doing the laundry and other related work. The word mos was present in the Akkadian language in the form of mešu 'to wash' (Labat 1948: No. 321). The Sumerian phonetic value of the cuneiform sign for the



72
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Akkadian mešu is luh, which is almost identical with the Hungarian lub, the root of luboz 'whirl or swing in water' and lubickol 'splash'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1269 Musou -; 1323 Mosou -; 1340 Muso; 1487 Moso. MOSOLYGO Mosolygó [moshojgo:]. 'Smiling, jovial'. The participle -ó is affixed to mosolyog 'smile' to form an adjective. The -og segment is the frequentative suffix which is preceded by the nominal suffix -oly. The root mos may be derived from the Sumerian múš 'facial expression' (Labat 1948: No. 102). The root mos is also present in the Uralic languages cf. Vogul mås- 'smile' (Benkő 1967: 294, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1416 Mosolgo. MURAI Murai [murai]. Also spelled Muray. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a dweller by the river Mura. This river flows in between two major watercourses, the Rába and the Dráva. The word-final element -a is most likely the Sumerian and old Hungarian adjectival suffix. Many Hungarian river names end in -a cf. Duna, Tisza, Rábca, Lajta, Száva. Others end in -os which is the present-day adjectival suffix, cf. Szamos, Maros, Kőrös, Kapos. The root mur is a variant of mar ‘corrode, eat away’ present in another river name Maros. It denotes a swift flowing river that erodes the shoreline. The reconstructed Finno-Ugric proto-word is *mura(-) or *murɜ- (Benkő 1967: 841, vol. 2). The speculation that the name may originate from the Lombard mor 'swamp' (Kiss 1978: 435) (cf. German Morast) is not convincing. The misidentification and naming a river as a swamp would constitute a major unawareness of nature, very unlikely of early men who were environmentally well informed. MURAKOZI Muraközi [murakøzi]. Also spelled Muraközy. The name contains the following elements: Mura (see above), köz 'community' and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). The name denotes a person from the community by the river Mura. NACSA Nacsa [nacha]. A relic from the distant past when the Sarmatians of Iranian origin ruled part of the Carpathian Basin c. AD 200-300. Nacsa may be derived from the East Iranian naša 'settlement, camp' (Németh 1986: VI, in foreword by János Harmatta). The name may denote a new settler. A servant named Nasa [nasha] was recorded in 1138 by the provost of Dömös in Komárom County (Fehértói 1983: 247). NAD Nád [na:d]. 'Reed'. A dweller by a reedy marsh may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1282 Nad-; 1482 Nadh-; 1697 Nád. NADAI Nádai [na:dai] Also spelled Náday. The name is comprised of nád 'reed', the archaic Hungarian adjectival suffix -a (same as the Sumerian -a) and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). It denotes the descendant of a dweller by a reedy marsh. NADANYI Nadányi [nada:nji]. A person from Nadány was named so as indicated by the derivational suffix -i. The name of present-day Kőrösladány in Békés County was called Nadan in 1222. This name appears to be of Iranian origin, probably given by the Sarmatians and slightly modified by the indigenous Magyars. The word nadan means 'ignorant' in the Persian language (Lambton 1988: 164). NADAS Nádas [na:dash]. A trade name denoting a reed-worker such as maker of reed roofs or a seller of bundled reed. The adjectival suffix -as indicates occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1259 Nados-; 1269 Nadas. NADASDI Nádasdi [na:dashdi]. Also spelled Nádasdy. The derivational suffix -i



73
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
(archaic -y) indicates a person from Nádasd a place in Vas County. The -d ending in Hungarian toponyms marks a place. The rest of the name is comprised of nád 'reed' and the adjectival suffix -as to indicate the prevailing vegetation in the area. Old Hungarian spelling: 1223 Nadost; 1244 Nadasd. NADOR Nádor [na:dor]. 'Viceroy'. There are three potential origins for this name, all of them from the area of ancient Mesopotamia. 1. Translation of the Sumerian title en-gi (en 'lord' gi 'reed') 'lord of the reeds', Hungarian nád 'reed' and úr 'lord'. 2. A variant of the Sumerian nigir 'steward of the palace' (Labat 1948: No. 348). 3. Derivation from the Persian nazer 'overseer, bailiff' which is a loan word from Arabic (Lambton 1988: 165). Old Hungarian spelling: 1504 Nadr-; 1554 nador-. NADORFI Nádorfi [na:dorfi]. The patronymic suffix -fi indicates a male descendant of Nádor. NADORI Nádori [na:dori]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant of Nádor. NAGY Nagy [nadj]. The Hungarian nagy 'large, big, important' may be either derived from the Sumerian [nu]g 'large, big' (Labat 1948: No. 72) or the also Sumerian na 'important' (Deimel 1939: No. 45). Old Hungarian spelling: 1055 nogu; 1199 Nogy; 1272 Nog-; 1326 Nogh; 1421 Nag; 1478 Nagh; 1448 nagy. NAGYFALUSI Nagyfalusi [nadjfalushi]. The name denotes a person from a 'big village'. It is comprised of nagy 'big', falu 'village', the adjectival suffix -s and the derivational suffix -i. See Nagy for etymology. The word falu is present in languages of the Uralic language family. The reconstructed Finno-Ugric proto-word is *palyɜ (Benkő 1967: 836, vol. 1). However, the Hungarian falu may have evolved from the Sumerian uru 'village' pronounced alu during the Neo-Babylonian period (Labat 1967: No. 38). NAGYFURO Nagyfúró [nadjfu:ro:]. 'Great borer'. The name distinguishes a skilled maker of holes from an average one. It contains the word nagy 'great', fúr 'to bore, to drill' and the participle -ó which forms adjectives from verbs to mark an occupation. See Nagy for etymology. The Hungarian fúr may be derived from the Sumerian bùr 'to bore, to drill' (Labat 1948: No. 411). A sound change from b to f is a recorded occurrence in the Hungarian language, cf. bodor > fodor 'curl, frill'. NAGYGYOR Nagygyőr [nadjdjø:r]. A difficult name to etymologize. The first segment nagy means 'big, great', see Nagy for etymology. The second segment Győr is the name of a city known for metallifacture and is also part of Diósgyőr, the name of another city having metallurgic industries. There is a strong possibility that the Hungarian győr is cognate with the also Hungarian gyűr, gyúr 'mould, knead' and is related to the Sumerian gur. Gur, when reduplicated as gur-gur, means 'craftsman who works with metal' (Labat 1948: No. 111) clearly shows relationship with metallurgy. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language indicates the correlation between gyűr and gyúr (Benkő 1967: 1140, vol. 1). The phonetic difference is almost negligible between győr and gyűr. Albeit no known written record of the Hungarian vasgyúró 'iron-moulder' exist in the sense of 'metal-worker' or 'smith', it has been recorded in 1429 as 'strong, muscular



74
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
man' (Benkő 1967: 1096, vol. 3). Back then the strongest man in a community was the black-smith by virtue of his work and he did bend, mould and hammer metals into desired shapes. Therefore we cannot be indifferent to the possibility that the original meaning of vasgyúró was 'black-smith'. Taking all this into consideration the most probable meaning of Nagygyőr is 'prominent metal worker'. Old Hungarian spelling of Győr: 1213 Jewr; 1221 Geur. NAMATH Namath [ne:met]. Anglicized form of Németh 'German'. See below for etymology. NANAI Nánai [na:nai]. Also spelled Nánay. A person from the village of Nána would be called so as indicated by the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). The village is in the former Hungarian Northern Highland, confined to Slovakia in 1920. The element -nána is part of toponyms in present-day Hungary cf. Alsónána, Bakonynána, Felsőnána, Kisnána, Tiszanána in Tolna, Veszprém and Heves Counties. The name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language or in the Slavonic languages. It may be derived from the Sumerian nun 'prince' (Labat 1948: No. 87). The pre-Semitic Sumerian name of Babylon was Nun-ki 'prince's place'. Nána may have been the property of a prince in antiquity. The word-final element -a is the Sumerian adjectival suffix marking the status of the village. Old Hungarian spelling: 1055 Nane; 1121 Nana; 1488 Nana. NANASI Nánási [na:na:shi]. Also spelled Nánásy. More recent equivalent of the older Nánai where the adjectival suffix -ás replaced the ancient -a. See Nanai for more detail. NAP Nap [nap]. A surname given for no obvious reason. Nap has a dual meaning in the Hungarian language: 'sun' and 'day'. The Sumerians also used one cuneiform sign for sun and day, only the pronunciation differed slightly: utu 'sun' and ud 'day' (Labat 1948: No. 380). The Sumerian word for 'planet, star' and 'to shine' is nap (Labat 1948: No. 129a), same phonetic form as the Hungarian nap ‘sun’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1195 nop-; 1246 Nap; NEDOBA Nedoba [nedoba]. A name of Slovakian origin derived from nádoba 'dish, cooking utensil'. A maker or seller of such items may have been named so. NEGYESI Négyesi [ne:djeshi]. Also spelled Négyessy. The fourth child in a family may have been named Négyes, comprised of négy 'four' and the adjectival suffix -es that denotes position. The -i (archaic -y) ending is the derivational suffix, thus Négyesi means 'descendant of the fourth child'. Négy is present in most Finno-Ugric languages in similar forms, cf. Finnish neljä, Estonian neli (Benkő 1967: 1007, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1131 Negus; 1277 Neges; 1396 Negess-; 1462 Negyes-. NEHEZ Nehéz [nehe:z]. The exact meaning of the word nehéz is 'heavy' but it is also used for identifying a difficult situation (nehéz helyzet) or person (nehéz ember). Old Hungarian spelling: 1367 Nehez; 1505 Nehez. NEMA Néma [ne:ma]. 'Mute'. The prevailing theory is that the Hungarian néma is derived from the Slavonic nêm, néma, némo 'mute' but its origin is uncertain. It may be possible that néma has evolved from the Sumerian nu-eme comprised of nu (Labat 1948: No. 75), a negation which may be translated as 'without' identical to the Hungarian ne, nem 'no, not' and eme 'language' (Labat 1948: No. 32). The meaning of the



75
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
compound nu-eme would then be 'no language' or 'without language', i. e. ‘mute’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1226 Nema; 1477 Nema. NEMES Nemes [nemesh]. The name indicates the social standing of being noble by birth, rank, excellence or worth. It is derived from nem 'genus, family' which has accompanied the names of prominent Hungarian families since the earliest times of their history. For example the Bor-Kalán nem. Descendants of those families automatically belonged to the nobility and were referred to as nemes, along with those who were elevated to this rank by decree. The word-final element -es is the adjectival suffix. Nemes is a recently coined surname by comparison and prevails only among common people, although some families having this name could have belonged to the nobility but were stripped of their title during the country's turbulent history. The word nemzet 'nation' is also derived from nem. The name and the title may be derived from the Sumerian nam 'genus, family' (Gostony 1975: 14), or nu, numun 'descendants' (Labat 1948: No 72). Old Hungarian spelling: 1231 Numus; 1347 Nemus; 1354 Nemes. NEMETH Németh [ne:met]. A German is called Német in the Hungarian language. The word-final element h marks the reduced plosion of the preceding t. The precise origin of the name is uncertain and its etymology is obscure. The prevalent proposal, listed in the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, suggests a Slavonic origin from nemec 'foreigner' or nemi 'dumb' (Benkő 1967: 1011, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1130 Nemti; 1211 Nimed; 1219 Nemeth; 1372 nemet. The problem with the Slavonic source is that the name was in use long before the status of those languages advanced to a position where they could exert their influence on non-Slavic people. For example, the existence of a Celtic tribe called Nemet is substantiated by coins unearthed in the area what the Romans later called Noricum. The Celtic inhabitants were conquered by Roman Emperor Augustus. Also, Tacitus (c. AD 56-120) mentioned a German tribe called Nemetas, who occupied the left bank of the Rhine in around AD 50 in the area of present-day Worms and Speyer (Furneaux 1907: 93). In line with this, the people of Northern Germany are always referred to as Német by old Hungarian chroniclers, while the population of Austria and Bavaria are constantly called Bavarii in Latin language chronicles (Bajor in Hungarian). This evidence indicates that the ancient form was better preserved in the Hungarian language than in any of the Slavonic languages. This leaves in doubt that Német is a Slavonic loan word in the Hungarian language. German immigration to Hungary is recorded as early as the beginning of the second millennium AD. The intermarriages between Hungarian and German nobility caused a migration of servants in both directions.
