The German squires and high ranking priests preferred to bring their German speaking servants and workers to the new country. This first wave of immigrants thus consisted mostly of peasants and farmhands. The Germans, at the beginning, have settled exclusively on crown lands in isolated communities and were granted special privileges. Considering the selfsupporting way of life in those days, a body of people like that, could exist for a long period of time without being exposed to the language and culture of the host country.



76
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
The second wave of Germans arrived following the Mongol devastation of Hungary in 1241. A large number of them were from Bavaria and they settled in Vas and Sopron counties. After Hungary became part of the Habsburg Empire, German speaking Roman Catholics migrated to Hungary en masse to fill the vacancy created by the Turkish genocide. The Austrian authorities did not allow Hungarians to repopulate the areas devastated by the Turks (Ács 1986: 36, 70, 163, 171). NINACS Ninács [nina:ch]. The name cannot be etymologized entirely in the Hungarian language. The initial element Nin is identical with the Sumerian nin 'lady' (Labat 1948: No. 556) which is present in the Hungarian language in the form of nén, néni 'older sister, dame'. The second element ács means 'carpenter' as an independent word, but in this case it appears to be an altered form of the adjectival suffix -ás. The name would then mean 'lady-like'. NEZO Néző [ne:zø:]. The literal translation of this name is 'onlooker, observer' but also denoted a medicine man in rural Hungary (B. Lőrinczy 2002: 70, vol. 4), one who can heal and see into the future. The name is comprised of néz 'look' and the participle -ő. Similar words exist in other Finno-Ugric languages, cf. Finnish nähdä, (näen) (Benkő 1967: 1017, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1426 -nezew; 1564 níző. NOGRADI Nógrádi [no:gra:di]. Also spelled Nógrády and Neógrády. The name is comprised of nog, the archaic spelling of the present-day nagy 'large, big and important’, see Nagy for etymology. The second element rád (the name of an old Hungarian clan) is a toponym in itself and part of numerous other toponyms in the Carpathian Basin. The word-final element -i (archaic -y) is the derivational suffix indicating origin. Rad means 'canal' in the Middle-Babylonian dialect, the equivalent of the Sumerian šita (Labat 1948: No. 83) whose phonetic value is similar to the Hungarian csatorna 'canal'. Taking all these into consideration, it may be that the Rád clan had a leading role in building canals for irrigation and ditches for draining swamps. Remnants of an ancient drainage and dike system extending in the easterly direction from Budapest to Ungvár and down south to Versec in the Lower-Danube area of historical Hungary is still detectable (Bakai 1997: 187, vol. 1). The city of Nógrád, and the County named after it, was probably the homeland of the dominant Rád clan, thus the qualifier nog. A person from Nógrád is called Nógrádi. The Slavonic source of Novi Grad, Novograd 'new fort' is doubtful. However, this origin was propagated by Hungarian linguists of Slavic descent and some families modified their name to Neogrady in an attempt to link their name to the Greek instead of the Slavonic languages. Neos means 'new' in Greek, the same as nov, novi, etc. means 'new' in the Slavonic languages. Cf. Latin novus 'new'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Naugrad; 1211 Neugrad; 1440 Nougrod. NYAKAS Nyakas [njakash]. 'Stubborn'. The name contains nyak 'neck' and the adjectival suffix -as. The exact translation is 'one who has a neck' but metaphorically used to denote a stubborn individual. Old Hungarian spelling: 1312 Nakas; 1319 Nyakas. NYARI Nyári [nja:ri]. Also spelled Nyáry. 'Summery'. A seasonal-name given to a person born in the 'summer'. The meaning of nyár is 'summer'. The word-final element -i



77
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
(archaic -y) is the derivational suffix. Old Hungarian spelling: 1398 -nyar-; 1416 nar-; 1645 nyári. NYARS Nyárs [nja:rsh]. 'Skewer'. Metaphorical name of a cook who roasts meat on a spit over open fire. Old Hungarian spelling: 1395 nÿas; 1558 nars. NYEKI Nyéki [nje:ki]. A dweller in a fenced place. The closest English term would be 'one who lives in a gated community'. Nyék is an obsolete word for 'fence' (Benkő 1967: 1039, vol. 2) to which the derivational suffix -i is attached. Nyék is also an ancient Hungarian tribal name probably of Finno-Ugric origin, thus Nyéki would denote a person from that tribe. Old Hungarian spelling: 1113 Nec; 1138 Neku; 1165 Nichw. NYERGES Nyerges [njergesh]. The name is comprised of nyereg 'saddle' and the adjectival suffix -es, it denotes a maker or seller of saddles. The reconstructed FinnoUgric proto-word may have been *närkɜ (Benkő 1967: 1039, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1227 Nerges; 1359 Nyerges NYERS Nyers [njersh]. 'Raw'. Metaphor for a ‘blunt person’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1395 Ners-; 1475 Nyers. NYILAS Nyilas [njilash]. 'Archer'. An expert in using bow and arrow or a maker of them may have been named so. The reconstructed Finno-Ugric proto-word may have been *ńole (Benkő 1967: 1045, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1359 Nylas; 1423 Nilas; 1464 Nylyas; 1671 nyilas. NYIRO Nyírő [nji:rø:]. Obsolete Hungarian word for 'barber'. The name is derived from nyír 'to cut, clip, trim' to which the participle -ő is suffixed. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Nereh; 1423 Nyrew; 1507 Nyerew; 1545 nirö-; 1584 Nyrö; 1597 Nyreö; 1602 Nyirő. NYITRAI Nyitrai [njitrai]. Also spelled Nyitray. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Nyitra, a city in historical Hungary, confined to Slovakia and renamed Nitra. The adjacent river is also called Nyitra. The name is of uncertain derivation. Hungarian sources suggest the hypothesized proto-word *Nitrahwa derived from the old German *nid 'flow' and the Gothic *alva 'water' (Kiss 1978: 475). Another possible source may be the Greek nitria 'a place where potash is mined, an alkali field'. The Greek connection is supported by the city's name recorded as Nitria in old documents until 1328 and part of the population is listed as Káliz (Györffy 1998: 429-436, vol. 4), the name of Turkish speaking Mohammedans. NYOMARKAI Nyomárkai [njoma:rkai]. A dweller by the nyomárok may have received this name. The word is compounded from nyom 'trail track' and árok 'ditch' describing a traveled path in a natural or man-made depression. The derivational suffix -i marks origin. A dweller by such a geographical feature may have been named so. NYUL Nyúl [nju:l]. 'Hare, rabbit'. Metaphorical name for a 'fast' or 'timid' person, a perceived characteristic of a hare. Old Hungarian spelling: 1130 Nulu; 1237 Nul. OBAJT Obajt [obajt]. A difficult name to etymologize. Probably related to jobbágy 'serf' which appears to be a distorted hybrid fit together from various sources. One of the sources are the Arabic ubajd 'servant of lower rank' the diminutive of abd 'male servant'



78
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
(Ahmed 2001: 3, 214). See Jobbágy for more information. OBBAGY Obbágy [oba:dj]. A name cognate with Obajt and Jobbágy. See above for explanation. OBESTER Óbester [o:beshter]. A derivation from the German obersten 'colonel'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1707 óbester-; 1742 Obriszter; 1788 Óbester. OCSAI Ócsai [o:chai]. A person from the village of Ócsa in Pest County. The initial ó means 'old, ancient' in the Hungarian language. The element csa may be derived from the Old Akkadian ša17 'pasture-land' (Labat 1948: No. 318). The same cuneiform sign was later pronounced ritu which is almost identical with the Hungarian rét 'pasture-land'. The meaning of Ócsa, therefore, would be 'old pasture-land'. The word-final element -i is the derivational suffix. A dweller by such a place would be called so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1243 Olsa; 1244 Olcha; 1294 Olza, Ozla; 1332 Ócsa. OCSENY Őcsény [ø:che:nj]. The name pertains to öcs 'younger brother' to which the nominal suffix -ény is attached. A village in Tolna County is called Őcsény. The word öcs is present in the Uralic and Altaic languages in similar phonetic form and identical meaning. The reconstructed Finno-Ugric proto-word is *ećɜ. The old Turkish içi 'younger brother' (Németh 1990: 267) is also part of this etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 wch-; 1284 Euch-; 1492 ewch-. ODON Ödön [ødøn]. A probable variant of the Sumerian udun 'stove, oven' (Deimel 1939: No. 249). A maker of ovens may have received the name. According to another interpretation, Ödön is the distorted form of Eugene, a compound of two Greek words: eu 'good, well' and genes 'born'. The science of eugenics is devoted to the improvement of children. Old Hungarian spelling: 1453 Ewden. OHEGYI Óhegyi [o:hedji]. Comprised of ó 'old ancient', hegy 'mountain' and the derivational suffix -i. A dweller by the Óhegy 'ancient mountain' may have been named so. OKROS Ökrös [økrøsh]. A keeper of oxen for use in agriculture may have received the name. It is comprised of ökör 'ox' and the adjectival suffix -ös. The ö in the middle was omitted by syncopation. The Hungarian ökör is derived from the Turkish öküz (Benkő 1967: 23, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1344 Vkrus; 1350 Wkrus. OLAH Oláh [ola:]. The Hungarian name for a Romanian. The name was applied originally to the inhabitants of Wallachia, a former principality between the southern slopes of the Transylvanian Alps and the Danube River. When Walachia united with Moldavia in 1861, the new country became Romania, a name chosen to emphasize their linguistic ties with the Romans and the Latin language. Hungarians, perplexed by the choice of name, continued to use the term Oláh in order to maintain the separate identity of Transylvanian Gypsies, who always called themselves Rom and their language Romany.
The movements of Oláhs to Hungarian Transylvania was not part of any official immigration process. They have begun to cross into Hungary uninvited, one by one at the end of the 12th century according to contemporary documents. Due to their astoundingly high birth rate, they outnumbered every other ethnic group by the mid-18th century in some areas, including the indigenous Hungarian Székelys (Ács 1986: 95, 152).



79
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Walach, the original name of the Romanians, is derived from the same root as the English Walloon and Welsh (Onions 1985), German Wallone, French wallon (Eckhard 1984: 1987) and the Latin volcae (Györkössy 1986: 603), all of which originally denoted Celtic tribes. The term is now also used for identifying foreigners, mostly speakers of the Romance languages, e.g. the German welsch 'foreigner' (Halász 1983: 2181, vol. 2). The name probably found its way to Hungary in the written form Vlah pronounced [ula:]. One should note that the grapheme v also represented the sound [u] in early Latin scripts. Contributing to the confusion is the misleading name of the Blaks, also known as Ulaks (Györffy 1987: 448, vol. 2), a people of Turkic origin who lived in the vicinity of the Walachians. Whatever the case is, the initial [u] changed to [o] and Hungarians have been retaining this form to the present day. Old Hungarian spelling: 1283 Olah; 1398 Holah; 1602 Olá. Another name derived from the same root is Olasz 'Italian', which also denotes Celts, the pre-Roman population of northern Italy, and reflects an obvious misconception about the identity of the population further south. Old Hungarian spelling: 1181 Uloz; 1257 Oloz; 1284 Olus; 1449 Olaz; 1545 Olasz. The Hungarian forename Ulászló contains the element Ulasz, the forerunner of Olasz to which ló, a derivation from the Sumerian lu 'man' (Labat 1948: No. 330) is attached to denote an 'Italian man'. Referring to Romanians and Italians as Celts is by no means unique to Hungarians. The popular Polish term for Romania is Woɫochi and the Poles still call Italy Wɫochy.
OLAJOS Olajos [olajosh]. The adjectival suffix -os attached to olaj 'oil' marks a maker or seller of cooking oil. The Hungarian Olaj is derived from the Latin oleum 'oil'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1419 Olayos; 1460 Olayws; 1522 Olayos; 1714 Olajos. OLDAL Oldal [oldal]. A dweller on the side of a settlement may have been called Oldal 'side'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1347 oldol-; 1422 -oldal. OLYVES Ölyves [øjvesh]. The adjectival suffix -es attached to ölyv 'hawk' indicates a keeper or trainer of hawks. The Hungarian ölyv is probably derived from the Turkic *elige or *iläy (Benkő 1967: 26, vol. 3). The modern Turkish word for hawk atmaca shows no resemblance to the reconstructed (*) forms. Old Hungarian spelling: 1093 Vlues-; 1055 uluues; 1211 Wlues; 1434 Ewlwes; 1449 Ewlywes; 1602 Ölyves. ONODI Onódi [ono:di]. Also spelled Onódy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Onód, a town in Borsod-Abaúj-Zemplén County. The root ono has no meaning in the Hungarian language, it may be a variant of the Sumerian unu 'dwelling, residence' (Labat 1948: No. 195) to which the grammatical designator -d is suffixed to denote a place. The same cuneiform sign pronounced unug was used for writing the name of the Sumerian city Uruk, accompanied by ki 'place' (Unug-ki). Old Hungarian spelling of Onód: 1296 Olnod; 1211 Vnud. OR Őr [ø:r]. The Hungarian őr 'guard' appears to be a derivation of the Sumerian urù 'to



80
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
guard' (Labat 1948: No. 331). Old Hungarian spelling: 1221 Euru; 1251 Eur;1355 Ewr; 1377 Or-; 1461 Ewr-. ORBAN Orbán [orba:n]. Also spelled Urbán. The name is derived from the Latin urbanus 'city dweller'. Orbán was first a forename given in memory of Pope Orban (died in 230), but later became a surname. It was spelled Vrbanus and Wrbanus in 1211, 1213 and 1550. ORDAS Ordas [ordash]. 'Wolf'. See Farkas for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1239 Wrdas, 1334 Ordos; 1338 Ordas. ORDOG Ördög [ørdøg]. 'Devil'. An unusual surname, may have been bestowed on a person acting the part of a devil at folk festivals. The word may originate in the Sumerian language, cf. udug 'devil, demon' (Labat 1948: No. 557). An unexplored etymology of the Hungarian ördög may be őr-dög 'deceased guardian', i.e. the spirit of a deceased individual that protects a living person. The equivalent of the present-day őr-angyal 'guardian angel'. The Hungarian őr 'guard' was spelled Euru in a 1221 place name, a form almost identical with the Sumerian urù 'to guard'. See Or above. The word dög is present in the surname Dugus, where the word-final element -us is the adjectival suffix. This would make an early form of uru-dug possible. The lexical item dög is now associated with dead bodies of animals and anything bad that should be avoided. However, it denoted a dead person in antiquity (B. Lőrinczy 1979: 1011, vol. 1). Taking all these into consideration, the ördög may have been a benevolent spirit until it became the representative of sin and wrongdoing. Hungarians still say ördögöm van 'I have a devil' when luck strikes them. (This concept was first explored by Zoltán T. Forray, president of Sándor Kőrösi Csoma Historical Society, Toronto). The semantic shift may have occurred at the time of change in religious beliefs. Old Hungarian spelling: 1075 orduk; 1270 wrdug; 1422 Ewrdegh; 1560 Ördög-. OREG Öreg [øreg]. The primary meaning of öreg is 'old, aged' but a person who spent a long time in one kind of activity or was the resident of the same home for an extended period of time is also said to be 'old at it'. As a name Öreg may denote a reliable, experienced person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1473 Oregh; 1516 ewregh; 1542 ereeg; 1544 öreg. ORI Őri [ø:ri]. Also spelled Őry. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) attached to őr 'guard' indicates a descendant of a watchman or member of a security organization. The most probable source of the Hungarian őr is the Sumerian urù, see Or above for detailed explanation. ORLEI Őrlei [ø:lei]. Also spelled Őrley. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Őrlő 'grinder, miller'. The Hungarian őr(-öl) 'to grind, to mill' may be related to the Sumerian ar, arà 'to grind, to mill' (Labat 1948: No. 401). The name is comprised of őr, the connecting consonant l and the participle -e. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language lists Turkic sources for the root őr, cf. Uighur ävir 'turn, rotate', Osmanli evir- 'turn', etc. The act of rotation, the ancient way of grinding with rotating a stone may have initiated the term for 'grind' and 'mill' (Benkő 1967: 36, vol. 3). However, the Sumerian ar, arà are phonetically and semantically closer to the Hungarian őr than the Turkic words. ORMANDI Ormándi [orma:ndi]. Also spelled Ormándy. This name is derived from the



81
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Turkish orman 'forest' and denotes a forest dweller. The suffix -d indicates a place and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) marks place of origin. Old Hungarian spelling: 1130 Vrmandi, 1202 Orman, Vrman. ORMAI Ormai [ormai]. Also spelled Ormay. A difficult name to etymologize. It may mean a person who has a prominent nose. Orr means 'nose' but it also means any protruding object including a mountain peak. In most cases the peak of a mountain is referred to as orom. What makes this name unusual is the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a that indicates condition. The suffix would be appropriate for a person with protruding nose. The word-final element -i (archaic -y) signifies a descendant of Orma (Ormos in modern Hungarian). OROSZ Orosz [oros]. 'Russian'. The name may be derived from the Finnish ruots (archaic rus) which means 'Swedish' and was given to the Varangians who crossed Finland on their way to establish a colony south of Lake Ladoga at Novgorod and Kiyiv (Kiev). However, some onomasticians derive the name from ruotsi 'rowers', name of the Swedish oarsmen serving on Viking ships. Russ remains the basis of the name in most Indo-European languages. The Uralic and Altaic language groups prefer to have the vowel o or u in front of the initial r. Early Hungarians did not differentiate between Russians and the Ukrainians of Galicia, though they called Ukrainians Kisorosz 'Lesser-Russian'. People from Galicia began entering Hungary in the 13th century. They settled in small groups in the sparsely populated mountainous regions of Máramaros, Bereg, Ung and Zemplén Counties. Earlier presence of people from Galicia may be attributed to volunteer service in the Hungarian king's army. Large waves of refugees arrived in Hungary during the 17th century escaping the tyranny of their landlords (Ács 1986: 101). Old Hungarian spelling: 1130 Uruz-; 1211 Vros; 1280 Wrz; 1295 Oroz. OROSZLAN Oroszlán [orosla:n]. 'Lion'. A brave man may have been named so. The name is derived from the Turkish arslan, aslan 'lion'. Hungarian linguists trace it to the hypothetical *arsilan and link it to the color 'reddish-yellow' . Old Hungarian spelling: 1247 Wruzlan-; 1256 Orozlan-; 1260 Oruslan-; 1754 Oroszlyán-; 1774 Oroszleány-; 1889 araszliány. ORS Örs [ørsh]. The exact Hungarian meaning of örs is 'a group of soldiers allocated to provide sentinels for guarding a place'. It is an inappropriate name for an individual. A more likely source is the Turkish örs [ørs] 'anvil', a personal name that has its equivalent in the Hungarian surname Üllő 'anvil'. ORSA Orsa [orsha]. The archaic rendition of Orsós 'maker or seller of spindles', derived from ors 'spindle' to which the archaic adjectival suffix -a is attached. Similar words exist in some of the Turkic languages having the same meaning, cf. Tatar orčïg, Bashkir orsoq, Uighur urćuk (Benkő 1967: 1095, vol. 2). Old Hungarian spelling: 1339 Orsus-; 1638 orsos. ORSOS Orsós [orsho:sh]. Modern form of Orsa, the present-day adjectival suffix -os replaced the archaic -a. See above for more information. ORSZAGH Országh [orsa:g]. The word-final element -h marks the reduced plosion of the preceding g. There are several possible etymologies for this surname and they all lead to the Sumerian language. While uru means 'city, circle' (Labat 1948: No. 38) and zag means



82
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
'territory' (Labat 1948: No. 332), the compound uru-zag can be interpreted as ország 'country' (Gostony 1975: 60). The remnants of large circular earthworks that served as fortifications are still visible throughout the Carpathian Basin. They were still in use during the Avar era (c. AD 670-896) and seem to fit Labat's interpretation of the cuneiform sign for 'city, circle'. An equally plausible etymology is the combination of the Sumerian uru 'man, hero' (Labat 1948: No. 575) and sag 'land' (Deimel 1939: 94). The combined meaning would be 'land of man' or 'land of heroes'. Another approach would be the Sumerian hursag 'mountain, highland' (Gostony 1975: 60). All three etymologies are very close to the phonetic value and meaning of the Hungarian ország ‘country, land’. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language derives ország from uru, the forerunner of the present-day úr 'lord', and -szág [sa:g] a version of the nominal suffix -ság [sha:g]. It suggests that ország 'country' is a variant of uraság 'squire, nobleman' (Benkő 1967: 1095, vol. 2). For úr the dictionary suggests a relationship with the Finnish uros 'adult man, hero', which appears to be a variant of the Sumerian uru 'man, hero' listed above. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Vrzac; 1423 Orzag; 1427 Orzaag; 1436 Orzagh; 1728 ország. ORVOS Orvos [orvosh]. 'Medical practitioner'. A name of uncertain derivation according to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language. One possible origin would be Finno-Ugric, cf. Finnish arpa 'fate, magic wand'. The other possibility is Old Turkic, cf. Uighur arwïš 'magic formula' (Benkő 1967: 1097, vol. 2). Both of these explanations indicate someone with magical powers, therefore the potentiality of a Sumerian source cannot be disregarded. The Sumerian ur5(-us) mean 'oracle' (Labat 1948: No. 401) which may be interpreted as 'an uncommonly wise person whose opinion is not disputed'. A medicine man would fit into that category. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Vruus; 1457 Orwos; 1522 Wrwos. OSKU Őskű [ø:shky:]. A person from Őskű, a village in Veszprém County, may have been called so. The village's name is compounded from ős 'very old, ancient, ancestor' and kű the old form of the present-day kő 'stone'. Ős is derived from the Sumerian úš 'ancestry' (Labat 1948: No. 69) and kő is present in the Finno-Ugric languages in various forms. The reconstructed proto-word is *kiße. However, the Sumerian cuneiform sign for ku 'to lay foundation' means 'foundation' when pronounced dúr, but dúr-mi-na means 'stone' (Labat 1948: No. 536). There seem to be a mismatch in the dictionary between phonetic values and meanings. Old Hungarian spelling: 1461 Ewskew. OSZ Ősz [ø:s]. 'Fall, Autumn'. One born in the fall may have been named so. The word ősz seems to be genuinely Hungarian. The Finno-Ugric proto-word *sikśe, listed in the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language, appears to be farfetched. Old Hungarian spelling: 1100 -euz; 1416 oz-; 1456 ez-; 1541 ész-, üsz; 1783 ősz-. OTVOS Ötvös [øtvøsh]. 'Goldsmith, silversmith'. The initial element öt is the archaic form of the present-day önt 'cast', which is followed by the connecting consonant v and



83
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
the adjectival suffix -ös that indicates occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1389 Wthwes; 1442 Ewthwes; 1455 Ewthues; 1463 Etwes; 1578 Eothveos; 1602 Eötves. OZ Őz [ø:z]. 'Deer'. An intriguing name for a person, unless one is a gamekeeper. The semblance between the Hungarian őz 'deer' and the Sumerian ùz 'goat' (Labat 1948: No. 122b) is so close that a misidentification cannot be dismissed. The old Hungarian spelling of őz would substantiate this possibility: 1138 Euz; 1330 wz; 1714 űz. OZVEGY Özvegy [øzvedj]. 'Widow(-er). A genuine Hungarian name absent in other languages. Old Hungarian spelling: 1302 Wzwegh; 1560 Oszűegh; 1790 Özvögy. PAAL Paál [pa:l]. Has no meaning in the current Hungarian language. Pa-al means 'temple administrator' (Deimel 1939: No. 169) in the Sumerian language. PAIZS Paizs [paizh]. Also spelled Pais. Archaic form of the present-day pajzs 'shield'. A maker of shields would have received such a name. The Hungarian pajzs is derived from the Italian pavese, the name of a special type of shield made in Pavia (Benkő 1967: 61, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Poiais; 1384 pajs; 1522 pais-; 1603 Pajs-. PAJTAS Pajtás [pajtash]. 'Pal, mate'. A probable derivation from the Turkish pay 'share, portion', paydaş 'shareholder'. The original Hungarian meaning of pajtás may have been friends who give and receive equally or share in good or bad. Old Hungarian spelling: 1363 Paytas-; 1597 paitas. PALA Pala [pala]. 'Slate'. A worker skilled in splitting blocks of slate into plates may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1717 pala. PALANKAI Palánkai [pala:nkai]. The name is comprised of the word palánk 'palisade', the archaic adjectival suffix -a and the derivational suffix -i. It indicates the descendant of a dweller within the palánk, a kind of fortification or fence consisting of a row of upright posts set firmly in the ground. The origin of the word palánk is unclear, cf. French palanque, German planken. A similar word was used in ancient Mesopotamia. The Sumerian gišbulug and the Akkadian palukku are both identified as a 'cane commune' (Labat 1948: No. 96). What makes this interpretation questionable is the qualifier giš which means 'wood, log' (Labat 1948: No. 96). Therefore, the cuneiform sign may have indicated a community surrounded by a palánk made of posts. Old Hungarian spelling: 1564 palank-. PALCZA Pálcza [pa:ltsa]. 'Stick, rod'. The word has no probable reason in the Hungarian language to form a surname. It is identical with the Slavonic palica 'stick, rod'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1452 Palcza. PALFI Pálfi [pa:lfi]. Also spelled Pálfy, Pálffy. The patronymic suffix -i indicates a descendant of Pál, a name cognate with Paál. Hungarian sources identify Pál with the Latin Paulus 'little, short' (Ladó 1978: 202). But a Mesopotamian origin is more likely. See Paal for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1358 Palfy-; 1359 Pal. PALLO Palló [palo:] A thick board or plank is called palló. A maker or seller of planks may have been named so. The reduplicated l marks a stressed consonant. The participle



84
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
ó is usually suffixed to verbs which would suggest that pal was once a verb. Old Hungarian spelling: 1512 pallo; 1546 palo. PALOC Palóc [palo:ts]. Also spelled Palócz. No satisfactory etymology has been advanced for the origin of this name. One explanation is the Slavonic plavec, polovec 'Cumanian'(Györfy 1990: 319, vol. 3), based entirely on phonetic resemblance and anecdotal evidence. Cumanians never lived in the area populated by the Palóc group at the junction of Heves, Borsod and Gömör counties of historical Hungary. Most of this area is now in Slovakia but there are no place names indicating their presence in that region. Old Hungarian spelling: 1220 Polouc; 1656 palócz-. A more rational approach would be to consider the word-final element -óc to represent a Hungarian suffix which lends an abstract quality to the preceding word, as in gombóc a kind of dumpling that looks like a gomb 'button' or gömb 'ball' and vadóc a person who behaves like a vad 'savage'. The word pal then would be regarded the root, but its corresponding phonetic variants, like par, bar and bal should also be taken into account because they are also part of surnames. Cf. Paróczai, Baróczy, Balczó. Consequently, it is not entirely certain that Palóc is the original, or the only form, but may be a phonetically modified shape which either superseded or followed the others. It is common for the sound b to change to its voiceless counterpart p in all languages including Hungarian. Thus, an original bal could have easily changed to pal at an earlier time. None of the aforementioned variants would yield a recognizable meaning except bal which means 'left' in the current Hungarian vernacular. Bal was also associated with baj 'trouble' in antiquity as in balsors 'misfortune', baleset 'mishap', and so on. The word baj has been also used for name formation, cf. Bajnóczi.
The foregoing discussion points in the direction of Balóc as a possible primary form, a name that would be given to a group of troublemakers. A people difficult to manage. If so, the name's ultimate origin may be the Sumerian verb bal 'to be hostile, to upset, to rebel' (Gostony 1975: 42). PANNA Panna [pana]. Derived from the Latin pannus 'rag' or pannonus 'raged, shabby', the name denotes a poorly dressed individual. The word-final element -a is the archaic adjectival suffix. PAP Pap [pap]. Also spelled Papp. The Hungarian pap 'father' (used only in a religious sense for a priest) corresponds to the Sumerian pab 'father' and the Neo-Babylonian pap 'father' (Deimel 1939: No. 169). Old Hungarian spelling: 1239 Pap-. PARADY Parády [para:di]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a resident of Parád, a town in Heves County known for its spa. The name is comprised of the Sumerian gišpa 'branch' (Labat 1948: No. 295) and the Mid-Babylonian rad 'canal' (Labat 1948: No. 83). The toponym denotes a place by the distributary of a canal. PARAZS Parázs [parazh]. The Hungarian parázs means 'glowing embers' in present-day vernacular. The Hungarian Historical-Etymological Dictionary lists the Slavonic pražit, pražiti 'to quick fry, to roast' as [an unlikely] source. A Hungarian-Latin Dictionary published in 1604 by Albertus Molnar Szenci in Nürnberg spells the word parás, the



85
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
equivalent of the Latin pruna. If the word-final element -ás qualifies for the Hungarian adjectival suffix -ás then par is the root and parás is an adjective. According to the Új Magyar Tájszótár (New Hungarian Dialect Dictionary) the word parázs is associated with the condition porhanyós 'powdery, crumbling' derived from por 'dust'. This would suggest that parázs is a variant of poros 'dusty'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1585 Paras-; 1861 porázs. PARKANYI Párkányi [pa:rka:nji]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a former resident of Párkány, a town in Esztergom County of historical Hungary. Confined to Slovakia in 1920 and its name was changed to Štúrovo. The toponym Párkány may be derived from the Sumerian bar or the Akkadian parakku both meaning 'fort' (Labat 1948: No. 334). Old Hungarian spelling: 1295 parcan; 1425 parkan. PATAKI Pataki [pataki]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a dweller by the patak 'stream, brook'. The name may be derived from the Sumerian pa6 'ditch, drain' (Labat 1948: No. 60), or the Akkadian pattu which is close to present-day Hungarian phonetic value. The word-final element -ak is an active voice suffix. Old Hungarian spelling: 1200 potac-; 1210 potok; 1317 patak. PATASI Patasi [patashi]. A probable modified form of the Sumerian pa-te-si 'princecurate' (Labat 1948: No. 60). The cuneiform sign is also pronounced ensi. The name denoted a leader who performed both civic and clerical duties in Sumerian society. The Persian padeshah 'sovereign' (Lambton 1988: 30) may have also originated in the Sumerian language. PATAY Patay [patai]. The archaic derivational suffix -y marks a descendant of Pata 'hoof'. If the word-final element -a is the archaic adjectival suffix then the Hungarian root pat, English foot, Greek podos 'hoof' and Hittite pata 'foot' are cognates. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Pata. PECS Pécs [pe:ch]. Also spelled Péch. The name of a city in Baranya County. A resident of that city may have been called Pécs. The city may have received its name for the profusion of muskrats in the area. Muskrats belong to the same family as rats and the Sumerian word for ‘rat’ is peš (Labat 1948: No. 596). The initial element in the Hungarian pézsma-patkány 'muskrat' is pézs [pe:zh]. Trappers of muskrats may have also been called by such a name. The suggestion that the Hungarian Pécs is derived from the South-Slav peč 'oven, furnace’ (Kiss 1978: 505) does not take into consideration that the Slavonic peč is the initial element in words which mean 'roast, fried meat'. Their word for oven is furuna. Old Hungarian spelling: 1290 Peech. PENGO Pengő [pengø:]. The name is derived from peng 'ring, jingle' to which the participle -ő is suffixed to form an adjective. The name may originate from wearing a kind of ornament or jewelry that jingled. Old Hungarian spelling: 1501 Pengew; 1735 Pengő. PENTEK Péntek [pe:ntek]. Derived from the Greek pente 'five' the name may have been bestowed on the fifth child or one that was born on a péntek 'Friday'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Pentuk; 1165 Pentiki; 1332 Pintuk; 1372 pentek-. PESTI Pesti [peshti]. The derivational suffix -i marks a resident of Pest on the east side of the Danube, opposite Buda. An independent city until the unification with Buda in



86
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
1873, situated on the wide flat that extends from the shore of the river. The origin of the name may be traced to the Sumerian peš 'wide' (Deimel 1939: No. 204). The word-final element -t is a causative suffix. Old Hungarian spelling: 1148 pest. PETER Péter [pe:ter]. A derivation from the Greek petra 'rock' PILLER Pillér [pile:r]. The name is derived from an international loan-word of which the English equivalent is pillar. The origin of the word may be the Latin pila. PINTER Pintér [pinte:r]. 'Cooper, hooper', derived from the German Binde 'bond, tie'. Old Hungarian spelling: 1406 pyter; 1419 Pynter; 1423 Pinther; 1588 pinter. PIRI Piri [piri]. Also spelled Piry. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) denotes a descendent of Pír 'flush of dawn, aurora' (Országh 1977: 1604, vol. 2). An early riser may have been named so. The Hungarian Historical-Etymological Dictionary associates pír and pírkad with the color piros 'red', whereas pir is the stem and -os is the adjectival suffix. But Hungarians call the first appearance of light in the morning pír whether it is accompanied by a tint of red or not. The Hungarian pír may be derived from the Sumerian pirig 'light' (Labat 1948: No. 130). PIROS Piros [pirosh]. The Hungarian piros 'red', the Greek pirros 'fire-like red' may originate from a common root. Old Hungarian spelling: 1330 Pyrus; 1419 Piros. POGACSAS Pogácsás [poga:cha:sh]. Also spelled Pogátsás. 'Biscuit maker or seller'. The name is derived from the Turkish pogaça 'flaky pastry'. Similar phonetic forms and meanings are in the Slavonic languages, cf. Slovak pogač and Croatian pogača. Old Hungarian spelling: 1423 Pogachas. POGANY Pogány [poga:nj]. 'Pagan'. A derivation from the Latin discriminating epithet paganus 'a Non-Christian' which originally meant 'country folks' but was applied to those who did not convert to Christianity. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Pagan; 1329 Pogan; 1635 pogány-. POSA Pósa [po:sha]. The name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. It may be derived from the Middle-Babylonian púš 'restless' (Labat 1948: No. 60) to which the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a is attached. POZSONYI Pozsonyi [pozhonji]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Pozsony, the medieval coronation city and one time capital of historical Hungary. Confined to Slovakia in 1920 and renamed Bratislava. The city received its name from Pos [posh] name of the captain of its fortress (Kiss 1978: 523) which may be related to the Middle-Babylonian púš 'restless' (Labat 1948: No. 60). RAB Rab [rab]. Also spelled Rabb. The reduplicated b marks a stressed consonant. The name means ‘captive, prisoner’ in the Hungarian language. The word rab is present in the Slavonic languages but has different meaning: ‘servant’. Cf. Russian rob, Bulgarian rab. ‘Servant’ means szolga in the Hungarian language. The Hungarian rab is not a servant but a captured criminal or prisoner of war. Captives were kept in shackles in the old days when allowed outside a confined area. Thus, the Hungarian rab may have originated in the Sumerian language in which gišrab means ‘shackles’ (Labat 1948: No. 149).



87
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1498 Rab-; 1522 Rab. RABAI Rábai [ra:bai]. Also spelled Rábay. The derivational suffix -i indicates a dweller by the river Rába in western Hungary. The river’s name is most likely derived from the Estonian raba ‘swamp’ or Finnish rapa ‘mud, slush’ (Papp 1978: 643) and describes the muddy condition of the main course of water surrounded by marshes. Old Hungarian spelling: 1234 Rabay. RACZ Rácz [ra:ts]. The Hungarian Rác, also spelled Rátz, is an old name for a Serb. A prolonged mass migration of Serbs into southern Hungary began in 1439 when they crossed the Danube and Rábca rivers in front of the advancing Ottoman armies. The Bácska region (confined to Serbia in 1920, now called Vojvodina) was their chief area of settlement where they received lands, homes, language rights and exemption from most feudal obligations (Singleton 1985: 35, 44, 45, 49, 50, 73). Rácz is derived from the name of a 12th century Serbian principality recorded in Latin texts as Rasa, Rascia, Raxia (Benkő 1967: 326, vol. 3) and called Rashka in English language literature. It is a place near Novi Pazar, between Serbia and Montenegro and was the nucleus upon which greater Serbia was built. Old Hungarian Spelling: 1403 Rach; 1437 Raach; 1455 Racz. RADA. Rada [rada]. This name is a classic example for phonetic change and the use of an ancient adjectival suffix. It is comprised of rad that morphed into ragy in recent times. The plosive d changed to palatalized gy, a common occurrence in the Magyar vernacular. The word-final element -a is the archaic adjectival suffix, also present in the Sumerian language. It was replaced by the present-day ás. Consequently, written as ragyás, the name means ‘pock-marked’. RADI Rádi [ra:di]. Also spelled Rády. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates origin. A resident of a locality in Pest County called Rád. Originally the name of an old Hungarian clan, Rád is now part of numerous other toponyms in the Carpathian Basin. The word rad means ‘canal’ in the Middle-Babylonian language, equivalent of the Sumerian šita (Labat 1948: No. 83) whose phonetic value is similar to the Hungarian csatorna ‘canal’.
The Rád clan may have had a leading role in building canals for irrigation, ditches for draining swamps and other purposes. Remnants of an ancient canal and dike system extending in the easterly direction from Budapest to Ungvár and then south to Versec in the lower Danube area of historical Hungary is still detectable. The toponym Rád was recorded in Pest County in 1395 as Raad. The name has no meaning in present-day Hungarian. Other surnames derived from it are Ráday, Radics, Radó, Radócz, Rádonyi, Radva, Radvány. RAKOCZI Rákóczi [ra:ko:tsi]. Also spelled Rákóczy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic y) indicates a person from the town of Rákóc in Zemplén County of historical Hungary. The town was confined to Slovakia and renamed Rakovec nad Ondavou. The name of the town is derived from rák ‘crayfish’ to which the attribute -óc is affixed. Identical words exist in the Slavonic languages with identical meaning, cf. Polish rak ‘crayfish’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1390 Rakocz. RAPAI Rapai [rapai]. Also spelled Rappai. The reduplication of p marks a stressed consonant. The name is derived from the Finnish rapa ‘mud, slush’ (Papp 1978: 643) to



88
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
which the derivational suffix -i is affixed to indicate a dweller by an area saturated with water. RAVASZ Ravasz [ravas]. ‘Sharp, crafty’. A probable derivation from the Persian rava ‘lawful’ (Lambton 1988: 79). The original Hungarian word may have denoted a person familiar with the laws of the land. Old Hungarian spelling: 1135 Ruoz; 1342 Rawaz; 1620 Ravasz. REGOS Regős [regø:sh]. ‘Minstrel, bard’. Derived from rege the name of tale-like verses composed in honor of the achievements of leading personalities or heroes and recited at festivities. The word-final element -s denotes occupation or status. Old Hungarian spelling: 1219 Regus; 1401 Reges. REJTO Rejtő [rejtø:]. The name is derived from rejt ‘hide, conceal’ to which the participle -ő is affixed to denote a secretive, needlessly reserved person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1380 Rychtew; 1885 Rűtő. REMENYI Reményi [reme:nji]. Derived from remény ‘hope, expectation’ appended by the derivational suffix -i, the name signifies the descendent of a person full of hope and ambition. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Remen. RENDEK Rendek [rendek]. The name is a composite of rend ‘order, law’ and the substantive suffix -ek. It denotes an orderly, law obeying person. The legislative body in the Kingdom of Hungary was called the Assembly of Rendek. The word rend may be derived from the Sumerian rin ‘balance’ (Labat 1948: No. 393) and du ‘to create’ (Labat 1948: No. 230). Old Hungarian spelling: 1248 Rend-; 1296 Reend. RESO Réső [re:shø:]. An unusual name, it may be a variant of the Akkadian rêšu ‘head, leader’ (Labat 1948: No. 115). Another possibility is that it denotes an ancient miner. The Hungarian rés means ‘opening, hole’ but the participle -ő turns it into an action noun, i.e. an occupational name. It would have originated in the distant past when mining was done on the surface of an outcrop by carving holes into layers of minerals. Similar words can be found in the Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *raćɜ (Benkő 1967: 390, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Resu. RETI Réti [re:ti]. Also spelled Réthi and Réthy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a dweller on, or by the rét ‘pasture-land’. The h marks the reduced plosion of the preceding t. The word rét may be a relic from the distant past. A comparable Akkadian term rîtu ‘pasture-land’ (Labat 1948: No. 318) was recorded in Mesopotamia during the NeoBabylonian period. Old Hungarian spelling: 1210 Rethi; 1291 Rythy. REZSO Rezső [rezhø:]. A variant of Réső. See Reso for etymology. RIKA Rika [rika]. The Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a is attached to the Sumerian rig7 ‘to rule, to govern’ (Labat 1948: No. 295c). Compare with Réka, the wife of Attila the Hun was named so. ROKA Róka [ro:ka]. ‘Fox’. Owes its origin for slyness or other similar attributes. A probable derivative of the Sumerian lul-ka5-a ‘lying fox’ (Gostony 1975: 91).



89
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1558 Roka. RONAI Rónai [ro:nai]. Also spelled Rónay. The name denotes a dweller on the róna ‘plain, grass-land’ as indicated by the derivational suffix -i. The word róna is derived from the Slovak rovná ‘flat land’ according to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language. However this suggested origin is doubtful because the original dwelling places of Slovakians were mountainous areas. The cuneiform sign for the Old-Akkadian ru6 is the same as the sign for the Sumerian edin ‘plain’ (Labat 1948: No. 168), equivalent of the Hungarian egyen ‘plain’ from which egyenes ‘straight’ is derived. The Old-Akkadian ru6 maybe a Sumerian loan-word to which the adjectival suffix -a is appended by inserting the connecting consonant n, thus the resulting term runa is an action noun. Both u and ó are round back sounds therefore interchangeable, cf. Hungarian Mohi > Muhi (name of a field). Old Hungarian spelling: 1339: Rona. ROVID Rövid [røvid]. The name denotes a ‘short’ person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1275 reuid-; 1342 Ryuid-; 1522 Rewyd; 1611 röuid. RUDA Ruda [ruda]. The archaic adjectival suffix -a is appended to rúd ‘bar, mast, pole’ to signify a maker of such implements. Present-day form would be Rudas. Old Hungarian spelling: 1214 Roda; 1327 Rudas. SAAD Saád [sha:d]. The name may be derived from the Turkish şad ‘happy, joyful’. An alternative etymology would link the name with şad the title of a high dignitary among Mongolian Turks bestowed on members of the ruling family. It may be translated as ‘prince’. The title is engraved on the Orkhon Monument erected around A.D. 720 in Mongolia (Györffy 1975: 63, 276). SAFRANYOS Sáfrányos [sha:fra:njosh]. The adjectival suffix -os affixed to sáfrány ‘saffron’ indicates a grower of the plant or seller of the herb. The name of the plant is derived from the Persian za’fran (Lambton 1988: 348). Old Hungarian spelling: 1496 Safranyos; 1731 Sáfrányos-. SAGI Sági [sha:gi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant of Ság. The word šag ‘heart, center’ is of Sumerian origin (Gostony 1975: 29). SAJGO Sajgó [shajgo:]. The participle -ó is affixed to sajog, an obsolete dialectical word, meaning ‘shine, glitter’ (Bárczi és Országh 1959-1962: 1116, vol. 5). Old Hungarian spelling: 1560 Salgo; 1602 Saigo; 1630 saygo. SAJTOS Sajtos [shajtosh]. ‘Cheese maker or seller’. The name is comprised of sajt ‘cheese’ and the adjectival suffix -os that marks an occupation. The word sajt denotes the pressing process in cheese making. Other words derived from the same source are sajtó ‘printing-press’, sajtol ‘squeeze’ and satú ‘benchclamp’ which is identical with the Akkadian šahtu ‘press, squeezer’ (Gostony 1975: 81). Old Hungarian spelling: 1368: Saytas; 1372 saÿt-; 1533 Said-’; 1558 Saytos; 1563 sajt. SALA Sala [shala]. Variant of Csala ‘tempting, seductive’ (Kálmán 1978: 43). Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Sala. SALGA Salga [shalga]. Archaic form of salgó, sajgó ‘shine, sparkle’. A bright, cheerful individual may have been named so. See Sajgo.



90
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1518 salyog; 1602 Saigo. SALLOS Sallós [shalo:sh]. A variant of Sarlós ‘maker or seller of sickles’. See Sarlós for etymology. SAMAN Sámán [sha:ma:n]. The title of a priest of Shamanism, an idolatrous religion once widely practiced in parts of Asia. Was unknown in Hungary before the end of the 18th century. The word may have originated in the Manchu-Tungus language, sa [sha] ‘know’ samán ‘one who has knowledge’ (Benkő 1967: 481, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1799 Sámány-; 1817 Sámán-. SAMU Samu [shamu]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It may be derived from the Sumerian šám ‘archer’ or the Akkadian šâmu ‘archer’ (Labat 1948: No. 187). SANTA Sánta [sha:nta]. Also spelled Sántha. ‘Lame, limping’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1367 Santha; 1410 Santa. SANYARU Sanyarú [shanjaru:]. ‘Wretched, poverty-stricken’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1513 sanaru; 1777 sonyoru; 1863 Sonyarú, sanyarú. SARGA Sárga [sha:rga]. The Hungarian sárga ‘yellow’ and the Akkadian arqu ‘yellow’ may derive from the same source. There are similar words in the Turkic languages, cf. Turkish sari [shari]. The name may have been bestowed on a person of pale complexion. Old Hungarian spelling: 1360 Sarga; 1642 sárga. SARI Sári [sha:ri]. Widely believed to be the endearing form of Sára which may be the derivative of the Sumerian šara ‘king’ (Labat 1948: No. 344). Old Hungarian spelling: 1610 sári. SARKANY Sárkány [sha:rka:nj]. The translation of sárkány is ‘dragon’ but widely used as a metonym for an antagonistic, bellicose person. The root-word may be derived from the Sumerian šur ‘furious, raging’ (Labat 1948: No. 329) followed by the connecting consonant k and the nominal suffix -ány. Similar words are in the Turkic languages, cf. Cumanian saxagan, Osmanli sazagan (Benkő 1967: 494, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 Sarcajn; 1222 Sarkan; 1630 Sárkány. SARLOS Sarlós [sharlo:sh]. The name is comprised of sarló ‘sickle’ and the adjectival suffix -ós indicating a maker, seller or frequent user of a sickle. Sarló appears to be the participle of sar evidenced by the suffix -ó. But the early forms of sarló, which include Solló, sollu and saló in addition to sarú and sóró, lend support to the possibility of sa [sha] to be the root of the word. The l or r are connecting consonants. Therefore, the Sumerian ša5 ‘to cut’ (Labat 1948: No. 97) is suggested for a possible source. Sarló is present in the following Uralic and Altaic languages: Udmurt (Votyak) śurlo, Komi (Zyrian) t’śarla, Cheremis sa’rla (Benkő 1967: 494, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1109 Sorlous. SARVARI Sárvári [sha:rva:ri]. Also spelled Sárváry. The derivational suffix -i (archaic y) indicates a resident of Sárvár, a city in Vas County. The name of the city is comprised of sár ‘mud’ and vár ‘fort’ suggesting that the original fortification was an earthwork. Old Hungarian spelling: 1288 Saarwar. SAS Sas [shash]. ‘Eagle’. A Hungarian metonym for a person having good eyesight.



91
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1213 Sos; 1256 Sas-. SEBA Seba [sheba]. Appears to be the archaic form of present-day Sebes ‘fast, swift’. The word-final element -a is the continuation of the Sumerian adjectival suffix. SEBES Sebes [shebesh]. ‘Fast, swift’. Also spelled Sebess. The root seb is associated with fastness and hot-headedness when appended by the adjectival suffix -es. An alternate etymology would be sebes ‘covered with sores’ or ‘having scars’. The initial element seb means ‘sore, scar’ appended by the adjectival suffix -es that indicates condition. Old Hungarian spelling: 1039 Schebis; 1137 Sebus; 1181 Sebes. SELLO Sellő [shelø:]. A dweller by the sellő ‘rapids’. The root sel is a variant of ser in sergő ‘merry go around’, or sür in sürgő ‘stirring’. The latter denotes the fast turbulent water typical of rapids. Old Hungarian spelling: 1792 sellö-. SEMJEN Semjén [shemje:n]. Derived from semlyék ‘bog, marsh’, the name suggests a dweller by a bog. Old Hungarian spelling: 1244 semek-; 1886 sömjék. SENYE Senye [shenje]. ‘Languishing’. Shortened variant of senyved ‘suffering, sickly’ Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Sene; 1164 Seneu; 1602 Senye. SEPER Seper [sheper]. ‘One who sweeps with a broom’. Also spelled söpör. A possible derivation from the Sumerian šar ‘to sweep’. Similar words are in the Turkic languages, cf. Turkish süpürmek ‘to sweep’, süpürge ‘broom’ (Akdikmen 1986: 59, 527), old Turkish sipir- ‘broom, brush’ (Clauson 1972: 792). Old Hungarian spelling: 1372 seper-; 1519 söpör-. SERA Séra [she:ra]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. It may be comprised of the Sumerian šèr ‘to link, to connect’ (Labat 1948: No. 152) and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a. It would then denote what is called in present-day Hungarian a szerelő ‘assembler’. SERENYI Serényi [shere:nji]. Descendant of a hard-working individual. The name incorporates the root ser which forms the root of other interrelated words that describe ‘speed, activity, diligence, hard-working’, the nominal suffix -ny and the derivational suffix -i. Old Hungarian spelling: 1194: suren; 1585 Sérény. SERES Seres [sheresh]. Also spelled Sörös. An occupational name, a maker or seller of ser/sör ‘beer’ was named so. The word-final element -es/-ös is the adjectival suffix that marks an occupation. Similar words exist in the Uralic and Altaic languages derived from the old Hindi surâ ‘beer, alcoholic beverage’ (Benkő 1967: 580, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1392 Seres; 1436 Swr; 1522 Seorws; Sér; 1560 seres. SIETO Siető [shietø:] ‘One who hurries’. A derivation from the Sumerian ši-ed ‘hurries’ (Gostony 1975: 54), appended by the participle -ő. Old Hungarian spelling: 1372 syetes-; 1450 siet-; 1637 sieteös; 1668 sijet-. SIK Sik [shi:k]. A probable derivation from the Turkish şik ‘neat, elegant’ (Akdikmen 1986: 346). SIKET Siket [shiket]. ‘Deaf’. Also spelled Süket. Old Hungarian spelling: 1332 Syket-; 1338 Siketh; 1402 Syketh; 1418 Swketh;



92
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
1602 Süket. SIKLOS Siklós [shiklo:sh]. Derived from siklik ‘glide, slide’ the name denotes one who moves around effortlessly in a graceful manner. Old Hungarian spelling: 1404 -siclus; 1790 sikló. SIKOS Síkos [shi:kosh]. ‘Slippery’. The name indicates a shifty, unreliable person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1351 Sykus; 1470 Sykos; 1637 sikos. SIMA Síma [shi:ma]. ‘Smooth’. The name may have been given to a refined gentleman of good manners. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Sima. SIMIG Simig [shimig]. A probable variant of the Sumerian simug ‘blacksmith’ (Labat 1948: No. 338). SIP Síp [shi:p]. ‘Whistle’. A whistle-maker may have been called so. The Hungarian síp and the Sumerian šip ‘whistle’ (Labat 1948: No. 60) are identical in phonetic value and in meaning. SIPOS Sípos [shi:posh]. ‘Whistler, pipe-player’. The adjectival suffix -os indicates an occupation. See Sip for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1222 Sypus; 1416 Sypos; 1544 sipos-. SIR Sír [shi:r]. A person who had the tendency for crying over every little thing may have been called Sír ‘cry, weep’. The word sir may be a derivation from the Sumerian ir6, also marked as siš and šèš (Labat 1948: No. 581). The cuneiform signs are the combination of “water’ and ‘eye’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1300 syr-. SISA Sisa [shisha]. This name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. The three possible origins include the Sumerian šeš, Midle-Babylonian šiš ‘to polish, to rub’ (Labat 1948: No. 331) affixed by the adjectival suffix –a, that indicates an occupation: polisher. The present-day equivalent would be the surname Csiszár ‘polisher’, derived from csisz(-ol) ‘to polish. The Lebanese shisha may also be considered if the bearer of the name is a descendant of an immigrant from Lebanon. It is the name of a water pipe used for smoking flavored tobacco. A maker of such pipes may have received the name. Then there is the Persian šiša ‘bottle’ (Golden 2000: 162 #23) which may be considered. SISKA Siska [shishka]. A person having large flap-ears (Benkő 1967: 548, vol. 3) may have been called so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1419 Siska. SIVO Sívó [shi:vo:]. ‘Wretched’. The name contains the root si ‘crying, bitterly complaining’ (Benkő 1967: 530, vol. 3), the connecting consonant v and the participle -ó. Old Hungarian spelling: 1464 Sywo; 1627 sivu; 1791 Sívó. SOLTESZ Soltész [sholte:s]. An altered form of the German Schultheiß the title of a magistrate in villages of German immigrants in Szepes County of historical Hungary. Old Hungarian spelling: 1393 solteez; 1480 Solthyz; 1549 Sotez; 1689 Szoltisz. SOLYMAR Solymár [shojma:r]. ‘Falconer’. A keeper or trainer of falcons may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1266 Solomar; 1355 Salmar. SOMJEN Sömjén [shømje:n]. A dialectal variety of semlyék ‘marsh’. The name denotes



93
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
a dweller by a marsh. Old Hungarian spelling: 1244 Semek-; 1347 Semlek-; 1550 Semÿek; 1848 sömjék. SOMLO Somló [shomlo:]. A dweller near the Somló Mountain in Veszprém County may have been named so. The name embodies the root som, the connecting consonant l and the participle -ó. A mountain is prominently elevated near the town above the surrounding low hills, thus the name. Its name may have originated in Near Eastern languages. The Sumerian cuneiform for u5 ‘summit’ has changed to šam6 during the NeoAssyrian period (Labat 1948: No.78). Old Hungarian spelling: 1297 sumlo-. SOMOGY Somogy [shmodj]. Appears to be the altered form of the Sumerian simug ‘blacksmith’ (Labat 1948: No. 338). It is the name of a county in Hungary that may commemorate the name of its founder. The old Turkic čum ‘dogberry’ may also be considered, but is an unlikely source. Old Hungarian spelling: 1055 sumig, sumug; 1224 Symig. SOS Sós [sho:sh]. ‘Salt worker or seller’. Comprised of só ‘salt’ and the adjectival suffix -s. The Hungarian só and the Old Turkic şor ‘salt’ (Clauson 1972: 868) may be derived from the same source. Old Hungarian spelling: 1093 Sos-; 1254 Sous-; 1338 Sows; 1358 Sos-. SOROS Sörös [shørøsh]. A variant of Seres ‘maker/seller of beer’. See Seres. SUBA Suba [shuba]. The Hungarian suba is an ankle long, wide sheepskin-coat. The name may have been bestowed on a person for being associated with such a garment one way or another. The word was spelled szuba [suba] in 1585 (Benkő 1967: 609, vol. 3), that would point to the Akkadian subâtu ‘garment, cloak’ (Labat 1948: No. 536). But according to the Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language suba is an international loan-word present in many languages derived from the Arabic gubba. C.f. German schoube ‘long coat’, French jupe ‘skirt’ (Benkő 1967: 609, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1290 Suba; 1441 Swba; 1533 Suba. SUKE Süke [shyke]. ‘Deaf’. Archaic form of the present-day süket. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Suke, Syke, Sykue; 1838 Süke. SULE Süle [shyle]. The name may be derived from sül ‘bake, fry’ and denote an occupation. An alternative source would be the Sumerian šul ‘hero’ (Labat 1948: No. 467) to which the adjectival suffix -e is affixed. SUMEG Sümeg [shymeg]. Also spelled Sümegh. An occupational surname, probable variant of the Sumerian simug ‘blacksmith’ (Labat 1948: No. 338). It is also the name of a city in Veszprém County that may commemorate its blacksmith founder. Old Hungarian spelling: 1301 Symeg. SURANYI Surányi [shura:nji]. The derivational suffix -i is a frequent element in Hungarian surnames, it would indicate either a resident of Beregsurány, a locality in Szabolcs-Szatmár County, called Suran in 1279 (Kiss 1978: 104), or a dweller by a young forest. Surján means ‘young forest’ in the Hungarian language and sarj ‘young shoot’. The root sur is present in the Sumerian language in the form of šar and means ‘foliage’ (Labat 1948: No. 152).



94
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
SUTA Suta [shuta]. ‘Left-handed’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1468 Swta; 1655 suta. SUTI Süti [shyti]. The derivational suffix -i is affixed to süt ‘bake’ to indicate a descendant of a baker. Similar words exist in some of the Uralic languages (Benkő 1967: 634, vol. 3), but the ultimate origin may be the Sumerian šug ‘to bake, to cook’ (Gostony 1975: 84). SUVEGES Süveges [shyvegesh]. The maker or seller of cone shaped hats may have been called so. The initial element sü may be derived from the Sumerian si ‘horned’ (Labat 1948: No. 112), the second element vég means ‘end’ and the adjectival suffix -es indicates occupation in present-day Hungarian. The name describes the maker of headgears that have a pointed top like a horn. The cuneiform sign for the Sumerian si was pronounced šé [she:] during the old Babylonian period, later followed by ši [shi]. Old Hungarian spelling: 1082 Siweg; 1389 Syueges; 1488 Syweges; 1714 Süveges. SZABAD Szabad [sabad]. ‘Free’. A person free of feudal obligations may have been named so. The Hungarian szabad is shared with the Slavonic languages, cf. Slovakian slobodny, Serbo-Croatian slobodan, etc. Old Hungarian spelling: 1282 Zobod; 1368 zabad; 1597 szabad. SZABADKAI Szabadkai [sabadkai]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Szabadka a city in Bodrog County of historical Hungary. Confined to Serbia in 1920, its name was changed to Subotica. The city’s name means ‘free place’, comprised of szabad ‘free’ and the Sumerian ki ‘place’ (Labat 1948: No.461) modified to -ka according to vowel harmony. Old Hungarian spelling: 1391 Zabotka. SZABO Szabó [sabo:]. The participle -ó forms an adjective from the verb szab ‘to cut’ that denotes an occupation: szabó ‘tailor’ The word szab may be a derivative of the Sumerian šab ‘to cut’ (Labat 1948: No. 295k). The Middle-Babylonian sab corresponds to the Hungarian szab both in pronunciation and meaning. The Hungarian szabja ‘sabre’ is also derived from the same word, so is the name of a 9th century Magyar chieftain Szabolcs. Old Hungarian spelling: 1271 Zobov; 1292 Zobou; 1295 Zabow; 1383 Zabo; 1638 Szabo. SZAKACS Szakács [saka:ch]. ‘Cook’. The name may be comprised of the Sumerian šuku ‘food, fare’ (Deimel 1939: No. 285) and the Hungarian ács, an altered form of the adjectival suffix -ás [a:sh] that denotes an occupation. Similar words exist in the Slavonic languages like sokač ‘cook’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1240 Zakac; 1286 Zakach; 1638 Szakacz. SZAKAL Szakál [saka:l]. May be derived from the Sumerian sukal ‘high official, leader’ (Labat 1948: No.321). Not the same as Szakáll. SZAKALL Szakáll [saka:l]. ‘Beard’. The word szakáll is shared with the Altaic languages, cf. Turkish sakal ‘beard’, Uighur saqal, Mongol Saqa (Golden 2000: 205 #23). A relic from the distant past, a hybrid word that embodies the Sumerian su6 ‘beard’ (Labat 1948: No. 18) Neo-Assyrian súh ‘hair’ (Labat 1948: No.403) which are obviously cognate to the Hungarian szőr [sø:r] ‘hair’. The second element áll means



95
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
‘chin’. Hungarians differentiate between hair on top of the head called haj and the hair on other parts of the body; szőr. Szakáll falls into the latter category thus the need for descriptive definition. Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 Zacal; 1228 Zakal; 1602 Szakál; 1685 Szakáll. SZALAGOS Szalagos [salagosh]. ‘Ribbon-maker’. The adjectival suffix -os indicates an occupation. The sza [sa] segment of the Hungarian szalag has an identical phonetic value and meaning with the Sumerian sa (Deimel 1939: No. 83). The word appears to be comprised of the etymon sza, the connecting consonant l and the metathetic form of the Sumerian suffix -ga described by Lenormant (Lenormant 1877: 392). Cf. Ugric *śalɜ ‘strap, bracelet, to tie up’ (Benkő 1967: 658, vol. 3). SZALLASI Szállási [sa:la:shi]. The name denotes origin, one from the szállás ‘temporary living quarter’. It contains the following elements: száll ‘stay at’, the adjectival suffix -ás and the derivational suffix -i. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Zalas; 1229 Zallas; 1595 Szalas. SZAM Szám [sa:m]. An unusual surname, probably given to forerunners of accountants, or stock keepers, for the Hungarian szám means ‘number’. The word is a probable derivation from the Sumerian ám ‘a determination placed after the signs of ordinary numbers’ (Labat 1948: No. 579), but is also part of languages belonging to the Altaic family. Cf. Chuvash sum, Turkic, Turkmen, Nogai, Uighur san (Benkő 1967: 667, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1645 szám. SZANA Szana [sana]. The name embodies the root element szán ‘take pity, allot, give out’ and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a to indicate a compassionate person. Present-day spelling would be Szánó. The word szán is probably derived from the Sumerian sùn(-na) ‘to give, to donate’ (Labat 1948: No. 164). Identical phonetic forms in the Turkic languages mean ‘consider, wish, desire’. The Historical-Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language originates the Hungarian szán from them (Benkő 1967: 672, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1229 Sana. SZANTO Szántó [sa:nto:]. The participle -ó forms an adjective from szánt ‘plow’, the resultant name denotes an occupation: plough-man, tiller’. The word szánt may have been derived from the Sumerian sun ‘to split, to break, to tear up’ (Gostony 1975: 79). Old Hungarian spelling: 1109 Zamthov; 1211 Zamthou, Samthou; 1239 Zampto; 1354 Zantho; 1638 szanto. SZAPARY Szapáry [sapa:ri]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a descendant of Szapár, or one belonging to that family. The surname is probably a variant of Sabar or Sabir, the name of an ancient people of Asian origin. Their name is probably derived from the Sumerian su-bir but is also part of the vocabulary of the Altaic language group. When the Sumerian cuneiform sign for edin ‘plain, steppe’ is combined with the sign for su ‘family, multitude’ (Labat 1948: No. 7), then the pronunciation is not su-edin



96
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
but su-bir4 (Labat 1948: No.168) and may mean ‘people of the steppe’. But, if it is derived from the Turkic sapmak ‘to depart, to go astray’ as suggested by Gyula Németh (Németh 1990: 72-75), then it could assume the form sapar when applied to a group of people who separated from the main branch. SZARAZ Száraz [sa:raz]. A reserved uncommunicative person may have been named Száraz ‘dry’, a word shared with other Finno-Ugric languages. The reconstructed protoword is *śarɜ (Benkő 1967: 178, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1082 Zarros-; 1227 Zaroz-; 1256 Zaraz. SZEKALLO Székálló [se:ka:lo:]. ‘Butcher’s shop-man’ (Bárczi és Országh 1959-1962: 136, vol. 6). An antiquated name for the person serving customers in a butcher’s shop. It is comprised of szék ‘chopping block’ and álló ‘standing’ i.e. one who stands and works by the chopping block. SZEKELY Székely [se:kej]. Name of the indigenous Hungarian-speaking population of Transylvania (called Erdély in the Hungarian language). Erdély was an integral part of Hungary until 1920 when it was confined to Romania by the Treaty of Trianon. The origin of the name has been explained in many different ways but only Sumerian, Celtic, Latin and Turkish etymologies seem to provide satisfactory results. One such explanation is from József Thúry (cited by Németh 1990:103), who traced the name to the Chagatai Turk sikil ‘noble, racially pure, son of a king, prince’. This etymology is utilized by Németh to emphasize the meaning ‘tribe of the prince’ instead of ‘racially pure’. He argues that other interpretations would not fit into the Turkic onomastic system for tribal names. However, the ‘racially pure’ concept would be the one supported by the Sumerian sikil ‘pure, unadulterated’ (Labat 1948: 564). This is also supported by the Celtic Dacie ‘clear, pure’ survived as a girl’s name in Irish-Gaelic, a variant of the English Dacey and Latin Candace (McLaughlin 2011: 71). The Romans conquered the Transylvanian Daci in AD 107 but abandoned the land in AD 270. There is no evidence that the original Daci have disappeared or relocated to another area. The old Turkish eskil and izgil have also been considered and traced to a group of Turks who lived by the River Volga (Kiss 1978: 601). Eski means ‘old, ancient’ in current Turkish and eskiler ‘the ancients’ (Akdikmen 1986: 125). Old Hungarian spelling: 1092 Scichul; 1095 Scicul; 1308 Zecul; 1310 Zekul; 1353 Zekel; 1361 Scekel; 1408 Zekely (Szamota 1902-1906: 900); 1794 Székely (Benkő 1967: 710, vol. 3). SZEKER Szekér [seke:r]. ‘Wagon’. A maker of such vehicles may have been named so. The Hungarian szekér may be a distorted form of the Sumerian gigir ‘wagon’ (Labat 1948: No. 551, 486). The Middle- Iranian *sakar ‘wagon’ (Benkő 1967: 707, vol. 3) may also be considered. Old Hungarian spelling: 1451 Zerer-; 1597 szeker-; 1616 szekèr; 1669 szekér. SZEKFU Szekfű [sekfy:]. ‘Carnation’. A grower of carnations may have been named so. The name of the flower is a composite of szék a variant of szik ‘saline soil’ and fű ‘grass’. The carnation’s ability to grow in saline soil and its grass-like leafs are responsible for the name. Old Hungarian spelling: 1380 zekfw; 1493 Zekfy; 1522 zegffy; 1531 zegfw; 1544 szek fü-; 1560 Szegfü.



97
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
SZELE Szele [sele]. The word-final element -e is the variant of the participle -ó modified by of vowel harmony. The participle turns a vowel into an action noun that denotes occupation, thus Szele signifies ‘one who cuts’. The word szel ‘to cut, to slice’ is present in the Finno-Ugric branch of the Uralic family of languages. Cf. Vogul (Mańsi) sil- ‘cut, slice’, Finn säliä ‘split’ (Benkő 1967: 704, vol. 3). However the ultimate origin may be the Sumerian sil ‘to cut up’ (Deimel 1939: No. 11) ‘to split’ (Labat 1948: No. 12). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Sele, Selle, Seleh, Zele, Zeleh. SZEMERE Szemere [semere]. The Hungarian Anonymus chronicler (c. 1200) mentions the clan zemera [sic] in section 6 and 33. It may be the archaic form of the present-day Turkish semer ‘stout’ (Akdikmen 1986: 326). The word-final element -e is the archaic Hungarian and ancient Sumerian adjectival suffix -a modified by vowel harmony. The estate of the Szemere clan was situated in the area encompassing the towns of Hoba and Szemere during the reign of the Árpád dynasty (896-1301) (Györffy 1975: 289). The town of Szemere is in Borsod-Abaúj- Zemplén County. Old Hungarian spelling: 1243 Scemere. SZEMES Szemes [semesh]. The adjectival suffix -es is affixed to szem ‘eye’ to indicate a ‘clever, sharp, shrewd’ person (Bárczi és Országh 1959-1962: 177, vol.6). An alternative etymology would link Szemes with the Sumerian sim ‘to sift, to riddle’ (Labat 1948: No. 79) and še ‘grain’ (Labat 1948: No. 367). The Sumerian sim denotes the action of winnowing grain or sorting seed by hand. A person doing it would be called Szemes. Old Hungarian spelling: 1135 Scemus; 1211 Scimis, Semes, Zemus; 1400 Zemes; 1698 szemes-. SZENDE Szende [sende]. ‘Gentle, tolerant’. The word szende was coined during the 19th century language standardization when new words were derived from existing ones (Benkő 1967: 721 vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1823 Szende. SZENTE Szente [sente]. The name contains the root szent ‘saint’ and the derivational suffix -i changed to -e by vowel harmony. The name denotes a descendant of Szent. The Hungarian szent is derived from the Latin sanctus ‘sacred’ like the English saint.
Old Hungarian spelling: 1156 scent; 1176 Zent; 1195 szent. SZEP Szép [se:p]. ‘Beautiful, comely’. CF. Persian ziba ‘beautiful, comely’ (Lambton 1988: 83). Old Hungarian spelling: 1116 Scip-; 1138 Zep-; 1416 zep; 1439 Zeep-; 1602 Szép. SZEPESI Szepesi [sepeshi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from the Szepes region of historical Hungary. Confined to Slovakia in 1920 and renamed Špišská. The name of the region is derived from the Hungarian szép ‘beautiful’ to which the adjectival suffix -es is affixed. See Szep for etymology. Old Hungarian spelling: 1279 Zepus. SZERB Szerb [serb]. ‘Serbian’. The name Serb is cognate with Sorb. The ethnic denominator Sorb is habitually used as a synonym for Wends, a people of Slavic origin living in northern Germany. The name Szerb entered the Hungarian lexicon after the battle of Kosovo in 1389



98
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
when Serbian refugees settled in the southern regions of historical Hungary. That area (Bácska and part of Bánát) was confined to Szerbia in 1920. Old Hungarian spelling: 1405 Szirb; 1516 Zerb-; 1783 Szerbi. SZERI Szeri [seri]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Szer, a frequent element of place names in the Carpathian Basin. Those place names were given in recognition that once a szer ‘diet’ was convoked there. Presence of the word szer is verifiable in both the Uralic and Sumerian languages having identical phonetic values and meanings that express the actions of a governing body. In the Hungarian language szer [ser] means ‘public body, proceedings, assembly’ while in Obdorski Ostyak sír [si:r] and śír [shi:r]. mean ‘custom, rule, law, statute’ (Benkő 1967: 730, vol. 3). The Sumerian pictograph transliterated as sar5 by René Labat (Labat 1948: No. 86), modified to šar5 by Florence Malbran-Labat (1988) and dated to the Ur period, is a vertical line intersected by two parallel horizontal lines. Its meaning is: rule ruler, order. The pictograph looks very much the same as the Hungarian insignia for the head of state and still being used as part of the coat of arms of the country. Another Sumerian pictograph that has the same look is pronounced šár ‘prince’, but the reduplicated form šár-šár is translated as ‘to discuss, to debate’ which is tantamount to the Hungarian ‘proceedings’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1015 Zeru; 1138 scer; 1150 Scerii; 1340 -zyr; 1385 zeer; 1872 szer-. SZERZE Szerze [serze]. A variant of szerző ‘writer, author’. The word is a composite of the Sumerian sar ‘to write’ (Deimel 1939: No. 118) the connecting consonant z and the participle -ő that was modified to -e by vowel harmony. Old Hungarian spelling: 1604 Szerző. SZIGET Sziget [siget]. A dweller on an island may have been referred to as Sziget ‘island’. It is a probable derivation from the Sumerian zìg ‘to rise, to stand up’ (Thomsen 1984: 322), or zi(g)-(ga) ‘heaving’ (Labat 1948: No. 84) to which the formative -et was affixed to form a derived noun from a verb. Considering that an island rises or stands above the water the Sumerian origin seems plausible. Old Hungarian spelling: 1055 ziget; 1109 Sciget; 1240 Zyget; 1293 zygueth; 1320 szygeth; 1322 sciget; 1521 szygeth. SZIJJARTO Szíjjártó [si:ja:rto:]. Archaic form of Szijgyártó ‘harness-maker’. Anglicized form is Seyarto. The name is comprised of szij ‘strap’, járt/gyárt ‘make, manufacture’ and the participle -ó. The participle -ó forms adjectives and names of occupations from verbs. See next entry for the etymology of szíj. The Hungarian gyár ‘factory’ and gyárt ‘make, manufacture’ are identical with the Sumerian gar ‘make, manufacture’ (Deimel 1939: No. 336). Cf. Turkish yaratmak ‘to create’, yaratan ‘maker, creator’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1322 Ziugartho-; 1375 Zyugyartou; 1697 Szijárto-. SZIJJ Szíjj [si:j]. The reduplicated j marks a stressed consonant. The Hungarian szíj ‘strap’ is derived from szil [sil], szel ‘to cut, to slice’. The word is shared with other Uralic languages, the reconstructed proto-word is *sale (Benkő 1967: 704, vol. 3). However, the Sumerian sil [sil] ‘to split, to cut up’ (Labat 1948: No. 12) may be the ultimate source of the word.



99
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1270 ziu-; 1451 Zy-; 1567 szij-. SZILADI Sziládi [sila:di]. Also spelled Szilády. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicate a dweller in or by a szilád ‘A place abundant in elm trees’. The root szil ‘elm’ is followed by the archaic adjectival suffix -a and the suffix -d that denotes a place. The palatal equivalent of this suffix is -gy in Szilágyi. This surname may have originated in the marshy region of Szilágyság, an area in historical Hungary confined to Romania in 1920. It is now part of the Satù Mare administrative district. The original Hungarian name has been abolished. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Sila; 1246 Scylag; 1494 Zilagysag. SZILAS Szilas [silash]. Appears to be the archaic form of szíjgyártó ‘strap-maker’, derived from the Sumerian sil ‘to split, to cut up’. See Szijjarto and Szijj for more information. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Sila. SZIRAK Szirák [sira:k]. Derived from Szír [sir] the Turkic name of a people, recorded on the Orkhon monument c. A.D. 720 in Mongolia. Its exact meaning has not been established (Györffy 1975: 278). SZOGI Szögi [søgi]. Also spelled Szegi and Szeghy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Szög. The word szög ‘nail’ is shared with other languages of the Uralic language family. A possible source of szög is the Sumerian sìg ‘to hit, to stab, to squeeze’ (Labat 1948: No. 295) which describes the action necessary to drive a nail into wood or other material. The name Szög may have been given to a maker of nails, spikes or other piercing implements such as szigony ‘harpoon’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1067 Sceg-; 1358 Zege; 1575 szëg. SZOMJAS Szomjas [somjash]. ‘Thirsty’. The name may have been bestowed on a heavy drinker. The adjectival suffix -as denotes personal characteristics when affixed to szomj ‘thirst’. The word szomj may be a variant of the Akkadian sûmû ‘thirst’ (Labat 1948: No. 28). Old Hungarian spelling: 1389 Zomyw; 1668 Szomjú. SZONDY Szondy [sondi]. The Turkish son ‘end, termination’ is followed by the suffix -d that denotes a place and the archaic derivational suffix -y. The name indicates one from the frontier or a remote place. The Hungarian surname Véghely is equivalent of the Turkish Szondy. SZUHAI Szuhai [suhai]. Szuha is the name of a tributary of the creek Sajó in Hungary. A dweller by the Szuha may have been named so. The derivational suffix -i indicates geographic origin. Szuha cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language, but is compatible with the Sumerian sùh ‘muddy, murky’ (Labat 1948: No.569). The SumeroHungarian adjectival suffix -a indicates condition. An appropriate name for a murky stream. It should also be noted that a means ‘water’ in the Sumerian language. The Slavonic origin from sucha ‘dry’ (Kiss 1978: 621) [proper spelling: suchy] is not convincing. Another Hungarian river in the same region Zagyva ‘clouded, murky, unclear’ emulates the name Suha. Old Hungarian spelling: 1292 Szuha-; 1299 Suha.



100
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
SZULE Szüle [syle]. The participle -e is suffixed to the root szül ‘give birth’ to indicate a ‘parent’. Szüle is an altered form of the prevalent szülő. The Sumerian sal-la ‘parent’ (Gostony 1975: 56) is the likely source of the word. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Sileu, Silev, Silleu; 1483 Zyle. SZURDOKI Szurdoki [surdoki]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a dweller by the szurdok ‘ravine, canyon’. The word-final element -ok is an inactive formative. Surdok may be derived from the Sumerian sùr ‘passage for water’ (Labat 1948: No. 405). Similar words are in other Ugric languages, the reconstructed proto-word is *śorɜ (Benkő 1967: 787, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 Zorduc; 1267 Zurduk-; 1601 Szurdok. SZUROMI Szuromi [suromi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from the szurom that has no meaning in the present-day Hungarian language but may be derived from the Sumerian šurim ‘farm, fold, pen’ (Deimel 1939: No. 101). The name would then denote one from the farm. SZUSZA Szusza [susa]. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language to a high degree of certainty. It is a possible derivation from szusz ‘breath, wind’ to which the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a is affixed to indicate a person who breathes fast and audibly. Another possibility is Susa, the name of an ancient city, capital of the kingdom of Elam, whose origins lie in prehistoric times. The remains of ancient Susa are in Iran at the village of Shush (Biblical Shuhsan), near the confluence of the Tigris and Euphrates rivers, 160 miles north of Abadan. The city was founded around 4000 B.C. The name is too old to ascertain the motivation behind it. A person from Susa may have been named so. TABI Tabi [tabi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Tab, a town in Somogy County. The town’s name may commemorate its founder. It is probably an older form of the Hungarian több ‘more, a number of, good many’ (Kiss 1978: 624). It may be a continuation of the Sumerian tab ‘to double, to multiply, twins’ (Labat 1948: No. 124). The appellation may denote one of the twins. Old Hungarian spelling: 1320 Tob; 1211 Thob. TAGANYI Tagányi [taga:nji]. The derivational suffix -i denotes a descendant of Tagány. The suffix -ány forms nouns from verbs when they are the instrument of action. The Hungarian tag(-ol) ‘dismember, break up’ is identical with the Sumerian tag ‘to break’ (Labat 1948: No. 126) both in phonetic value and in meaning.
