The Hungarian root tag means ‘limb, member, part’. The name may denote a butcher who dismembers the carcass, or any other occupation that involves the separation of a whole into parts. The Hungarian tagló ‘poleax, cleaver’ is also derived from the same root. TAKACS Takács [taka:ch]. An occupational name that means ‘weaver, fabric maker’. The name is comprised of tak, probably derived from the Sumerian túg (Neo-Babylonian túk) ‘fabric, cloth’ (Labat 1948: No. 536). The word-final element ács [a:ch] means ‘carpenter’ as an independent Hungarian word. However, it may be safe to suggest that when denoting occupations, it is the altered form of the adjectival suffix -ás [a:sh]. The change from [sh] to [ch] is affected by affrication. Hungarians share the word takács with the Slavonic languages, cf. Polish tkacz,



101
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Serbo-Croatian tkaš, Slovakian tkáš ‘weaver’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1330 Thakach; 1377 Takach-; 1648 takács. TAKARO Takaró [takaro:]. The literal translation of takaró is ‘cover’ but it is used metaphorically to denote an evasive person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Thocorou; 1369 Takaro. TAKSONY Taksony [takshonj]. Originally the name of a Turkish chieftain in medieval Hungary. The town of Taksony in Pest was named after him. The name is of Turkish origin, cf. toqšin, toqšun ‘wild, unrestrainable’ (Kiss 1978: 626). Old Hungarian spelling: 950 Taxin; 1270 Toxun. TALAS Tálas [ta:lash]. The adjectival suffix -as indicates a maker or seller of dishes when affixed to tál ‘dish, platter’. The word tál is part of the Ugric branch of the Uralic family of languages as well as the New Persian vocabulary; cf. Upper-Loswa dialect of Vogul (Mańsi) tulana ‘wooden bowl’, New Persian tal ‘bowl, platter’ (Benkő 1967: 826, vol. 3). However, all three may be derived from the Sumerian dal ‘potter’s wheel’, dugdal ‘vase’ (Labat 1948: No. 86). Old Hungarian spelling: 1402 Thalas; 1429 Tallas; 1459 Talas; 1634 Tálas. TALTOS Táltos [ta:ltosh]. The name denotes a member of the Pre-Christian Hungarian priestly caste. The initial element tál may be derived from the Sumerian tál ‘wisdom’ (Labat 1948: No. 383) followed by the connecting -t- and the adjectival suffix -os that indicates status and rank. It may be noteworthy that the original Sumerian pictograph is like the bishop’s hat. The title táltos was bestowed on sages, wizards, medicine man and spiritual leaders in old Hungarian society. Similar words are in the Ugric branch of the Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *tultɜ ‘magic’ (Benkő 1967: 832, vol. 3). TAMI Támi [ta:mi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a descendant of Tám ‘a supporter’. A parallel may be drawn with the Sumerian dim ‘pillar, bond, link’ (Labat 1948: No. 94). The Middle-Assyrian tam ‘pillar, bond, link’ is identical with the Hungarian phonetic value. The Hungarian gyám ‘guardian’ appears to be a variant of the Sumerian dim. TANDARI Tandari [tandari]. Also spelled Tandori. A probable derivation from tandoori a type of oven used throughout Asia. TAPAI Tápai [ta:pai]. Also spelled Tápay. The derivational suffix -i indicates one from Tápé, a town in Csongrád County, now part of Szeged. The name is of Turkic origin, cf. old Turkish, Uighur tapay ‘service’ (Kiss 1978: 628). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Tapaí. TAR Tar [tar]. Also spelled Tarr. The name may have been bestowed on a bald person. The Hungarian tar ‘barren, bald’ is identical with the Sumerian tar ‘to destroy, to cut’ (Labat 1948: No.12). This relationship is exemplified by the Hungarian verb tarol ‘to make barren, to destroy’ and tarló ‘stubble-field’. Similar words are in the Turkic languages in the form of taz, daz ‘bald’ (Benkő 1967: 847, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1229 Tor; 1315 Thor-; 1341 Taar; 1344 Thaar; 1371 Tar. TARKA Tarka [tarka]. ‘Motley’. A person regularly wearing mottled garb may have been named so. The name contains the root tar, the connecting consonant k and the adjectival suffix -a. The source of the word may be the Sumerian dar, dar-a ‘motley’ (Labat 1948: No. 114).



102
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Similar words are in the Turkic languages: Kazakh tarlan ‘spotty (horse)’; Kirghiz tarlan ‘grey’; Karachai talyïr ‘motly’; Osmanli dirgil ‘multicolored’; Mongolian tar-lan ‘motley’ (Benkő 1967: 854, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1470 Tharka; 1602 Tarka. TAS Tas [tash]. A name of Turkish origin: taş ‘stone, rock’. One of the seven chieftains that entered the Carpathian Basin in 896 was named Tas. TASZAJTO Taszajtó [tasajto:]. Regional variety of taszító ‘pushing, shoving’. A hustler may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1523 Thazytho. TATAR Tatár [tata:r]. ‘Tartar’. The name is used in Hungary as a synonym for a Mongolian. Old Hungarian spelling: 1176 Tathar; 1211 Tatar. TAUSZ Tausz [taus]. A name of Persian origin: ta’us ‘peacock’ (Lambton 1988: 320). The name may have been assigned to a breeder of pea-fowls. TAVASZI Tavaszi [tavasi]. Also spelled Tavaszy. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) marks a descendant of Tavasz ‘spring’. A person born in the spring may have received the name. Listed as a Uralic word in Hungarian dictionaries having a reconstructed protoword *toβkɜ. However, there is a strong possibility that the Akkadian Tammuz, derived from the Sumerian dDumu-zi the ‘god of spring’ (Labat 1948: No. 144) is the source. Another sign, the cuneiform for dumu whose semantic field includes ‘newborn’ and the phonetic form zi means ‘life’ (Labat 1948: No. 84). Spring is the season for plants to bud and begin to grow and for animal life to bring forth of offsprings. Life starts anew in the spring and the Sumerians may have called this season appropriately dumu-zi ‘newborn life’ which became Tammuz in the Akkadian culture at a later date and the name of their God for this season. The name of a Hungarian leader was Tonuzoba (c. AD 1000). The name may be a distorted form of Tammuz-aba ‘father-spring’, cf. Hungarian Tavasz-apa. TEGLAS Téglás [te:gla:sh]. The root of the word tégla ‘brick’ is most likely derived from the Turkish tugla ‘brick’. The word-final element -s is an adjectival suffix that indicates occupation, thus ‘brick maker’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1334 Tyglas-; 1347 Teglas. TEKENOS Tekenős [tekenø:sh]. Antiquated form of present-day teknős ‘maker of troughs and wash tubs’. The name is derived from the Turkish tekne ‘trough, vat’. The suffix -s indicates occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1430 Thekenews; 1458 Thekenes; 1521 Tekewnos; 1669 tekenyős. TEL Tél [te:l]. The name may have been bestowed on a person born in the winter. The Hungarian tél ‘winter’ is classified by linguists as a word of Uralic origin. The reconstructed proto-word is *tälβä ‘winter. But there is a good reason to consider the Sumerian til ‘end, old’ as the source. The cuneiform sign for til was used for expressing the passing of time, cessation, death and completion (Labat 1948: No. 69). The Hungarian tél is phonetically almost identical to til and could easily be associated with the last season of the year when vegetation goes dormant and animals start to hibernate. ‘Old man winter’ is a popular



103
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
expression for this season in many languages. Old Hungarian spelling: 1269 tel-. TELEK Telek [telek]. The name denotes the owner of a telek ‘parcel of land’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 theluch; 1206 theluc-; 1211 -thelegu, Telug; 1224 Teluk; 1228 telek-. TELEPI Telepi [telepi]. The resident of a telep ‘settlement’ may have been named so. The word-final element -i is the derivational suffix. Old Hungarian spelling: 1456 telep. TEMES Temes [temesh]. Dweller by the river Temes may have been named so. The Hungarian temes appears to be a variant of tömés derived from töm ‘fill, stuff’. The adjectival suffix -es indicates condition. In case of the river Temes in the Bánság district of historical Hungary the name may refer to the sandy deposits that gradually build up in its bed. This area was confined to Romania in 1920 and the name changed to Timiş. Identical words are in other Uralic languages, cf. Eastern-Mari (Cheremis) temem ‘fill, stuff’ (Benkő 1967: 960, vol. 3) but they all show close affiliation with the Sumerian temen ‘earth-fill, earthwork’ (Labat 1948: No. 376). Old Hungarian spelling: 1150 Temes. TEMESVARI Temesvári [temeshva:ri]. Also spelled Temesváry. One from Temesvár, a city by the river Temes in historical Hungary, may have been named so. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates origin. The meaning of Temesvár is ‘fort made of earthwork’, synonymous with Földvár ‘earthwork’. See above for more information. The element vár may be of Sumerian origin, cf. bár ‘circular wall’ (Deimel 1939: No. 49). Old Hungarian spelling: 1315 Temeswar. TENGERDY Tengerdy [tengerdi]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from Tengerd ‘place by the sea’ as marked by the grammatical designator -d that denotes a place. The Hungarian tenger ‘sea’ was most likely whittled out of the Sumerian engur ‘sea, ocean’ (Labat 1948: No. 484). Old Hungarian spelling: 1152 Tengurdi; 1211 Tengurd; 1256 Tengerd. TERENYI Terényi [tere:nji]. Also spelled Trényi. The derivational suffix -i indicates a dweller by a terény derived from tér ‘park, field, ground’. The suffix -ény converts a noun into a collective noun. The Hungarian tér is identical with the Sumerian tir ‘park, field, ground’ (Labat 1948: No. 375). TEVELY Tevely [teveli]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicate a person from Tevel. A village named Tevel is in Veszprém County and another is in Tolna County. Both villages preserve the Turkish name of their founder Tevel, grandson of Árpád, the founder of the dynasty named after him (Németh 1990: 79). Old Hungarian spelling: 905-959 Teveli. THOKOLY Thököly [tøkøli]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from Tököl a community in Pest County. The name is derived from the old Turkish tükäl ‘faultless, complete’ (Kiss 1978: 656). Old Hungarian spelling: 1272 Thukul. THURY Thúry [tu:ri]. The archaic derivational suffix indicates a descendant of Thúr derived from túr. Though the verb túr ‘to dig, to poke’ exist in the Hungarian language, it would be an unlikely choice for a surname. The Sumerian tur ‘small, short’ (Labat 1948:



104
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
No. 144) is the most likely source. It may also be the root element of törpe ‘dwarf, pigmy’. TIBOR Tibor [tibor]. A possible derivation from the Sumerian tibir ‘hand, palm’ (Labat 1948: No. 126). The Hungarian tenyér ‘palm of the hand’ shows a close resemblance to the Sumerian tibir. A person having bigger than average hands may have received the name. The name’s endearing form Tibi is almost identical with tibir. The Roman source Tibur, the ancient name of Tivoli a commune near Rome, as listed in the Book of Hungarian Forenames (Ladó 1978: 21) is possible but unlikely. TIKASZ Tikász [tika:s]. Archaic form of tyúkász ‘poultry-farmer’. The formative suffix ász converts a noun into occupation. Tik, tyúk mean ‘chicken, hen’. Similar words exist in the Turkic languages cf. Turkish tavuk, Uighur takïyu (Benkő 1967: 1020, vol. 3). The ultimate source may be the Sumerian ti-ku ‘chicken, hen’ (Gostony 1975: 140). Old Hungarian spelling: 1109 Tikus; 1275 Tykus; 1452 Thywkws; 1482 Thykos. TIMAR Tímár [ti:ma:r]. ‘Tanner, skin dresser’. The word tímár is shared with the Turkish timar ‘to dress leather after it is tanned’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 timar, Timar. TINODI Tinódi [tino:di]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Tinód a town in Transylvania. Confined to Romania in 1920 and renamed Tinăud. The toponym consists of tinó ‘young male ox’ and the suffix -d that denotes a place. The Hungarian tinó has counterparts in the Chuvash tina, Chagatai tana and in other Turkic languages (Benkő 1967: 920, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1219 Tinod. TOKAR Tokár [toka:r]. Derived from the Slavonic tokar ‘turner’, one who worked with a lathe. TOKODI Tokodi [tokodi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Tokod, a town in Komárom County. The place name is derived from tok ‘case, box’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1181 Tokod. TOLGYESI Tölgyesi [tøldjeshi]. Also spelled Tölgyesy and Tölgyessy. The name is comprised of the initial element tölgy ‘oak’, the adjectival suffix -es and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). The name denotes a descendant of a dweller by the oak grove. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Tulgus-; 1373 Telgyes; 1460 Thewlges; 1602 Tölgyes. TOMA Toma [toma]. The name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. The element tom may be derived from the Sumerian túm ‘to supply, to provide’ (Labat 1948: No.206). The Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a indicates an occupation. Toma may have been a wholesaler in antiquity. TOMLOS Tömlős [tømlø:sh]. The adjectival suffix -s indicates a maker of tömlő ‘water or wine bag made out of animal skin’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1374 Temles-. TORDA Torda [torda]. The name of a city in Aranyos-Torda County of historical Hungary. This area was confined to Romania in 1920 and the name was changed to Turda. This spelling agrees with the old Hungarian spelling Turda in 1075 (Kiss 1978: 652). The initial element tor may be derived from the Sumerian tur5 ‘entrance’ (Labat 1948: No. 58) to which the grammatical designator -da is suffixed to indicate a place.



105
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
The city is near a major geological crevice and the city’s location may have been the most suitable place in antiquity to gain passage to the area beyond. TOREK Törek [tørek]. The word törek ‘chaff’ has no probable reason to form a surname. The suffix -ek forms nouns from verbs. The verb tör ‘to break, smash’ may be derived from the Sumerian tar ‘to break, to slice’ (Labat 1948: No.12). A stone-breaker, -crusher may have been named so. TORMA Torma [torma]. ‘Horse-radish’. A grower of horse-radish may have been named so. The word is also present in the Turkic languages, cf. Uighur turma. Old Hungarian spelling: 1437 Thorma; 1530 Torma. TOROK Török [tørøk]. The ethnic name Török ‘Turk’ may have originated in Uighur, one of the Turkic languages, in which türk means ‘powerful, strong’ (Németh 1990: 41). This name may have been conferred on the Turkish nation in recognition of their military might. The Ottoman Turks use the same name for themselves. Turkish presence can be observed in Hungary from the beginning of their written history. Most of the founding tribes of the Kingdom of Hungary belonged to the Turkic ruling class and mingled with the indigenous Magyars. Another Turkish infusion occurred during the 150 years of Turkish rule in most parts of historical Hungary beginning in 1526. Old Hungarian spelling: 1055 turku; 1211 Turk; 1239 Thevrek; 1364 Turek; 1390 Terek; 1411 Thwruk; 1444 Thewrewk; 1476 Twrwk; 1560 Thörök; 1610 török. TORPE Törpe [tørpe]. ‘Dwarf, pigmy’. The name cannot be etymologized in the Hungarian language. May be a derivation from the Sumerian tur ‘small, short’ (Labat 1948: No. 144). Törpe is comprised of the root tör, the connecting consonant p and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -e (variant of -a). Old Hungarian spelling: 1525 Terpe; 1594 Teörpe. TOT Tót [to:t]. Also spelled Tóth. The old Hungarian name for a Teuton (German), erroneously applied to Slovaks. The term originally referred to the Teutonic tribes who lived in parts of north-western Hungary in the Carpathian Mountains as late as the 6th century A.D (Györffy 1977: 27). The name is an indication that the pre-Slavic population of that area was of Teutonic descent. Introduced during the early periods of contact between the indigenous Magyars and Teutons, the name Tót is so firmly established by tradition that it is able to eclipse the more recent name Slovak. Old Hungarian spelling: 1121 Tout; 1219 Tot; 1240 Thout; 1297 Toth. TUNDERES Tündéres [tynde:resh]. ‘Like a fairy’. The name is derived from tündér ‘fairy’ to which the adjectival suffix -es is affixed to indicate likeness. The word tündér is a combination of the verb tűn ‘appear, materialize’, the connecting consonant d and the deverbative -er. The Hungarian tündér may be compared to the Sumerian dingir ‘determinative preceding names of divinities’ (Malbran-Labat 1988: No. 13). An alternative etymology would be tünd(-öklő) ‘shine, glow, glitter’. Tünde a popular Hungarian female forename is derived from the root tűnd. Old Hungarian spelling: 1416 tunder-; 1571 tündör-; 1652 tundir-. TURUL Turul [turul]. The name of a hawk the Hun and Hungarian nobility of Turkic origin used for hunting game birds and small animals for sport. The Hungarian turul is an altered form of the Turkish toğrul [to:rul] ‘goshawk’ (Akdikmen 1986: 375). It is the



106
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
largest species of the accipiters. Old Hungarian spelling: 1274 Turul. TUSKO Tuskó [tushko:]. A small but stocky person may have been named so. The Hungarian tuskó ‘stump, root timber’ may be derived from the Sumerian tuš ‘base’ (Labat 1948: No. 536). TUZA Tuza [tuza]. ‘Salt merchant’. Comprised of the Turkish tuz ‘salt’ (Akdikmen 1986: 380) and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a. UDENYI Üdényi [yde:nji]. Dialectic form of idényi ‘seasonal worker’. Derived from üdő/idő ‘time, duration’ to which the nominal suffix -ny is affixed to indicate a period of action. The word-final element -i marks a derived noun. The Hungarian idő appears to be cognate with similar words in the Turkic languages, cf. Uighur öd ‘time’ (Benkő 1967: 188, vol. 2). See additional information below. UDO Üdő [ydø:]. Dialectic form of idő ‘time, duration’. A punctual person may have received the name. The word-final element -ő is the participle -ó modified by vowel harmony. The root üd, id is identical with the Sumerian ud ‘time duration’ (Labat 1948: No. 380). Old Hungarian spelling: 1372 ÿdew, ewdew; 1456 ÿde; 1649 ydü; 1794 üdü, üde. UDVARHELYI Udvarhelyi [udvarheji]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from an udvarhely, a name exclusively used for the royal lodges in the country for temporary residences of the king or his representative and his entourage. The name is a compound of udvar ‘court, yard’ and hely ‘place’. The Hungarian udvar, spelled hudwor in 1075, is shared with similar words in the Slavonic languages, cf. Serbo-Croat dvor court and yard’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1204 Wduorhel; 1263 vduarhely; 1373 Oduorhel; 1462 Vdwarhel. UGI Ugi [ugi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Ug a place in medieval Hungary in Baranya County, now part of Szársomló. Spelled Wgh in 1287 (Györffy 1987: 398). UGRAI Ugrai [ugrai]. ‘A person from Ugra, name of the region where the Vogul (Man’si) and Ostyak (Chanti) people live as recorded by Arab sources in the 10th century and by European scholars in the 15th and 16th centuries (Benkő 1967: 1025, vol. 3). The area is in western Siberia on the north side of the Ob River near the confluence of the Irtis. Variants of Ugrai were recorded in 1138 as Vgrin, Vgrun, Vgron (Fehértói 1983: 355-356). UJJ Ujj [uj]. Derived from új ‘new’ the name may have been bestowed on a newly arrived person to town. The reduplication of j marks a stressed consonant. Old Hungarian spelling: 1193 Vi; 1222 Wi; 1230 Huy; 1240 Vy; 1250 -uy; 1731 új-. UKK Ükk [yk]. Variant of ük ‘great-great grandparent’, synonymous with ‘ancestor, forbear’. The reduplicated k marks a stressed consonant. Considered to be of Ugric origin, the reconstructed proto-word is *ikkɜ (Benkő 1967: 1050, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Ikeh; 1215 Yku; 1395 yk; 1838 Uk. UNGHY Unghy [ungi]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates a person from Ung,



107
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
the name of a County in historical Hungary. Confined to the Ukraine in 1945, the name was discontinued. The h marks the reduced plosion of the preceding g. Spelled Hung and Vng in 1150 (Kiss 1978: 670), the meaning of the name is obscure, but shows strong resemblance to the Sumerian unu(g) ‘dwelling, habitation’ (Deimel 1939: No.136). The Sumerian city of Uruk was also referred to as unug-ki (Biblical Erech). UR Úr [u:r]. The Hungarian úr means ‘refined gentleman, sir, lord, proprietor’ synonymous with wealth, power and authority. It is a much used honorific title, like Mister and Sir in the English language. A variant is present in the Finnish language: uros ‘male, man, hero’ (Benkő 1967: 1035, vol. 3). But its Middle Eastern origin is more apparent: Sumerian u (unum) ‘lord, master’ (Deimel 1939: No. 247), ur ‘man, hero’ (Labat 1948: No. 575), Egyptian ur ‘great, superior’ (Budge 1978: 170, vol. 1). Old Hungarian spelling: 1135 ur-; 1184 vr; 1252 Wr; 1419 Ur-. URBAN Urbán [urba:n]. Also spelled Orbán. Derived from the Latin urbanus ‘city dweller’. UREGI Üregi [yregi]. The derivational suffix -i indicates a person from Ürög a settlement in Baranya County now part of Pécs. The toponym Ürög is derived from üreg ‘cave, hollow’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1113 Erig; 1171 Irig; 1551 üreg. URSZULAN Urszulán [ursula:n]. ‘Lion’. Distorted form of the Turkish arslan ‘lion’. The Hungarian oroszlán ‘lion’ is also derived from the Turkish arslan. Old Hungarian spelling: 1318 Vruslan; 1332 Oruzlan; 1369 Orozlan. URUS Urus [urush]. The adjectival suffix -s is affixed to the Sumerian úru ‘to plant, to cultivate’ (Labat 1948: No. 56). Urus may have been the old name of a farmer in Hungary. The toponym Orosháza ‘house of oros’ may be derived from the same Sumerian source. USTOS Üstös [yshtøsh]. A maker or seller of üst ‘cauldron, boiler’ may have been named so. The adjectival suffix -ös indicates occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1395 ÿst. UVEGES Üveges [yvegesh]. A maker or seller of üveg ‘glass’. The adjectival suffix -es indicates occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1530 Iveges. UZO Űző. [yzø:]. ‘One who pursues’. The name is comprised of űz ‘to pursue’ and the participle -ő. The Hungarian űz is almost identical with the Sumerian uš and the Babylonian úz ‘to pursue’ (Labat 1948: No. 211). Old Hungarian spelling: 1454 -wzo. VAD Vad [vad]. ‘Wild, undisciplined’. May have been bestowed on a person from the wilderness. Similar words are in other Ugric languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *βanta ‘forest, jungle’ (Benkő 1967: 1062, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Wod-, Wad-; 1215 Voad; 1372 vad-. VAGI Vági [va:gi]. ‘Dweller by the Vág’, name of a river in historical Hungary confined to Slovakia in 1920 and renamed Váh. The origin of the name is somewhat obscure. Some would derive it from the Old High German wag ‘rough, choppy (water)’ (Kiss 1978: 676). However, the Hungarian vág, the root of vágtat ‘rapid, fast running’, would be more fitting.



108
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1093 Vag. VAJAS Vajas [vajash]. Maker or seller of vaj ‘butter’. The adjectival suffix -as indicates occupation. Similar words are in other Uralic languages, cf. Votyak voj, Finn voi (Benkő 1967: 1069, vol. 3). The ultimate source may be the Sumerian ia ‘fat, fatty material’ (Labat 1948: No. 231). Old Hungarian spelling: 1075 woios; 1200 Uoyos; 1211 Voios; 1253 Wayas. VAJK Vajk [vajk]. The original pre-Christian Turkish name of Hungary’s first Christian king St. Stephen. Turkish spelling would be Vayk, derived from bay ‘sir, rich, leader’ (Németh 1990: 284), synonymous with bey ‘chief, ruler, prince’ (Akdikmen 1986: 49). The word-final element -k is the Turkish diminutive suffix that indicates ‘little prince’. Wealth and power went hand-in-hand in ancient Turkish society. Old Hungarian spelling: 1185 Voyku; 1264 Voyk. VAJTHO Vajthó [vajto:]. A maker or seller of vajtó a basket-size vessel hollowed out of wood (Benkő 1967: 1071, vol. 3). The name is comprised of váj ‘to hollow’, the past tense t followed by an h that marks the reduced plosion of the preceding t and the participle -ó. The Hungarian váj may be derived from the Sumerian bal ‘to hollow’ (Labat 1948: No. 9). Old Hungarian spelling: 1594 vayto. VAJTHA Vajtha [vajta]. A person from Vajta, a locality in Fejér County, may have received the name. It is of Turkish origin derived from bay ‘rich’ (Kiss 1978: 677). An alternative etymology would link the name with the Hungarian váj ‘to hollow’. See above for more information. Old Hungarian spelling: 1150 Boyta; 1324 Vayta. VAK Vak [vak]. ‘Blind’. A blind person would receive such a name. Old Hungarian spelling: 1211 Vocu; 1336 vok-; 1400 Wak; 1448 vak. VALLAS Vállas [va:lash]. ‘Broad-shouldered’. The name contains váll ‘shoulder’ and the adjectival suffix -as that indicates condition. Similar words are in other Finno-Ugric languages, cf. Finn olka, Estonian ola ‘shoulder’ (Benkő 1967: 1072, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1372 vall; 1646 Váll-. VAMOS Vámos [va:mosh]. ‘Customs-officer’, the collector of any duties or toll. Derived from the Persian vam ‘debt, loan’ (Lambton 1988: 173). The Hungarian adjectival suffix -os converts it into an occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1109 Vamus; 1275 Wamus. VAMOSI Vámosi [va:moshi]. The derivational suffix –i indicates a descendant of Vámos. See above for etymology. VARADI Váradi [va:radi]. Also spelled Várady. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a person from Várad a town in Baranya County. The toponym Várad is composite of vár ‘fort’ and the suffix -ad a metathetic variant of the grammatical designator -da that denotes a place. The vár segment may be a derivation from the Sumerian bár ‘sanctuary’ (Labat 1948: No. 344) or bár ‘circular wall’ (Deimel 1939: No. 49). Both interpretations of the cuneiform sign seem to describe a ‘fort’. But according to the Historical- Etymological Dictionary of the Hungarian Language vár is derived from the Iranian vara ‘entrenchment, defense’, Middle-Persian *var ‘fort, fortified place’, New-Persian bar ‘hill, mount, fortress wall’ (Benkő 1967: 1090, vol. 3).



109
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 waradi; 1200 Uarod, Warod. VARGA Varga [varga]. ‘Maker of foot-wear’. Also spelled Vargha. A derivation from varr ‘to sew’ followed by the connecting consonant g and the adjectival suffix -a that indicates an occupation. Old Hungarian spelling: 1389 Varga. VARJU Varjú [varju:]. ‘Crow’. Probably a sign-name, used as an emblem. Similar words are in other Uralic languages. The reconstructed proto-word is *βarɜ. Old Hungarian spelling: 1265 Worre-; 1341 woryou-; 1342 worrew; 1346 Voryu; 1347 Waryu; 1398 Varyu. VAROSI Városi [va:roshi]. ‘City dweller’. Also spelled Várossy. The name is comprised of vár ‘fort’, the adjectival suffix -os and the derivational suffix -i (archaic -y). The reduplicated s marks a stressed consonant. For etymology see Varadi. Old Hungarian spelling: 1224 Waras. VASS Vass [vash]. Derived from vas ‘iron’. The name probably denotes an ‘ironmonger’, but could refer to demeanor or integrity as well. The reduplicated s marks a stressed consonant. Similar words are in other languages of the Uralic language family, the reconstructed proto-word would be *βaśke (Benkő 1967: 1095, vol. 3). Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Wasas, Wosas; 1214 Wos-; 1370 Was-; 1553 Vas. VASTAG Vastag [vashtag]. Also spelled Vastagh. The literal translation is ‘thick, bulky’, synonymous with ‘stout’ when applied to a person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1451 Wasthagh. VATHA Vatha [vata]. The grapheme h marks the reduced plosion of the preceding t. Vata is a variant of Bata [B > V] derived from the Sumerian bad, bat ‘scepter, staff’ (Waddell 1927: 23). Identical with the Hungarian bot both in meaning and phonetic value. The Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a when affixed to vat/bat indicates a leader, a person in charge. The ‘staff’, originally a rudimentary weapon of the ancient warrior, slowly evolved from a wooden staff to a bejeweled scepter and became the symbol of power that endured to the present-day. Titles and names derived from bat, bad denoted leaders, heads of state like Buda, in ancient Magyar society prior to the invasion of the turkish Huns, Avars and Hungarians. The most notable Vatha was the leader of a popular Magyar uprising in 1046 to shake off foreign rule represented by the House of Árpád. That revolt is erroneously referred to by historians as a failed attempt to stop the spread of Christianity. VAZ Váz [va:z]. ‘Skeleton, shell’, but used for denoting a ‘scarecrow’ or a person of skin and bone. Old Hungarian spelling: 1416 uaz; 1577 váss; 1839 vázu. VEN Vén [ve:n]. ‘Old, worn-out’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1403 Wen; 1481 Ween; 1522 Weyn; 1602 Vén. VIDA Vida [vida]. The archaic form of vidám ‘joyful, merry’. The name is comprised of the root vid ‘gay, cheerful’ and the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix -a that indicates condition. Old Hungarian spelling: 1202 Vida. VIGH Vígh [vi:g]. ‘Gay, cheerful’. The word-final element h marks the reduced plosion



110
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
of the preceding g. Old Hungarian spelling: 1138 Wig; 1423 Vigh. VILAG Világ [vila:g]. The Hungarian világ ‘world’ has no probable reason to form a surname. It is derived from the Sumerian bil ‘to burn, heat, warmth’ (Labat 1948: No. 172). The cuneiform sign for bil is also pronounced as izi ‘fire’ which is almost identical with the Hungarian izzik ‘glows from heat’. The word világ suggests that the world was originally conceived by Hungarians as any place on earth that is exposed to the light and warmth of the sun. The following words are derived from the root vil: villan ‘flash’; villám ‘lightning’; világ(-osság) ‘light’; világít ‘glow’; világegyetem ‘universe’. A phonetic change from b to v is a recognized occurrence, cf. Greek Basil > Hungarian Vazul > Slavonic Vasil. The original Sumerian bil was preserved in the Hungarian bélyeg ‘stamp, brand (hot iron stamp) imprint’. It was spelled biluk in 1263 and bilig in 1597 (Szamota 19021906: 61). The earliest process of branding involved the use of hot iron, i.e. the stamp or mark on animals, even on humans, was the result of fire and heat, hence the bil segment in the Hungarian word for stamp. The element ág in világ may be the reversed form of the Sumerian –ga. It indicates dependence and is mainly employed in the formation of adjectives (Lenormant 1877: 269). The suffix -ga was active in the Hungarian language at one time, cf.: mézga ‘resin’ from méz ‘honey’. VIRAG Virág [vira:g]. ‘Flower’. Also spelled Virágh. A grower or seller of flowers may have received the name. The Hungarian virág is almost identical with the Sumerian girag ‘flower’ (Labat 1948: No. 483). Old Hungarian spelling: 1412 Wyragh. VIRRASZTO Virrasztó [virasto:]. ‘Vigil-keeper’ or ‘night-watchman’. The root vir is identical with vil, see Vilag above for etymology. The reduplication of r marks a stressed consonant. The causative suffix -aszt is followed by the participle -ó. Old Hungarian spelling: 1432 Virrazto; 1478 Wyrraztho; 1595 Verraztho, Viraztho. VONA Vóna [vo:na]. Also spelled Vonó. Derived from von ‘draw, pull’. The archaic adjectival suffix -a suggests that the form Vóna is older than Vonó which has the presentday participle -ó. A person having an engaging, magnetic personality may have been named so. Present-day form is vonzó. Old Hungarian spelling: 1616 uonno; 1643 vonyó. ZABOLAI Zabolai [zabolai]. Also spelled Zablay. The derivational suffix -i (archaic -y) indicates a descendant of Zabola, Zabla a maker of bridle-bits. The archaic adjectival suffix -a indicates occupation. The word zabla ‘bridle-bit’ is synonymous with the verb zaboláz ‘control, restrain’ Old Hungarian spelling: 1495 Zabala; 1530 Zabola. ZACS Zács [za:ch]. Also spelled Zách. A probable variant of the Sumerian záh ‘to flee, to escape’ (Labat 1948: No. 589). The name may denote a refugee. ZAGON Zágon [za:gon]. The name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. The initial element zág may be derived from the Sumerian zag ‘frontier, edge’ (Labat 1948: No. 332). The word-final element -on appears to be a variant of the suffix -ony used in the formation of nouns. The name, therefore, denotes a



111
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
‘dweller near the frontier’. Zágon is also a place name in Transylvania located in the former Háromszék County of historical Hungary adjacent to the old Romanian international boundary. This area was confined to Romania in 1920. The Slavonic source záhon ‘small garden plot’, suggested by the Etymological Dictionary of Geographical Names is not convincing. ZALAN Zalán [zala:n]. Zalán, also spelled Salán, was the leader of the local Magyar population in southern Hungary who resisted the infiltration of Árpád’s Hungarian army in 896. Etymologically the name fits into the Sumerian language. Zal means ‘to persist, to persevere’ (Labat 1948: No. 231) to which the antiquated substantive suffix -án, equivalent of the current Magyar -ány, is affixed. The name denotes a ‘firm, steady’ person. ZASZLOS Zászlós [za:slo:sh]. ‘Ensign bearer’. The lowest rank of commissioned officers in the Hungarian Army prior to 1945. The word zászló ‘flag’ is derived from száz ló ‘one hundred horses’ to which the adjective -s is suffixed to indicate status. According to folklore, each group of one hundred mounted soldiers during the Middle-Ages carried a flag to indicate the presence of 100 ló ‘horse’ i.e. ‘cavalry’, thus the name for their flag. Old Hungarian spelling: 1095 Zaztou [sic]; 1211 Zaaslou; 1587 Zazlo. ZAVAROS Zavarós [zavaro:sh]. ‘Vexing’. Derived from zavar ‘to disturb, to pester’. The adjective -ós indicates condition. Old Hungarian spelling: 1429 Zavaros. ZEGERNYE Zegernye [zegernje]. ‘Quarrelsome’. Old Hungarian spelling: 1803 Zekernye; 1829 Zegernye. ZENG Zeng [zeng]. One who had a ‘rich, vibrant’ voice may have been named so. Old Hungarian spelling: 1240 Zeng-. ZENTAY Zentay [zentai]. The archaic derivational suffix -y indicates one from Zenta a city in the Bácska region of historical Hungary confined to Serbia in 1920 and renamed Senta. The name of the city is derived from szent ‘saint, holy’ which is a Hungarian variant of the Latin sanctus ‘holy’. The word-final element -a is the Sumero-Hungarian adjectival suffix. Old Hungarian spelling: 1216 Zentha. ZILAH Zilah [zila]. The name cannot be etymologized in the present-day Hungarian language. It may be an altered form of the Sumerian silá ‘lamb’ (Labat 1948: No. 252) signifying a peaceful person. It is also a place name in Szilágy County of historical Hungary confined to Romania in 1920 and renamed Zalău. Old Hungarian spelling: 1150 Zyloc. ZOLD Zöld [zøld]. The word zöld ‘green’ is used to indicate a ‘young, immature’ person. Old Hungarian spelling: 1349 Zeld-; 1353 Zeuld-; 1444 zold; 1544 Zöld. ZOLTAN Zoltán [zolta:n]. A derivation from the Turkish sultan ‘ruler, prince’ (Ladó 1978: 229). Originally the title of a Muslim ruler or sovereign (Ahmed 2001: 206). It is present in the Persian language in the form of soltan but listed as an Arabic loan word. Old Hungarian spelling: 910 Zulta. ZOMOK Zömök [zømøk]. ‘Stocky, thick-set’. First recorded as zömök in1767. ZORGO Zörgő [zørgø:]. ‘Rattling, noise-maker’. First recorded as ʒerge- in 1538.



112
Michael Nogrady. 2012. Glossary of Hungarian surnames in Canada
ZSAKO Zsákó [zha:ko:]. Archaic form of zsákos ‘sack-maker’. The name is comprised of zsák ‘sack’ and the participle -ó. Similar words are in the Indo-European languages, cf. French sac, Dutch zak, English sack, but the ultimate source may be the Sumerian sag-kud ‘sack’ (Labat 1948: No. 115) or the Akkadian šaqqu ‘sack’ (Op. cit.). Old Hungarian spelling: 1545 sak. ZSIROS Zsíros [zhi:rosh]. ‘Fat’. The root zsír ‘fat, lard’ is followed by the adjectival suffix -os to indicate condition. This word is shared with the old Turkish çir [chir] ‘fat, lard’ (Clauson 1972: 424). Old Hungarian spelling: 1249 Ziros. ZSOMBEK Zsombék [zhombe:k]. ‘Clump of vegetation in a marsh’, a dweller in such an area may have received the name. Old Hungarian spelling: 1137 Somboc; 1396 Sombik; 1688 zsombék.
